Divine Transcendence
beginner
4 min
Maturidi's Method for Ambiguous Verses
Maturidi explains the proper approach to istiwa' without specifying interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi balances reason and revelation when interpreting ambiguous verses about Allah without fixing a single interpretation.

Core Idea
Affirm the verse, negate resemblance, but never fix one interpretation.

Maturidi addresses one of the most debated verses: Allah's istiwa' over the Throne. Instead of choosing a literal or metaphorical interpretation, he sets a method: affirm what reason and revelation teach, but do not lock into one meaning. This lesson explores that principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully, noting the key terms like istiwa'. Let the author's own wording set the foundation.
The word istiwa' is central to the discussion.
2
Distinguish the two affirmations
Maturidi first affirms based on reason (Allah existed without place), then based on revelation. Notice how he combines both as valid.
3
Understand the open‑ended conclusion
The final rule: negate tashbih but don't fix a single ta'wil. Ask yourself why leaving the interpretation open is important for divine transcendence.
This connects to the broader Maturidi methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى العَرْشِ اسْتَوَى، نقول بها على ما ثبت في العقل، من أن الله تعالى كان ولا مكان، ويجوز ارتفاع الأمكنة وبقاؤه على ما كان، فهو على ما كان، وكان على ما عليه الآن، جل عن التغيير والزوال والاستحالة والبطلان، ونقول بها على ما جاء به التنزيل، ثم لا نقطع تأويله على شيء لاحتماله غيره، ونؤمن بما أراد الله به، وكذلك في كل أمر ثبت التنزيل فيه نحو الرؤية وغير ذلك يجب نفي الشبه عنه والإيمان بما أراده من غير تحقيق على شيء دون شيء.
And 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne' (20:5) – we say it according to what is established in reason: that Allah existed when there was no place, and it is possible for places to cease to exist while He remains as He was. So He is as He was, and He was as He is now, exalted above change, passing away, transformation, and annihilation. And we say it according to what has come in revelation, then we do not definitively interpret it in any one way because of the possibility of other interpretations, and we believe in what Allah intended by it. And similarly, in every matter in which revelation is established, such as the vision and other things, it is necessary to negate resemblance (tashbih) from Him and to believe in what He intended without confirming anything in particular.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the Maturidi hermeneutical principle for dealing with verses that attribute bodily actions or location to Allah (mutashabihat). The specific verse is 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne' (istawa 'ala al-arsh). The author says we affirm this verse based on what reason establishes: Allah existed without any place (la makan) before creation, and places are contingent and can pass away while Allah remains unchanged. Therefore, His istiwa' does not imply change or physical location. Then we also affirm it according to revelation, but we do not fix a single specific interpretation (ta'wil) because the verse could bear other meanings; instead we believe in what Allah intended without settling on one interpretation. This principle extends to all revealed matters like the vision (ru'ya) and others: we must negate any likeness to creation (tashbih) and believe in Allah's intended meaning without insisting on a particular explanation.

Key Terms
الاستواء (al-istiwa')
المتشابهات (mutashabihat)
التأويل (al-ta'wil)
نفي الشبه (nafy al-shabah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi position is to not interpret ambiguous verses at all.
Correction: Maturidi actually affirms the verse based on reason and revelation, but refrains from assigning a single fixed interpretation.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the verse 'Ar-Rahman 'ala al-arsh istawa'?
A) It must be interpreted literally
B) We affirm it but do not specify a single interpretation
C) It is not to be believed
D) It only applies to this world

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's approach to mutashabihat combines rational negation of corporeality with an open-ended commitment to Allah's intended meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses about Allah's attributes, like 'istawā 'alā al-ʻarsh', do you find yourself either too quickly settling on a human-like image or too comfortably ignoring the words? Which tendency do you struggle with more, and what would it take for you to hold both affirmation and transcendence together as Maturidi teaches?
?
Maturidi says we do not fix one interpretation because of the possibility of other meanings. Are you willing to live with that uncertainty regarding Allah's attributes? Or do you feel a need for a single clear answer? What does your need for certainty reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom and your humility before His revelation?
?
If you truly applied the principle of negating tashbih (resemblance) to every attribute of Allah, what would change in your daily thoughts about Allah? Would it increase your awe or make you feel distant? How might this lesson reshape your personal relationship with the Quranic verses that describe Allah?

Etiquette of Not Attributing Evil to Allah
Maturidi explains the adab of not attributing evil creation to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi prohibits attributing the creation of evil to Allah and the principle governing proper attribution of actions to Him.

Core Idea
True adab: Affirm Allah's creatorship of all things, but only attribute to Him what evokes veneration or gratitude.

Maturidi teaches us a crucial theological etiquette: while Allah is the Creator of everything, including what we consider evil, we must not describe Him as the creator of filth or sin. This passage lays down the principle that all attributions to Allah must be dignified and express veneration, gratitude, or blessing. Let's explore the reasoning and how to apply it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage from Maturidi once, then the English translation. Notice the examples he gives.
The passage is short but dense; take time to absorb each phrase.
2
Identify the examples of what not to attribute
Maturidi mentions dung, filths, the devil, disbelief, and sins. Note that these are things considered evil or impure.
He also says one should not say 'O Creator of filths' even though Allah is truly their Creator.
3
Understand the principle of attribution
Maturidi gives a general rule: only attribute to Allah what comes out as veneration (ta'zim), gratitude (shukr), mentioning His blessings (ni'am), or His command (amr). Anything else, even if true, is not attributed out of adab.
This principle guides our speech about Allah, preserving reverence.
4
Reflect on the theological nuance
Why does Maturidi affirm Allah's creatorship of all things but prohibit certain verbal attributions? Consider the balance between truth and adab.
This is not a denial of Allah's power but a matter of proper etiquette.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the key point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينبه الماتريدي بأنه لا ينبغي إضافة خلق المعاصي إلى الله تأدباً : ' إن الله في التحقيق وإن كان رب كل شيء ، وإله كل شيء ، وخالق كل شيء ، وكل شيء له ، لا يقال ذلك في الأرواث والخبائث والشيطان وعلى ذلك يكره القول في الكفر والمعاصي إنها بقضاء الله وقدره وإرادته وكذلك عند الناس لا يقال : يا خالق الخبائث والأنجاس ونحو ذلك ، وإن كان هو في الحقيقة لكل شيء خالقاً ... وأصل ذلك أنه يضاف إلى الله تعالى كل ما كانت الإضافة إليه تخرج مخرج التعظيم أو مخرج الشكر أو مخرج ذكر نعمه أو أمره ، وما خرج على غير ذلك لا يضاف إليه ، وإن كان في الحقيقة خلقه
And al-Maturidi cautions that one should not attribute the creation of sins to Allah out of courteousness (ta'duban): 'Indeed, Allah in reality, even though He is the Lord of everything, the God of everything, the Creator of everything, and everything belongs to Him, one does not say that regarding dung, filths, and the devil. Likewise, one dislikes saying regarding disbelief and sins that they are by Allah's decree, measure, and will. Similarly, among people, one does not say: O Creator of filths and impurities and the like, even though He is in reality the Creator of everything... And the basis of that is that whatever is attributed to Allah in a manner that comes out as veneration (ta'zim) or gratitude (shukr) or mentioning His blessings or His command, then it is attributed to Him. And whatever comes out otherwise, it is not attributed to Him, even though in reality it is His creation.'
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage addresses the proper etiquette (adab) in speech regarding Allah's creation of evil. Maturidi warns that despite Allah being the Creator of all things, including dung, filth, and the devil, it is inappropriate to attribute these directly to Allah in speech. The general principle is that things are attributed to Allah only when the attribution serves to express veneration, gratitude, or mention of His blessings or command. Otherwise, even though Allah is truly their Creator, we avoid such attribution out of respect. This is an important theological nuance: while affirming Allah's creatorship of all things, one must observe proper decorum in speech.

Key Terms
التأدب (al-ta'adub)
الأرواث (al-arwath)
الخبائث (al-khaba'ith)
التعظيم (al-ta'zim)
الشكر (al-shukr)
النعم (al-ni'am)
الأمر (al-amr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi is denying that Allah creates evil or that He has power over it.
Correction: Maturidi affirms that Allah is the Creator of everything, but prohibits verbal attribution out of courteousness (adab). It is a matter of etiquette, not theology.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why should we not say that Allah created sins?
A) Because He does not actually create them
B) Out of courteousness (adab), because it does not express veneration or gratitude
C) Because sins are created by the devil
D) Because it is a lie

Key Takeaway
While Allah is the Creator of all things, we only attribute to Him what comes out as veneration, gratitude, blessing, or command, out of adab.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship, do you ever catch yourself silently blaming Allah or speaking about your trial as if He has wronged you? How can you practice the adab of attributing only veneration and gratitude to Him, even when you don't understand His wisdom?
?
Think of a time you complained about a difficulty to others. In that moment, did your words reflect reverence for Allah or did they indirectly attribute evil to Him without adab? What specific phrase can you use next time that turns your complaint into an expression of gratitude for what remains?
?
The principle is to only attribute to Allah what comes out as veneration or gratitude. Is there a repeated thought or phrase in your daily life that fails this test? Will you commit to removing it from your speech and replacing it with praise, starting today?

The Politeness of Attributing Only Good to God
Maturidi states the principle of attributing only worthy things to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that while God creates all things, evil should not be directly attributed to Him out of politeness (ta'dib).

Core Idea
We attribute to God only what brings glorification or gratitude, though He creates everything.

This passage from Imām Maturīdī’s Kitāb al-Tawḥīd addresses a subtle point: how to speak about God’s creation of evil. While affirming that God is the Creator of all things, including disbelief and sins, Maturidi teaches that it is improper to ascribe such things directly to God. Instead, attribution should follow the principle of what leads to glorification, gratitude, or mention of His blessings. Let’s examine the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and its literal translation. Notice the structure: an affirmation, a prohibition, and a concluding principle.
The Arabic has an ellipsis; the original may have more text.
2
Identify what Maturidi affirms about God
Maturidi opens by affirming that God is the Lord, God, and Creator of everything. This sets the theological foundation.
3
Notice what is prohibited to say
He then says we do not say that God creates filth, vile things, Satan, disbelief, or sins. It is disliked to attribute such things to God's decree.
This is a rule of speech, not a denial of creation.
4
Understand the basis for attribution
The principle: only those things that lead to glorification, gratitude, mention of blessings, or command are attributed to God. Anything else is not, even if He created it.
This guards against impoliteness (sūʼ al-adab).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الله في التحقيق وإن كان رب كل شيء، وإله كل شيء، وخالق كل شيء، وكل شيء له، لا يقال ذلك في الأرواث والخبائث والشيطان وعلى ذلك يكره القول في الكفر والمعاصي إنها بقضاء الله وقدره وإرادته وكذلك عند الناس لا يقال: يا خالق الخبائث والأنجاس ونحو ذلك، وإن كان هو في الحقيقة لكل شيء خالقاً ... وأصل ذلك أنه يضاف إلى الله تعالى كل ما كانت الإضافة إليه تخرج مخرج التعظيم أو مخرج الشكر أو مخرج ذكر نعمه أو أمره، وما خرج على غير ذلك لا يضاف إليه، وإن كان في الحقيقة خلقه
Indeed God, in reality, even though He is the Lord of everything, the God of everything, the Creator of everything, and everything belongs to Him, that (ascription of creation) is not said regarding filth, vile things, and Satan. And upon that, it is disliked to say regarding disbelief and sins that they are by God's decree, predestination, and will. Similarly, among people, it is not said: 'O Creator of vile things and impurities' and the like, even though He is in reality the Creator of everything... And the basis of that is that everything is attributed to God Almighty whose attribution brings about (an expression of) glorification, gratitude, mention of His blessings, or His command; and whatever goes beyond that is not attributed to Him, even though in reality it is His creation.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage defines Maturidi's principle of propriety in attributing actions to God. He begins by acknowledging that God is the Creator of all things, including evil, but then states that it is not proper to say that God creates filth, vile things, or Satan. Similarly, it is disliked to say that disbelief and sins are by God's decree, predestination, and will, even though in truth they are. This is out of politeness (ta'dib). The basis is that only those things that lead to glorification, gratitude, or mention of God's blessings or commands should be attributed to God. This passage guards against impoliteness toward God while upholding His absolute creation.

Key Terms
خالق
الأرواث
الخبائث
قضاء الله
قدره
إرادته
تعظيم
شكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because we should not attribute evil to God, God does not create evil.
Correction: Maturidi affirms God creates everything, but we avoid direct attribution of evil out of politeness, not because God does not create it.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why should we not say that sins are by God's decree?
A) Because God does not create sins.
B) Because it is impolite and not in the manner of glorification.
C) Because sins are caused by humans alone.
D) Because it leads to disbelief.

Key Takeaway
While God creates all things, we only attribute to Him what leads to glorification, gratitude, or mention of His blessings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When in your daily life do you catch yourself attributing misfortunes or hardships to God? How can you replace that with speech that glorifies Him or expresses gratitude?
?
If you truly internalized that God creates all things but you should only speak of Him with praise, what would you say to someone who is angry at God for a tragedy?
?
Maturidi says not even disbelief should be attributed to God in speech, even though He created it. Does your tongue sometimes claim that God 'made' you sin? What commitment will you make to align your speech with this adab?

Impossibility of Similarity Between God and Creation
Argument that any similarity to contingent things would negate divine perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that any similarity between God and contingent things would imply imperfection, thereby establishing God's absolute transcendence.

Core Idea
If God resembled creation, He would lose His perfection.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid presents a core argument for divine transcendence. By examining the attributes of contingent things—such as change, increase, decrease, and decay—Maturidi shows that God cannot share any of these without negating His perfection. The argument serves as a proof that the world is created and that its Creator is absolutely unique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is heavily garbled; focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the key point
Notice that the author argues that if God had any similarity to contingent things—such as change, increase, or decay—His perfection would be invalidated. This is the central claim.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider how this argument proves that the world is created and that God is absolutely transcendent, free from any likeness to creation.
This connects to the doctrine of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم سُتَّتُه ضرورة فقالوا، ونفوا عنه جميع ما احتمل غيره، إذ احتمل غيره التفاضل في الذوات، والاختلاف في الصفات، أو احتمل غيره الاستحالة والتغير بما يتمكن فيه الزيادة والنقصان، وإن كان بعض ذلك ثابتاً فهو نوع المحتمل لذلك، على أن ثباته بالتسخير على ما هو عليه من دوام الحركة أو السكون، أو باحتمال التقاد[؟] الذي هو آفة الموجود بما فيه احتمال الفناء ووجود الأشباه له لَبَطَل عنه صفة الكمال والتمام، أو تمكن النهاية له، والأحد[؟] الذي يُتَوَهَّم الأتم والأنقص والأوفر والأقصر، فهذه الوجوه من آيات حدث العالم وأدلة محدثه. فلو كان المحدث[؟] مما به عرف حدث العالم وأن له محدثاً ليلحقه من[؟] وفيه فساد العالم، وشهادته على محدث حكيم ذلك الوجه ما لحق غيره[؟] عليم متعال عن الأشباه والأضداد، مع ما كان كل غيره له حدث الوجوه، فلو كان لشيء منه شبه يسقط عنه من ذلك القدم، أو عن غيره الحدث، أن غيره حوادث اعترضت بتوته[؟] وهم القول أصحاب الهيولى والمسلمون، والوسهم[؟] ولا قوة إلا بالله
And they claimed its concealment necessarily and said, and they denied from Him everything that something else could possibly have, since something else could possibly have differentiation in essences and difference in attributes, or something else could possibly have change and alteration by which increase and decrease are possible in it, and if some of that is established then it is a type of possibility for that, provided that its persistence is by subjugation on what it is of perpetual motion or stillness, or by the possibility of decay[?] which is a defect of the existent due to the possibility of annihilation in it and the existence of similars to it, then the attribute of perfection and completeness would be invalidated from Him, or the possibility of an end for Him, and the one[?] which is imagined as the more perfect and the more deficient and the more abundant and the more scarce, so these aspects are signs of the occurrence of the world and proofs of its being created. For if the created thing[?] were that by which the occurrence of the world is known and that it has a creator to follow from it[?] and in it is the corruption of the world, and its testimony upon a wise creator that aspect did not affect what is other than it[?] All-Knowing, exalted above similars and opposites, while everything other than Him has the aspects of contingency, so if there were for something of Him a likeness, it would remove from Him that eternity, or from other than Him contingency, that other than Him are contingencies that opposed his destruction[?] and they are the opinion of the proponents of hylé and the Muslims, and their arrow[?] and there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for God's transcendence by showing that any similarity between God and created things would entail imperfection. The author begins by referencing those who claim necessity and deny from God all that contingent things can possess, such as differentiation in essences or attributes, change, increase, decrease, and decay. If God had any of these, His perfection would be negated. The various aspects of contingency in the world—such as motion, stillness, potential for decay, and possibility of annihilation—serve as signs that the world is created and has a Creator. If God were similar to any contingent thing, He would lose the attribute of eternity or contingency would be attributed to other things incorrectly. This reinforces that God is absolutely unique and free from any likeness to creation.

Key Terms
حدث العالم
قدم
كمال
شبه
أشباه وأضداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what would happen if God had any similarity to contingent things?
A) He would remain perfect
B) His perfection and completeness would be invalidated
C) He would become more knowable
D) The world would become eternal

Key Takeaway
God's absolute uniqueness is proven by the impossibility of any similarity to contingent creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, do you ever unconsciously picture Him as a larger, more powerful version of a human? How does this passage challenge that tendency, and what will you do today to replace it with true tanzih?
?
You claim Allah is unique, but do you ever expect Him to act like a human—changing His mind or feeling emotions like anger as we do? How does the argument that any similarity would negate His perfection refute that, and what step will you take to correct your belief?
?
This lesson proves that even the possibility of similarity invalidates divine perfection. What is one area of your life where you treat Allah as if He were like a created being (e.g., in your expectations of reward and punishment), and how will you change that today?

Why Allah Cannot Be Called a Body
Maturidi classifies two meanings of 'body' and rules both inapplicable to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'body' (jism) cannot be applied to Allah according to Imam Maturidi, and the two possible meanings of 'body' that he rules out.

Core Idea
Calling Allah a 'body' either likens Him to creation or becomes meaningless.

Imam Maturidi tackles a key objection from those who might describe Allah as a 'body' in some sense. He carefully separates the term into two possible meanings—each one found to be incompatible with divine transcendence. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the structure of two meanings become clear.
Notice that Maturidi uses a numbered classification (أحدهما / وثانيها).
2
Identify the two meanings of 'body'
The first meaning is based on the quiddity (māhiyya) of body in the witnessed world: something with directions, ends, or three dimensions. The second meaning is body as mere affirmation (ithbāt) without any quiddity.
3
Analyze the refutation of the first meaning
Maturidi argues that the first meaning implies parts and limits, which are signs of being created (ḥadath). Applying it to Allah would make Him like created things, contradicting 'nothing is like Him'. Also, without those specific meanings, the term loses rational meaning and must come from revelation—but it doesn't.
See the argument about 'by way of hash' leading to absurdity.
4
Analyze the refutation of the second meaning
Even if 'body' is taken as pure affirmation (no specific meaning), it is never used that way in language—accidents and attributes aren't called 'body'. So this meaning is also invalid.
The second meaning is a hypothetical that fails due to actual usage.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Both meanings are ruled out. The term 'body' cannot be applied to Allah under any interpretation. This safeguards God's transcendence.
Consider how this argument protects tanzīh (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم القول بالجسم يخرج على وجهين : أحدهما في مائية الجسم في الشاهد أنه اسم ذى الجهات ، أو اسم محتمل النهايات ، أو اسم ذي الأبعاد الثلاثة ، فغير جائز القول به فى الله سبحانه على أدلة الخلق وإمارة الحدث ؛ إذ ذلك معنى الأجزاء والحدود التي من آيات الحدث، وقد بينا أن ليس كمثله شيء ، وفى ذلك ايجاب جعله كأكثر الأشياء . وإن كان على التسمية به بلا تحقيق ما ذكرنا خرج الإسم عن المعروف به ، فبطل تعرف ذلك من جهة العقل والاستدلال . وحقه السمع عن الله ، إن الجسم أسمائه ، ولم يرد عنه ولا عن أذن لأحد تقليده ، فالقول به لا يستعب ولو وضع بالتحت من غير دليل حسنى أو سمعى أو عقلى لوسع القول بالجسد والشخص ، وكل ذلك مستنكر بالسمع ، وليسع القول بكل ما بستی به الخلق ، وذلك فاسد
وثانيها أن يكون الجسم ليست له مائية تعرف سوى الإثبات ، فيجوز القبول به لو لم يراد به غيره، لكنه لا أحد يجعل الجسم من أسماء الإثبات ؛ إذ لا يُسمى به الأعراض والصفات على احتمالها اسم الإثبات ؛ لذلك بطل القول به .
The Shaykh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: Then the statement about the body (jism) is of two aspects: one of them concerns the quiddity (māhiyya) of the body in the witnessed world, that it is a name denoting that which has directions, or a name that admits of ends, or a name that has the three dimensions. So it is not permissible to apply it to God, exalted, based on the evidence of creation and the sign of temporality (ḥadath); for that implies parts and limits which are among the signs of temporality. And we have explained that nothing is like Him, and that would necessitate making Him like most things. If [one uses] the name without realizing what we mentioned, the name departs from its known meaning, so it becomes invalid to know it by reason and inference. Its proper source is revelation from God; if the body were among His names, it has not been reported from Him nor has anyone been given permission to follow that. So using it is not permissible, even if [one says] 'by way of hash' without a sensory, revelatory, or rational proof; then it would be permissible to say body, person, and all that is disapproved by revelation, and it would be permissible to say everything by which creation is described, and that is corrupt. The second aspect is that the body may have no quiddity known except affirmation (ithbāt); then it would be permissible to accept it if nothing else is intended by it. But no one makes the body one of the names of affirmation, for accidents (aʿrāḍ) and attributes (ṣifāt) are not called by it as such; therefore, saying it is invalid.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the term 'body' (jism) into two possible meanings, followed by a ruling that neither can be applied to Allah. The first meaning is the body as understood in the witnessed world: a thing with directions, ends, or three dimensions. Such a body is composed of parts and has limits, which are signs of createdness (ḥadath). Since Allah is unlike anything, applying this term would liken Him to created things. Moreover, if one uses the term without these specific meanings, it becomes meaningless in rational discourse. The term must come from revelation, but it is not authentically reported as a name of Allah. The second meaning is that 'body' might be used simply to affirm existence without any quiddity. However, no one uses 'body' as a mere affirmation; it is not applied to accidents or attributes in that sense. Therefore, both meanings are invalid for Allah.

الإثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might argue that if 'body' is used in a vague sense (bi-l-tahatt), it could be permissible to apply to Allah.
Correction: Maturidi refutes this by showing it leads to applying all creaturely descriptions to Allah, which is corrupt. The term must have a clear meaning grounded in revelation or reason, and neither supports it.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi rule out the application of the term 'body' (jism) to Allah in its first sense?
A) Because 'body' implies parts and limits, which are signs of createdness.
B) Because 'body' is a term that only applies to humans.
C) Because 'body' is not an Arabic word.
D) Because 'body' is used only for physical objects.

Key Takeaway
Neither the ordinary meaning of 'body' (with dimensions) nor a vague affirmation-only sense can apply to Allah; both lead to unacceptable implications.

Maturidi's Refutation of the Human Analogy
Maturidi responds to the objection that 'thing not like things' could imply 'human not like humans'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the analogy that God could be called 'human not like humans' based on the affirmation of existence without corporeality.

Core Idea
God is a thing without being like things, but not a human because He is not a body.

In this passage, Maturidi faces a clever objection: if we can call God 'thing not like things', why can't we call Him 'human not like humans'? He dismantles this analogy by showing that the term 'thing' in this context affirms existence without implying any similarity to created things, whereas 'human' necessarily implies a body. Maturidi's response is a masterclass in precision: he carefully distinguishes between affirming existence and affirming a specific category, and then exposes the hidden contradiction in the objector's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector asks: since you say God is a 'thing not like things', why can't we say He is a 'human not like humans'? Notice the apparent symmetry.
This is a common rhetorical move in kalam: turning the opponent's own logic against them.
2
Identify Maturidi's first response
Maturidi points out that the reason we affirm 'thing' for God (existence) does not apply to bodies. Therefore, the analogy fails because the basis is different.
Think about why accidents and attributes are called 'things' without implying corporeality.
3
Understand the second response
If the objector means that 'not like things' is purely an affirmation of existence (the letter of affirmation), then his question is meaningless because 'human' is a different category.
4
Analyze the direct answer and the contradiction
Maturidi's direct answer: God is not a body, so we say 'not like bodies'. Then he shows that the objector's assumption that 'thing not like things' means 'not like bodies' leads to a contradiction if we apply 'body' to God.
The contradiction is that if God were a body, then 'thing' would only exclude some things (bodies), not all things, nullifying the claim of 'body not like bodies'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : لأن السبب الذي ألزمنا القول بالشيء لم يوجد في الجسم ، لذلك لم نقل. وبعد، فإنه لا يحلو فيما يريد الزامنا من القول بالجسمية من أن يلزمنا بقولنا بالشيء ، فوجدنا أكثر الأشياء وهي الأعراض والصفات من غير لزوم القول فيها بالإسمية - يمنع ذلك . وإن كان يريد بقولنا : لا كالأشياء حرف الإثبات ليدل على مائية المثبت ، فلا وجه لهذا السؤال . يقول : إذ جاز أن يكون شيئا لا كالأشياء لم لا جاز أن يكون إنسانا لا كالناس ؟ قال الشيخ رحمه الله : فجواب مثله أن يُقال : لأنه ليس بجسم ، فيقال : لا كالأجسام ، وليس هذا النوع بمعارضة إنما هو محاكمة، ونحن لا تلك [؟] إيجاد الإله حتى تقابل بمثل هذا، فيقال لنا: إذ جعلتم ذا ليم [؟] لا جعلتم ذا، بل يتعالى عن الجمل [؟] على جهة، بل يوصف بما هو عليه، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم المعارضة عند التحصيل تناقض؛ لأنه قال: إذ قلتم: شيء لا كالأشياء لا كالأجسام؟ فإذا قلنا: جسم يصير قولنا: شيء لا كالأشياء هو شيء لا كبعض الأشياء، إذ الجسم وفي ذلك بطلان القول بجسم لا كالاجسام، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
It was said to him: Because the reason that compelled us to affirm the term 'thing' is not found in the body, therefore we did not say (it about the body). Moreover, it is not permissible concerning what he wants to compel us to say about corporeality that he should compel us by our saying 'thing'. For we found most things—namely, accidents and attributes—without necessitating the term 'name'—this prevents that. And if he intends by our saying 'not like things' the letter of affirmation to indicate the whatness of the affirmed, then this question has no basis. He says: Since it is permissible for Him to be a thing not like things, why is it not permissible for Him to be a human not like humans? The Sheikh, may God have mercy on him, said: The answer to such a one is to say: Because He is not a body, so it is said: 'Not like bodies.' And this type is not an objection but a [؟] judgment. And we do not that [؟] creation of God to be confronted with such. It is said to us: When you made Him possessor of limb [؟] you did not make Him possessor of [؟]. Rather, He is exalted above sentences [؟] in a manner, but He is described by what He is. And there is no power except with God. Then the objection, upon investigation, is contradictory; because he said: Since you said 'thing not like things' (do you mean) 'not like bodies'? Then if we say 'body', our saying 'thing not like things' becomes 'thing not like some things', since the body, and in that is the nullification of the statement 'body not like bodies'. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from an opponent and Maturidi's response. The objector argues that if we can call God a 'thing not like things', then by analogy we could call Him a 'human not like humans'. Maturidi first notes that the reason for affirming 'thing' for God (affirmation of existence) does not apply to bodies, so the analogy fails. He also points out that most things (accidents, attributes) are called 'thing' without implying corporeality. If the objector meant that 'not like things' is a pure affirmation of existence, then his question is baseless. The direct answer is that God is not a body, so we say 'not like bodies'—a different formulation. Maturidi then shows the contradiction: the objector assumes 'thing not like things' is equivalent to 'not like bodies', but if we apply 'body' to God, then 'thing' would only exclude some things (since bodies are a subset of things), which nullifies the claim of a 'body not like bodies'. Each point follows the author's sequential refutation.

Correction: Maturidi shows that 'thing' here affirms existence without similarity, while 'human' implies a body, so the analogy fails. God is unlike all things including humans.

B) Because 'human' is a more specific term than 'thing'.
C) Because the objector used a faulty Qur'anic analogy.
D) Because Maturidi denies that humans can be described as things.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's refutation shows that affirming God's existence as a 'thing' does not open the door to any arbitrary analogy; each attribute is carefully defined by what it necessarily implies.

Meaning of 'Thing Not Like Things'
Maturidi defines 'thing not like things' as negating whatness while affirming existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi's phrase 'thing not like things' simultaneously affirms God's existence and negates any resemblance to creation by removing the 'whatness' of created things.

Core Idea
God is a 'thing' without the 'whatness' of created things.

Imam al-Maturidi unpacks a central phrase of Islamic theology: 'thing not like things.' This statement is not a simple negation; it carefully balances affirming God's existence while denying any similarity to creation. In this passage, Maturidi explains that the phrase removes the 'whatness' (māhiyya) of created things from God, and he distinguishes between two types of created things: essences and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the words settle before analyzing.
The Arabic has some uncertain spots due to OCR, but the core meaning is clear.
2
Identify the two types of 'things'
Maturidi divides created things into two categories. Find them in the text and note how he defines each.
Essences (a'yān) are bodies; attributes (ṣifāt) are accidents (a'rāḍ).
3
Understand what 'whatness' means
'Whatness' (māhiyya) refers to the quiddity or essence of a thing—what makes it what it is. Maturidi says the phrase removes this from God.
This means God does not share the quiddity of any creature.
4
See the balance between negation and affirmation
Notice that the phrase 'thing' affirms existence, while 'not like things' negates similarity. Maturidi warns that removing either part leads to error: anthropomorphism or denial.
This is the key theological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابو منصور رحمه الله : ثم معنى قولنا شيء لا كالأشياء هو إسقاط مائية الأشياء ، وهي نوعان : عين وهو جسم ، وصفة وهي عرض [؟] مائية الأعيان وهو الجسم ، والصفات وهي الأعراض. فإذا أزلنا ذلك المعنى الذي [؟] أبطلنا [؟] التشبيه [؟] منه، الذي هو بذلك المعنى، كما إذا أزلنا معنى الإثبات ونفى التعطيل أبطلنا القول به، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: Then the meaning of our saying 'thing not like things' is the removal of the 'whatness' of things. And they are two types: an essence, which is a body, and an attribute, which is an accident [؟]. The 'whatness' of essences is the body, and attributes are accidents. So if we remove that meaning which [؟] we nullify [؟] the comparison [؟] from it, which is by that meaning. Just as if we remove the meaning of affirmation and negation of nullification, we nullify the statement about it. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage provides a definition. Maturidi explains that when we say God is 'a thing not like things', we are negating the 'whatness' (māhiyya) of things from God—that is, God does not share the essence or attribute of any created thing. He then divides things into two categories: essences (which are bodies) and attributes (which are accidents). The 'whatness' of essences is the body, and attributes are accidents. By negating 'whatness', we negate both body and accident from God. If we were to remove this negation of similarity, we would fall into anthropomorphism; if we remove the affirmation of existence, we fall into denial (taʿṭīl). Thus, the phrase 'thing not like things' simultaneously affirms God's existence and negates any resemblance to creation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'not like things' only negates similarity without affirming existence, or that it denies any positive description of God.
Correction: Maturidi clearly states that the word 'thing' affirms existence (ithbāt), and removing that affirmation leads to ta'ṭīl (denial). Thus the phrase both affirms and negates.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does 'thing not like things' negate from God?
A) The name 'thing' itself.
B) The 'whatness' (māhiyya) of things.
C) Only the attribute of corporeality.
D) The possibility of describing God with any positive attribute.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's phrase 'thing not like things' affirms God's existence while negating any created whatness, guarding against both anthropomorphism and denial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What specific change can you make in your prayer or supplication to honor His uniqueness?

Why Allah is Not a Body
Al-Maturidi refutes the claim that Allah is a body by showing it entails temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why describing Allah as a body is impossible according to Imam al-Maturidi's argument from temporality.

Core Idea
Calling Allah a body implies He is temporal — and that is impossible.

Imam al-Maturidi confronts a dangerous theological error: describing Allah as a body. He argues that the very definition of a body — as something composed of accidents and limits — points to creation. To apply it to Allah would contradict His eternal nature. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define 'body' (jism) in kalam terms
Review the classical definition: a body is a locus of accidents and has boundaries. These are signs of being created.
Accidents (a'rad) are non-essential attributes that change.
2
Read al-Maturidi's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how al-Maturidi demands proof from every aspect.
The phrase 'from every aspect' is key.
3
Analyze the logical structure
Identify the conditional: 'If it is possible... then...' and the conclusion that it is impossible.
4
Reflect on the consequence
Understand that if something has the marks of temporality, it cannot be eternal. Hence Allah cannot be a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أحب أن يقول في الله بالجسم على التحقيق مما بينا من معاني الأجسام التي هي محل الأعراض المحتملة للنهايات ونحو ذلك يكلم في معاني خلق الأجسام المشاهدة، إن أمكن تثبيته من كل جهة من جهاته من حيث تلك الجهة فالقول به في الله محال فاسد؛ لأنه وصف له بما قام دليل حدثه، وإن كان لا يتهيأ إيجابه فحقه التسمية، وإن ثبت قبل به، وإلا لا.
And whoever wishes to say about Allah that He is a body (jism) according to the verification of what we have explained of the meanings of bodies, which are the loci of accidents susceptible to boundaries and the like — let him discuss the meanings of the creation of observed bodies. If it is possible to establish it from every aspect of His aspects in terms of that aspect, then the statement about Him as a body is impossible and corrupt; because it is a description of Him by that whose proof of temporality has been established. And if its affirmation is not possible, then it is entitled to rejection, but if established, it is accepted, otherwise not.
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine of corporealism (tajsim) regarding Allah. The author argues that bodies (ajsam) are defined as loci of accidents (a'rad) and are subject to boundaries (nihayat). These are marks of temporality (huduth). Therefore, applying the term 'body' to Allah would imply that He is temporal, which is impossible. The author challenges anyone who affirms a body for Allah to demonstrate it fully from all aspects. If they cannot, the statement is rejected. The argument relies on the established proof that everything that is subject to temporality is created, and God is eternal.

Key Terms
جسم
عرض
حد
حدث
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can call Allah a body metaphorically without implying temporality.
Correction: Al-Maturidi argues that the very definition of body includes being a locus of accidents and having limits, which are marks of creation. Therefore, even metaphorical application is invalid if it implies temporality, because Allah is eternal.

Checkpoint
Why is describing Allah as a body considered impossible according to this passage?
A) Because bodies are composed of parts.
B) Because bodies are defined by limits and accidents, which imply temporality.
C) Because God has no attributes.
D) Because the Quran does not use the term 'body' for God.

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot be a body because bodies are defined by accidents and limits, which are signs of being created, and Allah is eternal.

Maturidi's Three Proofs Against Contingent Descriptions
Maturidi refutes the permissibility of contingent attributes with three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why describing Allah with contingent attributes leads to contradictions, and the three proofs Maturidi offers to demonstrate its invalidity.

Core Idea
Describing Allah with contingent things leads to absurdities, proving it is impossible.

Imam Maturidi responds to those who claim it is permissible to describe Allah with contingent attributes. He presents three clear proofs to show why this view is invalid, using logic and consistency. This lesson walks through each argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
First Proof: The Problem of Opposites
Read the opening argument where Maturidi says that if describing God with contingencies were allowed, it would permit describing Him with opposites like good and evil, which is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the consequence is impossible, so the premise must be false.
2
Second Proof: Hearing and Non-Sounds
Examine the second proof: non-sound objects are not described as 'heard' but as 'known'. The difference in the letter of affirmation does not necessitate a difference in the attribute or in God's Essence.
This shows that the opponent's reasoning does not force a change in God's attributes.
3
Third Proof: Inconsistency in Negation of Justice
Read the third proof: the opponents themselves do not consider the negation of injustice to be an attribute of essence, revealing an inconsistency in their position.
This undermines the opponent's overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ، فإن المسألة ساقطة لأنه علق الحكم بجواز القول وقد بينا المسألة قد عرفنا بما سبق أن لا يجوز أن يوصف الله بحادث ، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز الوصف بمصلح ومفسد وخير وشرير ، وذلك باطل ؛ فثبت أنه لا بما ظن ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضا أن كل غير الصوت لا يتكلم فيه بتسميع ، وجائز أن يتكلم فيه بعلم ثم لم يجب التفريق بينهما بالاختلاف في حرف الإثبات ، ولم يوجب في ذاته فيا كذلك في حق النفى ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . منع وأيضا أنه لا يجوز وصفى الله تعالى بنفى العدل ، ثم لم يقل هو صفة الذات عندهم ، ثبت أن تقديره فاسد .
Moreover, the question is invalid because he tied the ruling to the permissibility of the statement, and we have already explained the issue that it is not permissible to describe God with a contingent thing. If that were permissible, then describing Him as a doer of good and corrupt, good and evil would be permissible, and that is false. So it is established that it is not as he thought, and there is no power except with God. Also, every non-sound is not spoken of with hearing, and it is possible to speak of it with knowledge, yet the distinction between them is not necessitated by the difference in the letter of affirmation, nor does it necessitate in His Essence; likewise in the case of negation, and there is no power except with God. Another argument: it is not permissible to describe God Almighty with the negation of justice, yet they do not say it is an attribute of essence. Thus it is established that his estimation is corrupt.
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage provides a series of arguments to refute the opponent's claim that describing God with contingent things is valid. Maturidi first declares the question itself invalid because the opponent tied a ruling to the mere possibility of speech, but it is established that God cannot be described by contingent things. If that were allowed, then one could describe God as both a reformer and corrupter, good and evil, which is impossible. He then adds two further proofs: 1) For non-sound objects, one does not speak of them as 'heard' but as 'known'. Still, the difference in letter (affirmative vs negative) does not force a difference in the attribute itself or in the Essence. 2) If one says God is not unjust, that negation is not considered an attribute of essence by the opponents themselves, so their reasoning is inconsistent. Therefore, their conclusion is invalid.

Key Terms
الحادث
المصلح
المفسد
التسميع
نفي العدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say describing God with contingent things is invalid?
A) Because contingencies do not exist
B) Because it would allow describing God with opposites like good and evil
C) Because God is not affected by time
D) Because contingencies are attributes of essence

Key Takeaway
Describing Allah with contingent attributes is logically impossible because it leads to contradictions and inconsistencies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first proof shows that allowing one contingent attribute forces you to accept even contradictory ones. Where in your own understanding or worship are you tolerating small contradictions that could unravel your tawhid?
?
If describing Allah with 'good' alone is impossible because it leads to absurdity, how might you unknowingly limit Allah to human notions of goodness? Are you willing to surrender every mental image you have of Him?
?
The opponents’ inconsistency lies in treating some attributes as essential and others not. Have you ever selectively applied theological principles to suit your comfort? What would it cost you to be fully consistent?

Refutation: God Cannot Be Described with Accidents
Refutation of linking judgment to speech; God cannot be described with accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why describing God with accidents (ḥādith) leads to absurd consequences and why the opponent's linking of judgment to speech is invalid.

Core Idea
Describing God with accidents would imply opposing qualities, which is impossible.

Imam Maturidi here refutes an opponent who argues that God can be described with temporal attributes. He points out that the opponent's argument is flawed because it ties the ruling to the permissibility of speech. Moreover, he reminds us that it has already been established that God cannot be described with accidents, as that would permit contradictory descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly, trying to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the phrase 'وبعد' which indicates a continuation.
2
Identify the opponent's position
The opponent had claimed that it is permissible to describe God with accidents based on the permissibility of speech.
3
Understand the invalid link
The author says the opponent's argument is invalid because he made the ruling dependent on 'jawāz al-qawl' (permissibility of speech), which is not a valid basis.
The author had already established in previous sections that describing God with accidents is impermissible.
4
Examine the absurd consequence
If it were allowed to describe God with an accident, then one could describe Him with both reformer and corrupter, good and evil—contradictory qualities—which is absurd.
This shows the logical impossibility.
5
Final conclusion
The author concludes that the opponent's assumption is false, and affirms reliance on God's power.
The phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' is a common invocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن المسألة ساقطة لأنه علق الحكم بجواز القول وقد بينا المسألة قد عرفنا بما سبق أن لا يجوز أن يوصف الله بحادث، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز الوصف بمصلح ومفسد وخير وشرير، وذلك باطل؛ فثبت أنه لا بما ظن، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And after, the issue is invalid because he made the ruling dependent on the permissibility of speech, and we have explained the issue—we have known from what preceded that it is not permissible to describe God with an accident (ḥādith). And if that were permissible, then description with a reformer and a corrupter, good and evil would be permissible, and that is false. So it is established that it is not as he thought, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument. The author begins by stating that the opponent's claim (masʾala) is invalid because it made the ruling (ḥukm) conditional on the permissibility of speech (jawāz al-qawl). The author asserts that it has already been established that it is impermissible to describe God with an accident (ḥādith), i.e., something that comes into existence. If it were permissible, then one could describe God with terms that imply good and evil (muṣliḥ, mufsid, khayr, sharr), which is absurd. Therefore, the opponent's argument fails. The concluding phrase 'there is no power except with God' is a standard pious expression.

جواز القول
مصلح
مفسد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can be described with accidents or temporal attributes.
Correction: Describing God with accidents would lead to contradictory descriptions (muṣliḥ/mufsid, khayr/sharr), which is impossible. Therefore, God cannot be described with accidents.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Describing God with accidents leads to absurd consequences, so it is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

What specific step will you take this week to search for and uproot any accidental attributes you might unknowingly ascribe to Him?

Interpreting God's 'Coming' and 'Bringing' in the Quran
Maturidi argues that Quranic verses about God's 'coming' do not indicate temporality in His speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi insists that Quranic terms like 'coming' and 'bringing' must be interpreted in a way that befits God's transcendence, not as implying change or temporality.

Core Idea
God's 'coming' is not temporal change; it befits His lordship.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi responds to an opponent who uses Quranic verses mentioning God's 'coming' and 'bringing' to argue that God's speech is created (temporal). Maturidi firmly rejects this, explaining that such terms must be understood in accordance with God's transcendence above change and passing away. He illustrates his point with Prophet Abraham's statement about the setting stars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Pay attention to the key terms: الإتيان, المجيء, and الآفلين.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
Notice that the opponent uses verses about God's 'coming' to argue for the temporality of speech. Find this in the passage.
The opponent's reasoning is that 'coming' implies movement and change.
3
Grasp Maturidi's principle
Maturidi states that Allah is exalted above change and passing away. Therefore, attributes like speech and action are not subject to temporality.
This principle is key to his interpretation.
4
See the application to 'coming' and 'bringing'
Maturidi argues that both terms must be diverted to an aspect that befits lordship, not creaturely change.
He uses the same logic for both 'coming' and 'bringing'.
5
Reflect on the Abraham example
The verse 'I do not love those that set' shows that change (setting) is characteristic of creatures. This supports the idea that God does not change.
The example reinforces the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج في حدث الكلام بذكر الإتيان والمجيء ، وهو من ذلك الوجه بحدث ، وقد بينا أن الله تعالى إذ وصيف بالكلام - على تعاليه عن احتمال التغير والزوال فمثله في صفة الكلام والفعل وما ذكرت على أن الله قد أضاف المجيء إلى نفسه ، ثم لم يجب أنه حدث ، بل صرف إلى الوجه والذي يحق بالربوبية ، فمثله الأول. وكذلك وجب صرف الإتيان إلى الوجه الذي يحق بالربوبية ، لا إلى ما عرف به الخلق من التغير والزوال ، فمثله في حقيقة الفعل والكلام على ما قال لا أحب الأفلين ، ومن يكون على حال ثم على أخرى فهو من الآفلين بالتحقيق ، والله أعلم.
And he argued for the temporality of speech by mentioning 'bringing' (al-ityān) and 'coming' (al-majī'), and it is in that respect temporal. And we have explained that when Allah most high is described as having speech—upon His exaltation above the possibility of change and passing away—then the same applies to the attribute of speech and action and what was mentioned. For Allah has attributed 'coming' to Himself, yet it does not necessitate that it is temporal; rather it is diverted to the aspect that befits lordship. So the same applies to the first. And likewise it is necessary to divert 'bringing' to the aspect that befits lordship, not to what is known among creatures of change and passing away. So the same applies in the reality of action and speech, according to what He said: 'I do not love those that set' (al-Anʿām 6:76). And whoever is in one state and then in another, he is indeed of those that set. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 652

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the interpretation of anthropomorphic expressions in the Quran, specifically 'coming' (ityān) and 'bringing' (majī'). Maturidi's opponent uses such verses to argue that God's speech is temporal (created). Maturidi responds that when Allah is described with speech, He is exalted above change and passing away; therefore, terms like 'coming' and 'bringing' must be understood in a way befitting His lordship. They do not imply movement or change. He cites the example of Abraham saying 'I do not love those that set' to illustrate that change (like setting) is characteristic of creatures, not of God. Thus, the divine attributes of speech and action are eternal and not subject to temporality.

Key Terms

المجيء
حدث الكلام
التغير
الزوال
الربوبية
الآفلين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quranic verses about God's 'coming' imply that God moves or changes, and thus His speech is created.
Correction: Maturidi explains that such verses must be interpreted in a way that befits divine lordship, free from change and temporality.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the view that God's 'coming' implies temporality in His speech?
A) Because God's speech is created and thus temporal.
B) Because Allah is exalted above change and passing away, so 'coming' must be interpreted transcendently.
C) Because the Quranic verses about 'coming' are metaphorical and have no real meaning.
D) Because 'coming' refers to the coming of angels, not God.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's approach preserves divine transcendence by interpreting anthropomorphic language in a way that avoids attributing change to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'And your Lord comes' (Q. 89:22), what is your immediate mental image? Does it resemble a physical movement? How does Maturidi's insistence on divine transcendence challenge that assumption? Will you commit to reminding yourself, every time you encounter such verses, that God's acts are not like ours?
?
Think of a situation in your life where you have assumed that God's attitude toward you changes based on your actions—perhaps after a sin you feel He is distant, or after a good deed you feel He is pleased. Does this lesson's emphasis on God's unchanging attributes affect how you view His mercy and justice? How can you practically anchor your trust in His constancy rather than your fluctuating states?
?
Abraham rejected the stars because they set—they passed from one state to another. Examine your own heart: what are you relying on that is subject to change? Your health, your wealth, your relationships, your own efforts? Are they not like the stars that set? In light of this lesson, what would it mean for you to direct your ultimate trust solely to the God who does not change?

The Unlikeness of God's Speech: Scriptural and Logical Proofs
Maturidi presents a logical argument and scriptural proofs for the unlikeness of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Maturidi argues that God's speech cannot be analogous to created speech, using logical reasoning and Quranic verses.

Core Idea
God's speech is utterly unlike any created speech, as established by the Quran and reason.

Imam Maturidi begins by presenting a conditional argument: if God's speech were patterned on another's speech, similarity would arise. But the Quran explicitly negates any similarity to God. He then supports this with two key verses and concludes that God's speech is fundamentally different from all created speech. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
The Arabic passage presents a logical conditional and scriptural proofs. Read it carefully without analyzing too deeply.
2
Identify the conditional
Notice the structure 'if X then Y' and how Maturidi uses it to set up the argument.
The conditional is: if God's speech were on the pattern of another's speech, then similarity would arise.
3
Analyze the Quranic verses
What do 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11) and 'They created like His creation' (13:16) imply?
42:11 negates similarity; 13:16 shows similarity in action leads to similarity in essence.
4
Understand the conclusion
How does Maturidi establish that God's speech is different (khilāfiyya)?
The conclusion is that God's speech is different from all created speech, just as His essence is different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا يخلو من أن يكون على تقدير كلام غيره فيكون فيه تشابه ، ودل قوله : لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ على نفي الشبه له في الصفة والذات ، وأيد ذا قوله : خَلَقُوا كَخَلْقِهِ فدل أن شبه الفعل يوجب التشابه ، مع القول أن الخلق لو اجتمعوا لا يأتون بمثله فانتفى الشبه . إذ فيه تماثل ، فثبت له الخلافية لكلام الخلق جميعا على ما ثبت لذاته ، مع ما لم يمتحن جميع كلام الخلق ليدرك منتهى معانيه ، وقد ذكر كلام النمل والهدهد وتسبيح الجبال وغيرها مما لا يفهم شيء من ذلك بالحروف المعجمة ولا على المفهوم من كلام البشر .
وإذ ثبت أن من الكلام ما لا يبلغ تقديره وسع الخلق ولا يبلغه فهم ، فمن أحب تقدير كلام الرب بذلك فهو مُغفَل . وكذلك فعله تعالى خارج عن وصف فعل الخلق . وفى ثبوت الخلافية من جميع الوجوه نفي الحدثية ؛ لما به يقع الوفاق، وبظل معنى الأعراض والتفرق والاجتماع والحد والغاية والزيادة والنقصان ؛ إذ ذلك وصف كلام الخلق، والله الموفق .
Then [the attribute of speech] cannot be free from being [considered] on the pattern of another's speech, thus similarity (tashabuh) would arise. And His saying 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11) indicates the negation of similarity (shabah) to Him in attribute and essence. And this is supported by His saying 'They created like His creation' (13:16), which indicates that similarity in action necessitates similarity [in essence]. With the statement that if all creation gathered they cannot produce the like of it, thus similarity is negated. For in that there would be likeness (tamāthul). So it is established that His [speech] is different (khilāfiyya) from all speech of creation, just as is established for His essence — along with the fact that not all speech of creation has been examined to perceive its ultimate meanings. And the speech of the ant, the hoopoe, the glorification of the mountains, and other things have been mentioned, from which nothing is understood by articulate letters nor in the way of human speech.
And since it is established that there is speech whose measure no creation can attain nor understanding reach, whoever wishes to measure the speech of the Lord by that is heedless. Likewise His action is beyond the description of the action of creation. And in the establishment of difference (khilāfiyya) from all aspects lies the negation of contingency (ḥadathiyya); because by it agreement occurs, and the meaning of accidents (a'rāḍ), separation, composition, limit, boundary, increase, and decrease disappear — for that is the description of created speech. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 657

Commentary
This passage continues the argument that God's speech cannot be analogous to created speech. Maturidi presents a conditional: if God's speech were on the pattern of another's speech, then there would be similarity (tashabuh) between God and creation. But the Quranic verse 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11) negates any similarity in attribute and essence. The verse 'They created like His creation' (13:16) shows that if someone creates like God's creation, then there is similarity in action, which would lead to similarity in essence. But it is impossible for creation to produce the like of God's creation, so similarity is negated. Therefore, God's speech is utterly different (khilāfiyya) from all created speech, just as His essence is different. Moreover, creation cannot exhaust all speech to understand its ultimate meanings; for example, the speech of the ant, the hoopoe, and the glorification of mountains are not even understood in human terms. So anyone who tries to measure God's speech by created standards is heedless. God's actions are also beyond description. This difference (khilāfiyya) from all aspects negates contingency (ḥadathiyya) because it removes the features of accidents, separation, composition, limit, etc., which characterize created speech.

Key Terms
تشابه
شبه
الخلافية
تماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's speech is like human speech but just more powerful.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's speech is fundamentally different in kind, not just degree.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does Maturidi directly use to negate similarity in attribute and essence?
A) 13:16
B) 42:11
C) 42:11 and 13:16
D) 2:255

Key Takeaway
God's speech is fundamentally different from all created speech, as proven by reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear the Quran, do you ever treat it as just another book—comparing its style, arguments, or emotional impact to human products? How does the truth that God's speech is utterly unlike any human speech confront that attitude right now? What will you change in the way you listen to or recite the Quran starting today?
?
If God's speech is fundamentally different from all created speech, then your own words—whether in supplication, in conversation, or in your inner thoughts—cannot be placed on the same level as His. Are there moments when you treat your own opinions or plans as if they carry the same weight as divine revelation? How can this lesson humble you before every verse of the Quran?
?
Maturidi argues that the impossibility of creating like God disproves any claim to similarity. Think of an area in your life where you implicitly act as if your achievements, wisdom, or virtue are on par with what God does or commands. How does the absolute unlikeness of God's speech expose the arrogance of such thinking? What concrete step can you take today to acknowledge that His speech—and His standards—are utterly above your own?

Beyond Human Speech: Examples and Doctrinal Consequences
Maturidi expands on the unlikeness with examples and derives doctrinal consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how examples of non-human speech and the concept of khilāfiyya negate contingency and accidental attributes in God's speech.

Core Idea
Divine speech transcends human comprehension and cannot be measured by created standards.

Maturidi continues by noting that not all speech of creation has been examined to understand its ultimate meanings. He gives examples of speech (ant, hoopoe, mountains) that are not understood through letters or human language. He then warns against measuring divine speech by such standards and concludes that the absolute difference of God's speech negates contingency and removes accidental attributes.

3

What does it mean to measure God's speech by created standards?
It means trying to apply human-like categories to divine speech, which is heedless.
4
Grasp the consequence
How does khilāfiyya negate hadathiyya and remove accidental attributes?
Absolute difference removes characteristics of created speech like composition, limit, etc.

?

?
If God's speech is free from limits, boundaries, and composition, why do you still argue with others about its exact wording or structure, as if it were a created thing? What would it take for you to submit to its absolute difference and let that transform your heart?

The Logical Flaw in Potentiality
Maturidi refutes the potentiality model by highlighting its logical consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the concept of potentiality in a first principle leads to contradictions, particularly regarding corruption and the eternity of the Qarmatians' eternal origin.

Core Idea
Potentiality implies vulnerability to corruption, undermining claims of eternal origin.

Maturidi begins by attacking the foundational claim that things exist in a first principle 'in potentiality' and later appear 'in actuality.' He shows this leads to absurdities: the first principle becomes susceptible to corruption, contradicting its supposed eternity. The Qarmatians' view of eternal origination faces the same flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on potentiality
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the claim that things are in a first principle potentially and then appear actually.
The term 'bi-l-quwwa' means 'in potentiality' and 'bi-l-fiʿl' means 'in actuality.'
2
Trace the logical consequence
Notice Maturidi's reasoning: if the first principle is a composite of potentialities, it is subject to corruption (talf wa fanāʾ). This is because potentialities imply change and composition.
Maturidi says 'it has become susceptible to corruption and annihilation.'
3
Apply to the Qarmatians
See how this refutation also applies to the Qarmatians who claim the origin is eternal yet originated by ibdāʿ. Their position is also flawed because if the origin contains potentialities, it cannot be eternal.
The Qarāmiṭa are a sect that Maturidi frequently refutes.
4
Reflect on the method
Understand that Maturidi uses a reductio ad absurdum: he shows that the presuppositions of the potentiality model lead to unacceptable conclusions, thereby refuting it.
This is a common kalam method of argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تستمد منه النَّفْس الكل فيمد الهيولى ومنه تركيب العالم - أن يتلف الأول ؛ إذ هذا حق كل شيء في شيء بالقوة يظهر بالفعل وقد صيروا جميعا الوجود للحال دليل الأولية ، لكن الأول جوهر الكل والثانى جوهر الجزء ، وقد صار جوهر الكل معروفا بجوهر الجزء ؛ إذ ليس مما يبلغه أحد بالحس ، وبالله التوفيق . منه كما الأدلة وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت جميع ما بيتنا من الحوادث والحاجات التي هي للحدث لذلك الأصل ؛ إذ صار محتملا للتلف والفناء ، وذلك أيضا يلزم القرامطة يجعلونه أبديا ، وإن كانوا يقولون : كان بعد أن لم يكن / بالإبداع ، ولا قوة إلا بالله ودليل الجهل تمن فيه ما فيه بالقوة بأحوال ما فيه ومن فيه وما يكون ذكرت من جهل النطف والحبوب وغيرها ، فيلزم كذلك في الهيولى والطينة وما قالوا ، ولزم أن ليس لما قالوا تدبير ، ولا كان شيء من ذلك به ، ولكن إن كان فهو يمن علم ما يكون فيجعل أصله مبروزا فيه بالقوة ، يظهر بالفعل بما جعل له من المواد والأمكنة التي بها تنمو في ذلك القول بالتوحيد ، وأنه منشى ذلك كله ليكون به كل شيء يكون على ما قالوا ، أو أن يكون محدثا لكل شيء يكون أبدا على ما يشاء أن يكون من 10 أصول وابتداء ، أو كيف شاء على ما يقوله أهل التوحيد . وإذ الله سبحانه قادر على إنشاء أصل فيه كل شيء مبروزا ، هو قادر على ابتداء كل شيء كما شاء من غير بروز بالقوة ولا خروج بالفعل ، ولكن بالتقدير والتكوين . وإن زعموا أن الأشياء كانت في الأصل مستجنّة بجوهرها فيظهر بالفعل فهي أيضا. ترجع إلى ما قلنا ؛ لأن ذلك قولهم فى النطف والحبوب ، مع ما في هذا مما في العقل دفعه بما لا يحتمل تمكن أضعاف الشيء بجوهرها فيه ؛ للتناقض والفساد وتكذيب العيان . أو أن يكون ذلك الأصل الذى سموه طينة أو هيولى أو مبدعا أو نفس الكل يملك إنشاء العالم لا بأن كان فيه ولكن بالفعل والتكوين على ما شاء ، كيف شاء ، لا مرد لحكمه ولا نقيض على تدبيره ، فهو قول [أهل] التوحيد ، لكنهم سموا بأسماء غير الأسماء التي هي أسماء منشئ العالم عند أهل التوحيد ومبدعها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
The universal soul draws from it, supplying the matter (hayūlā) and from it the composition of the world – that the first should perish; for this is the truth of everything in something: in potentiality (bi-l-quwwa) it appears in actuality (bi-l-fiʿl). And they have all made existence for the state a proof of the firstness, but the first is the substance of the whole and the second is the substance of the part. And the substance of the whole has become known through the substance of the part, since it is not something that anyone attains by sense. And with Allah is success. ... And if this is established, all the accidents and needs that we have mentioned as belonging to the originated are established for that origin, since it has become susceptible to corruption and annihilation. And that also follows for the Qarāmiṭa who make it eternal, even if they say: it came to be after it was not, by way of origination (ibdāʿ). And there is no power except with Allah. And the proof of ignorance: that in it there is what is in it potentially, in the states of what is in it and from it and what will be – mentioned from ignorance of the sperm drops and seeds and other things. So likewise it follows regarding the matter (hayūlā) and the clay (ṭīna) and what they said. And it follows that there is no governance for what they said, nor was any of that by it. But if it is, then He knows what will be, so He makes its origin emerge in it potentially, appearing in actuality by what has been appointed for it of materials and places by which it grows – in that statement regarding tawḥīd, and that He is the originator of all that so that everything that will be is by it, according to what they said; or that He is the creator of everything that will be eternally as He wills, from origins and beginnings, or as He wills according to what the people of tawḥīd say. And Allah, glorified, is able to create an origin in which everything is made to emerge; He is able to initiate everything as He wills without emergence in potentiality nor coming forth in actuality, but by determination (taqdīr) and formation (takwīn). And if they claim that things were in the origin hidden in their substance, then appear in actuality, then they too return to what we said, because that is their statement regarding sperm drops and seeds, besides what is in this that the intellect repels due to the impossibility of the multiple of a thing being contained in its substance – due to contradiction, corruption, and the denial of sensory evidence. Or that that origin which they called clay or matter (hayūlā) or originated (mubdaʿ) or the universal soul possesses the creation of the world – not by its being in it, but by actuality and formation as He wills, how He wills, no repelling of His judgment and no opponent to His decree. So that is the statement of the people of tawḥīd, but they named it with names other than the names which are the names of the Creator of the world among the people of tawḥīd and its Originator. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 663

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of philosophical and sectarian views that posit a primordial substance (matter, clay, universal soul) containing all things potentially, from which the world emerges in actuality. Maturidi argues that such a view leads to absurdities: if the first principle is subject to corruption, then composite things are also; and the Qarāmiṭa's claim of eternal origination faces the same problem. He then presents the tawḥīd view: Allah creates directly by decree and formation, without any latent potentiality. Finally, he notes that if these philosophers actually meant that the origin itself creates the world directly (not from within it), then they would be agreeing with Ahl al-Tawḥīd, but they use different names for the Creator. The argument proceeds by showing the inner contradiction of the potentiality model and affirming divine omnipotence.

بالفعل
جوهر الكل
جوهر الجزء
التلف
الفناء
القرامطة
الإبداع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world emerged from a pre-existing substance (matter/clay) that contained all things potentially, and these things appeared in actuality over time.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this belief is logically flawed: if the first principle contains potentialities, it is composite and thus subject to corruption and annihilation, contradicting its supposed eternity. Also, the idea of a thing containing its multiples in potentiality is rationally impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Matturidi, why does the potentiality model lead to corruption (talf/fanāʾ) of the first principle?
A) Because potentiality requires a change from one state to another, implying composition and contingency.
B) Because the first principle is made of matter which decays naturally.
C) Because the Qarmatians claimed it is eternal.
D) Because potentiality is identical to actuality.

Key Takeaway
Potentiality in the first principle implies composition and corruption, refuting claims of an eternal originating substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that everything you see could explain itself without needing God? Think about your own existence: are you merely a product of potential matter, or do you sense a deeper dependence on the Eternal? How would your day change if you truly believed that nothing containing potentiality can be eternal – that everything around you is a sign of its Creator? Commit to one act today that reminds you of your contingency, such as reciting 'Allāhu Ṣamad' with full awareness.
?
When you look at the world, do you see it as self-sufficient or as a sign pointing beyond itself? This passage argues that any model of origin based on potentiality leads to corruption. How often do you let the temporary nature of things – like a sunset or a decaying leaf – remind you of your need for the Eternal? Write down one specific thing you will do this week to connect your awareness of decay to trust in al-Bāqī (the Everlasting).
?
The Qarmatians claimed that the first principle could be eternal despite being originated. Have you ever held a belief – perhaps about your own self-sufficiency or the world's permanence – that conflicts with the clear signs of dependence in creation? What would it take for you to surrender that belief and accept the evidence of tawḥīd? Set a concrete intention: study one verse about creation (e.g., Al-Baqara 2:117) this week and reflect on its implications for your understanding of God's oneness.
