Divine Transcendence
advanced
7 min
Direct Creation Without Potentiality
Maturidi affirms direct creation by Allah's decree and contrasts it with the potentiality model.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's affirmation that Allah creates directly by determination (taqdīr) and formation (takwīn), without any need for potentiality in an eternal substance.

Core Idea
Allah's power suffices to create from nothing, bypassing any latent potentiality.

After refuting the potentiality model, Maturidi presents the correct tawḥīd view: Allah is able to create an origin containing everything already actualized, or he can initiate each thing individually by his decree and power. The key is that creation does not require a transition from potentiality to actuality; rather, Allah's will and power are sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the tawḥīd affirmation
Read the passage where Maturidi states: 'Allah, glorified, is able to create an origin in which everything is made to emerge; He is able to initiate everything as He wills.'
Notice the two alternatives: creating a full origin or initiating each thing directly.
2
Understand the two modes of creation
Compare the two possibilities: 1) Creating an origin that contains everything potentially (but this is rejected earlier). 2) Creating each thing directly by taqdīr and takwīn. Maturidi affirms direct creation.
Taqdīr is divine decree, takwīn is formation.
3
Contrast with the potentiality view
See how Maturidi contrasts the tawḥīd view with the claim that things were hidden in the origin's substance and then appeared actually. He argues that if they claim that, it reduces to the same absurdity as sperm drops.
He says 'they too return to what we said' meaning the same refutation applies.
4
Reflect on divine omnipotence
Appreciate that Maturidi emphasizes Allah's absolute power to create as He wills, without any constraints of potentiality or material substrate.
This is central to Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تستمد منه النَّفْس الكل فيمد الهيولى ومنه تركيب العالم - أن يتلف الأول ؛ إذ هذا حق كل شيء في شيء بالقوة يظهر بالفعل وقد صيروا جميعا الوجود للحال دليل الأولية ، لكن الأول جوهر الكل والثانى جوهر الجزء ، وقد صار جوهر الكل معروفا بجوهر الجزء ؛ إذ ليس مما يبلغه أحد بالحس ، وبالله التوفيق . منه كما الأدلة وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت جميع ما بيتنا من الحوادث والحاجات التي هي للحدث لذلك الأصل ؛ إذ صار محتملا للتلف والفناء ، وذلك أيضا يلزم القرامطة يجعلونه أبديا ، وإن كانوا يقولون : كان بعد أن لم يكن / بالإبداع ، ولا قوة إلا بالله ودليل الجهل تمن فيه ما فيه بالقوة بأحوال ما فيه ومن فيه وما يكون ذكرت من جهل النطف والحبوب وغيرها ، فيلزم كذلك في الهيولى والطينة وما قالوا ، ولزم أن ليس لما قالوا تدبير ، ولا كان شيء من ذلك به ، ولكن إن كان فهو يمن علم ما يكون فيجعل أصله مبروزا فيه بالقوة ، يظهر بالفعل بما جعل له من المواد والأمكنة التي بها تنمو في ذلك القول بالتوحيد ، وأنه منشى ذلك كله ليكون به كل شيء يكون على ما قالوا ، أو أن يكون محدثا لكل شيء يكون أبدا على ما يشاء أن يكون من 10 أصول وابتداء ، أو كيف شاء على ما يقوله أهل التوحيد . وإذ الله سبحانه قادر على إنشاء أصل فيه كل شيء مبروزا ، هو قادر على ابتداء كل شيء كما شاء من غير بروز بالقوة ولا خروج بالفعل ، ولكن بالتقدير والتكوين . وإن زعموا أن الأشياء كانت في الأصل مستجنّة بجوهرها فيظهر بالفعل فهي أيضا. ترجع إلى ما قلنا ؛ لأن ذلك قولهم فى النطف والحبوب ، مع ما في هذا مما في العقل دفعه بما لا يحتمل تمكن أضعاف الشيء بجوهرها فيه ؛ للتناقض والفساد وتكذيب العيان . أو أن يكون ذلك الأصل الذى سموه طينة أو هيولى أو مبدعا أو نفس الكل يملك إنشاء العالم لا بأن كان فيه ولكن بالفعل والتكوين على ما شاء ، كيف شاء ، لا مرد لحكمه ولا نقيض على تدبيره ، فهو قول [أهل] التوحيد ، لكنهم سموا بأسماء غير الأسماء التي هي أسماء منشئ العالم عند أهل التوحيد ومبدعها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
The universal soul draws from it, supplying the matter (hayūlā) and from it the composition of the world – that the first should perish; for this is the truth of everything in something: in potentiality (bi-l-quwwa) it appears in actuality (bi-l-fiʿl). And the substance of the whole has become known through the substance of the part, since it is not something that anyone attains by sense. And with Allah is success. ... And if this is established, all the accidents and needs that we have mentioned as belonging to the originated are established for that origin, since it has become susceptible to corruption and annihilation. And that also follows for the Qarāmiṭa who make it eternal, even if they say: it came to be after it was not, by way of origination (ibdāʿ). And there is no power except with Allah. And the proof of ignorance: that in it there is what is in it potentially, in the states of what is in it and from it and what will be – mentioned from ignorance of the sperm drops and seeds and other things. So likewise it follows regarding the matter (hayūlā) and the clay (ṭīna) and what they said. And it follows that there is no governance for what they said, nor was any of that by it. Commentary
This passage is a refutation of philosophical and sectarian views that posit a primordial substance (matter, clay, universal soul) containing all things potentially, from which the world emerges in actuality. Maturidi argues that such a view leads to absurdities: if the first principle is subject to corruption, then composite things are also; and the Qarāmiṭa's claim of eternal origination faces the same problem. He then presents the tawḥīd view: Allah creates directly by decree and formation, without any latent potentiality. Finally, he notes that if these philosophers actually meant that the origin itself creates the world directly (not from within it), then they would be agreeing with Ahl al-Tawḥīd, but they use different names for the Creator. The argument proceeds by showing the inner contradiction of the potentiality model and affirming divine omnipotence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah creates the world by actualizing potentials already present in some pre-existing substance.
Correction: Maturidi affirms that Allah creates directly by His will and power, not by actualizing potentials. He is able to create everything anew without needing any pre-existing matter or potentiality.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how does Allah create?
A) By actualizing potentialities in a pre-existing substance.
B) By determination (taqdīr) and formation (takwīn), without potentiality.
C) By allowing the universal soul to evolve from matter.
D) By setting in motion a chain of cause and effect.

Key Takeaway
Allah's creation is direct and unmediated by potentiality; He simply decrees and forms as He wills.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah can create anything directly without any prior material, why do you still place your trust in material means—your job, your health, your savings—as if they are the true causes of your provisions? Will you today consciously acknowledge that it is only His decree that brings anything into existence, and then act on that reliance?
?
You have just learned that the intellect rejects the possibility of a tiny seed containing a fully grown tree in actuality. Yet how often do you fall into the same error, assuming that your small efforts must contain the potential for success, instead of seeing success as an immediate creation of Allah? Are you willing to let go of the illusion that the outcome is hidden within your actions, and instead ask Him directly for what you need?
?
The passage calls sensory evidence to witness the absurdity of potentiality. When you look at the world, do you truly see things emerging from nothing by Allah's command, or do you unconsciously attribute them to secondary causes? Right now, think of one thing you are worried about. Can you say from your heart, 'Allah can bring it about without any cause or process, just by His say-so'? Write it down and commit to verbalizing that conviction today.

Divine Transcendence

The Name of the Creator
Maturidi notes agreement in concept but misnaming in the philosophers' view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's final point: if the philosophers' origin is said to create directly by its own will, they agree with Ahl al-Tawḥīd but misuse the names of the Creator.

Core Idea
Calling the Creator by other names does not change the truth of tawḥīd.

Maturidi concludes his refutation with a generous observation: if the philosophers mean that the origin (which they call clay, matter, or universal soul) itself creates the world directly, by actuality and formation, without any potentiality, then this is exactly the statement of tawḥīd—except they apply wrong names. The passage underscores that the issue is not just rejection but correct naming and attribution of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the final conditional statement
Read: 'Or that that origin which they called clay or matter or originated or the universal soul possesses the creation of the world... by actuality and formation as He wills.'
Notice the 'or' (أو) presents a third option.
2
Identify the common ground
Understand that if they attribute creation to the origin itself acting by its own will, power, and independence, then they agree with Ahl al-Tawḥīd that there is a single omnipotent Creator.
Maturidi says 'So that is the statement of the people of tawḥīd.'
3
Note the misuse of names
See that the philosophers call this creator by names like 'clay' (ṭīna), 'matter' (hayūlā), or 'universal soul' (nafs al-kull), which are not the names that Ahl al-Tawḥīd use for Allah.
The correct names are the beautiful names of Allah, e.g., al-Khāliq, al-Mubdiʿ.
4
Reflect on the implication
Realize that Maturidi's approach is not merely to refute but to clarify: the truth of tawḥīd is independent of the names used. The real error is in the earlier claim of potentiality, not in the general belief in a single origin.
Maturidi ends with 'And there is no power except with Allah.'

تستمد منه النَّفْس الكل فيمد الهيولى ومنه تركيب العالم - أن يتلف الأول ؛ إذ هذا حق كل شيء في شيء بالقوة يظهر بالفعل وقد صيروا جميعا الوجود للحال دليل الأولية ، لكن الأول جوهر الكل والثانى جوهر الجزء ، وقد صار جوهر الكل معروفا بجوهر الجزء ؛ إذ ليس مما يبلغه أحد بالحس ، وبالله التوفيق . منه كما الأدلة وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت جميع ما بيتنا من الحوادث والحاجات التي هي للحدث لذلك الأصل ؛ إذ صار محتملا للتلف والفناء ، وذلك أيضا يلزم القرامطة يجعلونه أبديا ، وإن كانوا يقولون : كان بعد أن لم يكن / بالإبداع ، ولا قوة إلا بالله ودليل الجهل تمن فيه ما فيه بالقوة بأحوال ما فيه ومن فيه وما يكون ذكرت من جهل النطف والحبوب وغيرها ، فيلزم كذلك في الهيولى والطينة وما قالوا ، ولزم أن ليس لما قالوا تدبير ، ولا كان شيء من ذلك به ، ولكن إن كان فهو يمن علم ما يكون فيجعل أصله مبروزا فيه بالقوة ، يظهر بالفعل بما جعل له من المواد والأمكنة التي بها تنمو في ذلك القول بالتوحيد ، وأنه منشى ذلك كله ليكون به كل شيء يكون على ما قالوا ، أو أن يكون محدثا لكل شيء يكون أبدا على ما يشاء أن يكون من 10 أصول وابتداء ، أو كيف شاء على ما يقوله أهل التوحيد . وإذ الله سبحانه قادر على إنشاء أصل فيه كل شيء مبروزا ، هو قادر على ابتداء كل شيء كما شاء من غير بروز بالقوة ولا خروج بالفعل ، ولكن بالتقدير والتكوين . وإن زعموا أن الأشياء كانت في الأصل مستجنّة بجوهرها فيظهر بالفعل فهي أيضا. ترجع إلى ما قلنا ؛ لأن ذلك قولهم فى النطف والحبوب ، مع ما في هذا مما في العقل دفعه بما لا يحتمل تمكن أضعاف الشيء بجوهرها فيه ؛ للتناقض والفساد وتكذيب العيان . أو أن يكون ذلك الأصل الذى سموه طينة أو هيولى أو مبدعا أو نفس الكل يملك إنشاء العالم لا بأن كان فيه ولكن بالفعل والتكوين على ما شاء ، كيف شاء ، لا مرد لحكمه ولا نقيض على تدبيره ، فهو قول [أهل] التوحيد ، لكنهم سموا بأسماء غير الأسماء التي هي أسماء منشئ العالم عند أهل التوحيد ومبدعها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
The universal soul draws from it, supplying the matter (hayūlā) and from it the composition of the world – that the first should perish; for this is the truth of everything in something: in potentiality (bi-l-quwwa) it appears in actuality (bi-l-fiʿl). And they have all made existence for the state a proof of the firstness, but the first is the substance of the whole and the second is the substance of the part. And the substance of the whole has become known through the substance of the part, since it is not something that anyone attains by sense. And with Allah is success. ... And if this is established, all the accidents and needs that we have mentioned as belonging to the originated are established for that origin, since it has become susceptible to corruption and annihilation. And that also follows for the Qarāmiṭa who make it eternal, even if they say: it came to be after it was not, by way of origination (ibdāʿ). And there is no power except with Allah. And the proof of ignorance: that in it there is what is in it potentially, in the states of what is in it and from it and what will be – mentioned from ignorance of the sperm drops and seeds and other things. So likewise it follows regarding the matter (hayūlā) and the clay (ṭīna) and what they said. And it follows that there is no governance for what they said, nor was any of that by it. But if it is, then He knows what will be, so He makes its origin emerge in it potentially, appearing in actuality by what has been appointed for it of materials and places by which it grows – in that statement regarding tawḥīd, and that He is the originator of all that so that everything that will be is by it, according to what they said; or that He is the creator of everything that will be eternally as He wills, from origins and beginnings, or as He wills according to what the people of tawḥīd say. And Allah, glorified, is able to create an origin in which everything is made to emerge; He is able to initiate everything as He wills without emergence in potentiality nor coming forth in actuality, but by determination (taqdīr) and formation (takwīn). And if they claim that things were in the origin hidden in their substance, then appear in actuality, then they too return to what we said, because that is their statement regarding sperm drops and seeds, besides what is in this that the intellect repels due to the impossibility of the multiple of a thing being contained in its substance – due to contradiction, corruption, and the denial of sensory evidence. Or that that origin which they called clay or matter (hayūlā) or originated (mubdaʿ) or the universal soul possesses the creation of the world – not by its being in it, but by actuality and formation as He wills, how He wills, no repelling of His judgment and no opponent to His decree. So that is the statement of the people of tawḥīd, but they named it with names other than the names which are the names of the Creator of the world among the people of tawḥīd and its Originator. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 663

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of philosophical and sectarian views that posit a primordial substance (matter, clay, universal soul) containing all things potentially, from which the world emerges in actuality. Maturidi argues that such a view leads to absurdities: if the first principle is subject to corruption, then composite things are also; and the Qarāmiṭa's claim of eternal origination faces the same problem. He then presents the tawḥīd view: Allah creates directly by decree and formation, without any latent potentiality. Finally, he notes that if these philosophers actually meant that the origin itself creates the world directly (not from within it), then they would be agreeing with Ahl al-Tawḥīd, but they use different names for the Creator. The argument proceeds by showing the inner contradiction of the potentiality model and affirming divine omnipotence.

Key Terms
طينة
هيولى
نفس الكل
مبدع
تقدير
تكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The tawḥīd position requires that the Creator be called by the specific names 'Allah' or 'al-Raḥmān' alone; other names like 'the Origin' or 'the Universal Soul' are necessarily wrong.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if the meaning is correct (creation by will and power), the names themselves are secondary. However, the chosen names should be those that properly honor the Creator and avoid associations with materiality.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the philosophers' use of names like 'clay' or 'universal soul'?
A) These names are completely false and cannot refer to the Creator under any circumstances.
B) If they ascribe creation by will and power to that entity, they agree with tawḥīd but use incorrect names.
C) The names are acceptable as long as they refer to the first principle.
D) The philosophers have no concept of a Creator at all.

Key Takeaway
When the philosophers ascribe true divine attributes to their origin, their view aligns with tawḥīd, but they err by using inappropriate names.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever described Allah with a name or attribute that is not from His revealed names, perhaps out of cultural habit or philosophical influence? What will you do today to ensure you only name Him as He has named Himself?
?
The philosophers ascribed true divine attributes to their 'origin' but used the wrong name. Is there any area of your life where you affirm Allah's power over something yet practically attribute its outcomes to other created causes? How will you correct this inconsistency starting now?
?
Maturidi ends with 'there is no power except with Allah.' In what specific situation today have you acted as though your own power or someone else's power was sufficient? How will you actively depend on Allah's power alone in that matter?

Allah Existed Without Place
Maturidi argues that Allah existed without place and place is not an attribute of Him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that Allah does not need a place and that place is a created attribute, not essential to Allah's existence.

Core Idea
Allah's existence is independent of place; He was before creation and requires no location.

This lesson presents a foundational argument from Imam al-Maturidi that Allah is not bound by place. The passage establishes that Allah existed without place and that attributing place to Him would imply need and change, which are characteristics of created things. By the end, you will see how this reasoning upholds divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argument's flow come through.
Note the opening phrase: 'Allah was and there was no place'.
2
Identify the key principle
The passage begins with a clear principle: Allah existed without place. Highlight this and note that places are created and can be removed.
This principle is the foundation of the whole argument.
3
Understand the argument against change
Maturidi argues that change, removal, and transformation are signs of origination (ḥudūth). Since Allah is eternal, He cannot change. This refutes the idea that Allah could 'move' or 'be contained'.
Change indicates that a thing's state is not essential to it.
4
Grasp the implication of affirming place
If we say Allah is in a place, we imply He needs that place for establishment, just as bodies need places. This would liken Allah to created things, which is impossible.
Maturidi says affirming place enables 'need' (ḥājah) for Allah.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the Creator of places is not in need of any place. Allah is beyond any description that implies location.
This establishes tanzīh (transcendence) regarding place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : الأصل فيه أن الله سبحانه كان ولا مكان وجائز ارتفاع الأمكنة وبقاؤه على ما كان ، فهو على ما كان ، وكان على ما عليه الآن ، جل عن التغير والزوال والاستحالة والبطلان ؛ إذ ذلك أمارات الحدث التي بها عرف حدث العالم ، ودلالة احتمال الفناء ؛ إذ لا فرق بين الزوال من حال إلى حال ، ليعلم أن حاله الأولى لم تكن لذاته ؛ إذ لا يحتمل زوال ما لزم وبين أنها ليست لذاته ، لما احتمل هو قبول الأعراض وانتقال الأحوال، وذاته ولا قوة إلا بالله و بعد ، فإن فى تحقيق المكان له والوصف له بذاته فى كل مكان تمكين الحاجة له إلى ما به قراره على مثل جميع الأجسام والأعراض التي قامت بالأمكنة وفيها تقلبت وقرب، على خروج جملتها عن الوصف بالمكان ، فمن أنشأها . وأمسك كايتها لا يمكان، يتعالى عن الحاجة إلى مكان ، أو الوصف بما عليه العالم أن كليته لا في مكان ، وأنه يجزئياته في المكان
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The basis in this is that Allah, exalted is He, was and there was no place, and it is possible for places to be removed while He remains as He was. So He is as He was, and He was as He is now. He is exalted above change, removal, transformation, and annihilation; for that is the signs of origination by which the originatedness of the world is known, and it indicates the possibility of annihilation; for there is no difference between removal from state to state, to know that His first state was not for His essence; for it is impossible to remove what is necessary, and it is known that it is not for His essence, because it is possible for it (the essence) to accept accidents and transition of states. And His essence — and there is no power except with Allah. And further, in affirming place for Him and describing Him as being in every place, there is enabling the need for Him to have that by which He is established, like all bodies and accidents that subsist in places and in which they change and draw near, while all of them are outside the description of place. So the One who originated them and holds their [؟] is not with a place; He is exalted above need for a place, or the description that the world is that its entirety is not in a place, and its particulars are in a place.
Source pages p. 668

Commentary
This passage is a proof that Allah does not need a place. The author, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, begins by stating the foundational principle: Allah existed when there was no place (كان ولا مكان). Places are created and can be removed, but Allah remains unchanged. He is exalted above change, removal, transformation, and annihilation, because these are characteristics of originated things (أمارات الحدث). Change and removal indicate that the original state was not essential to the thing's essence, since what is essential cannot be removed. Similarly, if something can accept accidents and transitions, its original state is not essential. Then the author argues that attributing place to Allah would imply that He needs a location for His establishment, like bodies and accidents that subsist in places. But Allah is the Creator of all places and is not in need of a place. He is beyond the description that His entirety is not in a place while His parts are, because He is not a body.

Key Terms
مكان (place)
حدث (origination)
ذات (essence)
أعراض (accidents)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because Allah is described as 'with' creation or 'in' the heavens, He must occupy a place.
Correction: Maturidi refutes this by showing that place is a created thing. Allah existed without place before creation, and He remains independent of place. Descriptions like 'with' are metaphorical or indicate His knowledge and power, not physical location.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does Allah not need a place?
A) Because He is a spirit and spirits do not need places.
B) Because He existed without place before creation, and place is a created thing.
C) Because He is everywhere at once.
D) Because He is too great to be contained by any place.

Key Takeaway
Allah does not need a place because He existed before place, and place is a created thing; attributing place to Him would imply need and change, contradicting His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you believe Allah is not in a place, but where do you usually 'find' Him when you pray? Is your connection to Him tied to a specific location—a mosque, a prayer mat, a quiet room? What if that place were taken from you—would your sense of His presence vanish? Do you truly trust that He is as near to you in a noisy street as in the stillness of the masjid, or do you rely on geography for your faith?
?
This passage says that change and need for place are signs of createdness and contingency. Think of the one thing in your life that you cling to for security—a job, a relationship, a home. If that thing changed or disappeared, would your peace also vanish? Does your heart depend on a 'place' (a circumstance, a person) to feel safe with Allah? What would it mean to live as though Allah alone is your unchanging place?
?
Attributing place to Allah implies He needs that place, just as bodies need locations. Have you ever unconsciously treated Allah as if He were limited—expecting Him to act within the boundaries of your plans, your time, your understanding? Where in your life have you boxed Allah into a 'place' of your own making—a set of rituals, a comfortable theology? Are you ready to let go of that box and worship a God who is beyond all your categories?

Divine Transcendence

If Allah Were in a Place, He Would Be a Part of the World
Maturidi refutes that Allah is in a place because that would imply He is a part of the creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the belief that Allah is in a place is incompatible with His divinity, as argued by Imam al-Maturidi.

Core Idea
If Allah were in a place, He would be a part of the world—an impossibility for the Creator.

Imam al-Maturidi presents a crisp rational argument against the notion that Allah occupies a place. He shows that being in a location would make Allah a component of the universe, which is a sign of deficiency. Since the entire world subsists without being in a place, its Sustainer is even more beyond location. This lesson unpacks Maturidi's concise proof and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the Conditional Structure
The passage begins with a conditional statement ('if...then'). Find it and understand what the 'if' clause proposes and what its consequence would be.
The conditional is 'لو جعل في مكان' meaning 'if He were placed in a place'.
3
Understand the Implication of 'Part of the World'
Maturidi says that being in a place would make Allah a part (جزئية) of the world, which is a mark of deficiency (أثر النقصان). Reflect on why that is a problem for divinity.
A part is limited and dependent, unlike the Creator who is perfect and self-sufficient.
4
Analyze the Argument from the World's Subsistence
Maturidi then argues that the entire world subsists not by places (لا بالأمكنة للجملة). If the creation doesn't need a place to exist, its Creator is even more deserving of being independent of place.
The word 'أحق وأولى' emphasizes priority and greater right.
5
Synthesize the Conclusion
Combine the two parts: the negative argument (location implies deficiency) and the positive argument (the world itself doesn't need place). Conclude that Allah is free from spatial limitations.
Maturidi ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله', acknowledging reliance on Allah's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الله تعالى لو جعل في مكان لجعل بحق الجزئية من العالم، وذلك أثر النقصان ، بل لما استقام قيام جميع العالم لا بالأمكنة للجملة ، فقيمه على ذلك أحق وأولى ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
If Allah, exalted is He, were placed in a place, He would be placed by right of being a part of the world, and that is the mark of deficiency. Rather, since the entire world subsists not by places for the whole, then the One who sustains it is more deserving and prior [to not needing a place]. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 668

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof against Allah being in a place. Maturidi argues that if Allah were in a place, He would necessarily be a part of the world (جزئية من العالم) — a component of creation — and that is a sign of deficiency (النقصان). He then makes an argument from the world's subsistence: the entire world (جميع العالم) stands and persists not through places (لا بالأمكنة للجملة), meaning that the world as a whole is not held together by being in a place; rather, it is sustained by its Creator. Therefore, the Creator (قيمه) is even more deserving of not needing a place. The conclusion is that Allah, as the sustainer of the universe, transcends the need for location.

Key Terms
مكان (place)
جزئية (part/component)
نقصان (deficiency)
قيام (subsistence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What would it imply if Allah were in a place, according to Maturidi?
A) That He controls all places.
B) That He is a part of the world.
C) That He is greater than the world.
D) That He is hidden from creation.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Maturidi proves that Allah cannot be in a place because location would make Him a part of creation, whereas the Creator transcends all spatial limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah as being 'somewhere' — like inside a mosque or in the sky? How does Maturidi's argument challenge that mental image? What practical step can you take to correct your imagination in your next prayer?
?
When you feel distant from Allah, do you subconsciously attribute it to a physical distance? How does the truth that Allah is not limited by place change your approach to seeking closeness to Him?
?
The passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' — recognizing that all power is from Allah. How does this humble admission connect to the argument that Allah is not in a place? Will you begin your next act of worship with this phrase to realign your heart?

The Throne as a Symbol of Majesty
Maturidi introduces analogy to explain Throne's purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mention of the Throne in the Quran points to divine majesty rather than a physical location, through Maturidi's analogy of how great matters are referenced.

Core Idea
The Throne is a symbol of grandeur, not a location for Allah.

Imam al-Maturidi begins by addressing a common misinterpretation: when the Quran mentions the Throne (al-arsh), many assume it implies a physical place for Allah. To correct this, he draws on a linguistic and rational principle: references to great things are about their importance, not specificity. This lesson walks through the opening analogy that sets the stage for the entire argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening analogy
Focus on the first few lines where Maturidi compares the Throne to other grand matters like the perfection of religion. Notice how he says such references are about greatness, not specific location.
The example of 'Today I have perfected for you your religion' (5:3) is used to show that the mention of Mecca is about the religion's completion, not a place.
2
Identify the connection to the Throne
Maturidi explicitly says 'Similarly is the matter of the Throne' (فمثله أمر العرش). Understand that the Throne is mentioned for its greatness, just like other grand references.
3
Reflect on the principle
Think about the general principle: when something great is mentioned, it is to highlight its importance, not to specify a location. How does this apply to the Throne?
This principle is key to avoiding anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على موضع كذا ، لا على خصوصن ذلك في الحق ذلك في الحق ، ولكن معلوم أن من له ملك ذلك فما دونه أحق ، وعلى ذلك قوله تعالى : " اليوم أكملت لكم دينكم " بما صارت له أم القرى " وأنيس الذين كفروا من دينهم " وكذا ما ذكر من إرسال الرسل إلى الفراعنة وإلى أم القرى لا يتخصص ذلك ولكن بذكر عظم الأمر ، فمثله أمر العرش ، وهو كقوله : " أَكَابِرَ مُجْرِمِيهَا " وقوله : " أَمَرْنَا مُتْرَفِيهَا " على لحوق غير مهم ، علوه عن الأمكنة وتعاليه ، ويحتمل أن يكون على المنفى بوصف المكان إذ هو أعلى الأمكنة عند الخلق ، ولا تقدر العقول فوقه شيئا ، فأشار إليه لتعليم علوه الحاجة ، وعلى ذلك قوله : " مَا يَكُونُ مِنْ نَجْوَى ثَلَاثَةٍ إِلَّا هُوَ رَابِعُهُمْ " والنجوى ليس من نوع ما يُضاف إلى المكان ، ولكن يضاف إلى الأفراد فأخبر يعلوه عن الأمكنة وتعاليه عن أن يخفى عليه شيء ، ثم بقدرته بقوله : " وَنَحْنُ أَقْرَبُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ حَبْلِ الْوَرِيدِ " أى بالسلطان والقوة وبألوهيته في البقاع كلها ؛ لأنها أمكنة العبادة ، وبقوله : " وَهُوَ الَّذِي فِي السَّمَاءِ إِلَهٌ " ويملك كل شيء بقوله : " لَهُ مُلْكُ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ " ثم بعلوه وجلاله بقوله : " وَهُوَ الْقَاهِرُ فَوْقَ عِبَادِهِ " وقوله : " وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ " وقوله : " وَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ " فجمع في هذه الأحرف ما فرق في تلك ليُعلم أنه بكل ما سمي به ووصف كان ذلك له بذاته لا بشيء من خلقه، وكذلك عزه وشَرَفَه ومَجدُه ، جل ثناؤه عن الأشباه ، ولا إله غيره .
Upon such and such a place, not upon the specificity of that in the truth, that in the truth. But it is known that whoever possesses the dominion of that, then what is beneath it is more deserving. And upon that is His saying: 'Today I have perfected for you your religion' (5:3) because the Mother of Towns (Mecca) became his; and 'And that they may cause the disbelievers to forget their religion' (?) And likewise what is mentioned of sending messengers to the Pharaohs and to the Mother of Towns – that is not specific, but rather by mentioning the greatness of the matter. Similarly is the matter of the Throne. And it is like His saying: 'The great ones of its criminals' (?) and His saying: 'We commanded its affluent ones' (?) upon [something] not important? His highness above places and His transcendence. And it is possible that it is based on the negation of the description of place, since it is the highest of places in the creation's estimation, and intellects do not conceive anything above it. So He indicated it to teach the need for His highness. And upon that is His saying: 'There is no secret conversation of three except that He is the fourth of them' (58:7). And secret conversation is not of the kind that is attributed to place, but is attributed to individuals. So He informed of His highness above places and His transcendence from anything being hidden from Him. Then (He informed of) His power by His saying: 'And We are closer to him than the jugular vein' (50:16), meaning by dominion and strength and by His divinity in all regions, because they are places of worship. And by His saying: 'And He is the One who is in the heaven God' (43:84). And He owns everything by His saying: 'To Him belongs the dominion of the heavens and the earth' (25:2). Then by His highness and majesty by His saying: 'And He is the Subduer above His servants' (6:18). And His saying: 'And He is All-Knowing of everything' (6:101). And His saying: 'And He is All-Powerful over everything' (11:4). So He gathered in these letters what He scattered in those, so that it may be known that He, by everything He is named and described with, that is His by His essence, not from anything of His creation. Likewise His might, honor, and glory. Exalted is His praise above likenesses, and there is no god but Him.
Source pages p. 670

Commentary
This passage is an argument by Imam al-Maturidi explaining that when the Quran mentions the Throne (al-arsh) of Allah, it does not imply that Allah is confined to a spatial location. Rather, it is a reference to His majesty and greatness. Maturidi begins by drawing an analogy: when something great is mentioned, it is not meant to specify a particular instance but to highlight the greatness of the matter. For example, the verse 'Today I have perfected for you your religion' is not about a specific place but about the religion as a whole. Similarly, mentioning the Throne is about Allah's grandeur. He then notes that the Throne is the highest thing in creation's imagination, so it is used to teach the need for Allah's transcendence above all places. He supports this with verses that affirm Allah's closeness (as in the secret conversation verse) and His being with His servants everywhere by His knowledge and power, not by physical presence. He then cites verses about Allah's power, knowledge, and dominion over everything. The conclusion is that all these attributes belong to Allah by His essence, not derived from His creation, and He is exalted above any resemblance to created things.

Key Terms
العرش (al-arsh, the Throne)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Throne is a physical place where Allah resides.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that mentioning the Throne is about Allah's greatness, not about a location.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the Quran mention the Throne?
A) To specify that Allah sits on it
B) To indicate Allah's greatness and majesty
C) To show it is the highest created object
D) To prove Allah has a body

Key Takeaway
The Throne is mentioned in the Quran to highlight Allah's majesty, not to assign a place to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses about the Throne, do you instinctively imagine a physical structure? How does Maturidi's principle that 'mentioning greatness is not about location' challenge that image, and what must your heart do to accept a God beyond space?
?
If the Throne is a symbol of majesty rather than a place, then why do you still find yourself trying to 'locate' Allah in your mind? What does that impulse reveal about your need for control, and how can you train your soul to be content with transcendence?
?
Maturidi uses the verse 'Today I have perfected for you your religion' to argue that the mention of Mecca is about greatness, not specificity. Is there any part of your practice that you have confined to a particular time or place, forgetting that Allah's command is universal and majestic? What change does this demand from you starting today?

Allah's Highness Beyond Place
Maturidi discusses Throne as highest place and closeness verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the Throne as the highest created thing to affirm Allah's transcendence above all places, and how Quranic verses on closeness (e.g., secret conversation) reinforce that His presence is by knowledge, not physical location.

Core Idea
Allah's highness is above all places; His closeness is by knowledge, not physical presence.

In the previous lesson, we learned that the Throne is mentioned for its greatness. Now Maturidi deepens the argument: the Throne is the highest thing in creation, so it is used to teach that Allah is above all places. He then addresses the apparent paradox of divine closeness, using the secret conversation verse to show that Allah is with us by knowledge and power, not by being in a location.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Throne as the highest place
Maturidi notes that the Throne is considered the highest place in creation. Intellects cannot conceive anything above it. So Allah uses it to point to His transcendence, not to indicate He is located there.
This is a pedagogical tool: the highest created thing points to the Creator's infinite highness.
2
Examine the secret conversation verse
Read the verse 'There is no secret conversation of three except that He is the fourth of them' (58:7). Maturidi explains that 'conversation' is not a place but an action of individuals. Thus, this shows Allah's knowledge encompasses all, not that He is physically present.
The verse is about divine omniscience, not spatial presence.
3
Connect to Allah's transcendence
Maturidi concludes that these verses inform us of Allah's highness above places and His transcendence from being hidden from anything. Allah is close by His knowledge and power, not by location.
This refutes anthropomorphists who take such verses literally.

Divine Attributes by Essence
Maturidi concludes with verses on divine attributes by essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi gathers Quranic verses on power, knowledge, and dominion to conclude that all divine attributes belong to Allah by His essence, not derived from His creation, and that He is exalted above all likenesses.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are intrinsic to His essence, not borrowed from creation.

Having established that the Throne indicates majesty and that Allah is transcendent above places, Maturidi now ties everything together. He cites a series of Quranic verses that describe Allah's attributes—power, knowledge, dominion, subduing—and argues that these prove all divine qualities belong to Allah by His very essence, not from any created thing. This final lesson assembles the complete argument and concludes that Allah is exalted above any likeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of Quranic verses
Maturidi quotes several verses: about power (nearness to the jugular vein), divinity in heaven, dominion, subduing, knowledge, and power over all things. Notice how each verse highlights a different attribute.
These verses are from various chapters: 50:16, 43:84, 25:2, 6:18, 6:101, 11:4.
2
Understand the gathering of attributes
Maturidi says Allah 'gathered in these letters what He scattered in those'—meaning these comprehensive verses summarize the attributes scattered in other verses. This shows that all attributes are true of Allah by His essence.
The phrase 'by His essence' means His attributes are not acquired from creation.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that Allah is described by might, honor, and glory, exalted above any resemblance to created things, and there is no god but Him. This is the doxological end of the passage.
This reinforces tawhid and refutes any anthropomorphism.

Three Linguistic Interpretations of Istiwa
Maturidi reports three linguistic meanings of istiwa with Qur'anic and poetic support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three linguistic meanings of istiwa (establishing) as reported by Maturidi and how they serve to maintain divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Istiwa does not mean sitting; it means conquest, elevation, or completion.

Imam Maturidi presents a linguistic analysis of the Qur'anic term istiwa, showing that it has multiple meanings beyond the physical act of sitting. By citing classical Arabic usage and poetry, he demonstrates that istiwa can denote conquest, elevation, or completion. This lesson explores those three interpretations and their significance for avoiding anthropomorphism regarding Allah's attribute of istiwa.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation. Notice how Maturidi first explains the meaning of 'arsh' before listing the three meanings of istiwa.
The passage includes poetic lines that illustrate metaphorical uses of 'arsh'.
2
Identify the three meanings
Find the three interpretations of istiwa: istila (conquest), 'uluww (elevation), and tamam (completion). Note the Qur'anic examples given for each.
3
Examine the literary argument
Maturidi mentions that some scholars interpreted 'istawa ila as-sama' as 'He created' by analogy. Reflect on why this metaphorical interpretation is used to avoid anthropomorphism.
The poetry at the end reinforces that 'arsh' can mean dominion, not a physical throne.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Understand that these linguistic options help Muslims interpret Qur'anic verses about Allah's attribute of istiwa without likening Him to created beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم : يريد بالعرش الملك ؛ إذ هو اسم ما ارتفع من الأشياء وعلا حتى سمي به السطوح ورؤوس الأشجار . والاستواء قيل فيه بأوجه ثلاثة : أحدها الاستيلاء ، كما يقال : استوى فلان على كورة كذا بمعنى استولى عليها ، والثاني العلو والارتفاع كقوله : فَإِذَا اسْتَوَيْتَ أَنتَ وَمَنْ مَعَكَ عَلَى الْفُلْكِ ، والثالث التمام كقوله تعالى : وَلَمَّا بَلَغَ أَشُدَّهُ وَاسْتَوَى ، وقد قيل بالقصد إلى ذلك وجه بعض أهل الأدب قوله : ثُمَّ اسْتَوَى إِلَى السَّمَاءِ بمعنى خلق على التمثيل بفعل الخلق فيما يتلو فعلهم أن يكون بالقصد ، وإن كان لا يقال : له قصد ، ولا قوة إلا بالله قال الشاعر : ظننت أن عرشك لا يزول ولا يغير وقال آخر : إذا ما بنو مروان ثلت عروشهم وقال النابغة : وأود وا كما أودت اباد وحمير عروش تفانوا بعد عز وأنهم هووا بعد ما نالوا السلامة والغنى
And some of them said: He intends by the Throne the dominion, because it is the name for whatever is elevated from things and high, to the extent that surfaces and treetops are named by it. And regarding istiwa, three aspects are mentioned: one of them is conquest (istila), as it is said: 'so-and-so istawa over such-and-such a territory,' meaning he conquered it. The second is elevation and height, like His saying: 'When you and those with you are established (istawaytum) on the ship' (23:28). The third is completion, like His saying: 'And when he reached his maturity and became established (istawa)' (28:14). And it has been said with intent to that, some people of literature interpreted His saying: 'Then He turned (istawa) to the heaven' (41:11) as meaning He created, by analogy to the action of created beings in that their action is with intent, although it cannot be said that He has intent. And there is no power except with Allah. The poet said: 'I thought that your throne would not perish nor change.' And another said: 'When the Marwanids destroy their thrones.' And al-Nabigha said: 'They perished, as perished Ibad and Himyar, thrones that vanished after glory, and they fell after attaining safety and wealth.'
Source pages p. 671

Commentary
This passage reports views of others (not necessarily Maturidi's own opinion) on the interpretation of the Throne and istiwa. First, some said 'arsh means 'kingdom' or 'dominion', because the word 'arsh linguistically refers to anything elevated, like roofs and treetops. Then three possible meanings of istiwa are given: 1) istila (conquest, domination) – when someone takes control of a region; 2) 'uluww (elevation) – as in being on a ship; 3) tamam (completion, reaching maturity) – as in a person reaching full strength. Some literary scholars interpreted the verse 'Then He istawa to the heaven' as meaning 'He created', by analogy with created beings who act with intention, though they acknowledge that terms like 'intention' cannot be applied to Allah in the same way. The passage ends with poetic quotations using 'throne' in metaphorical contexts to support that the word can refer to rule or power, not a physical seat.

Key Terms
الاستواء
الاستيلاء
العلو
التمام
العرش
الملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Istiwa means sitting or settling on the Throne in a physical sense.
Correction: Linguistically, istiwa has other meanings such as conquest, elevation, or completion, which are used in the Qur'an and Arabic speech, thus avoiding anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three meanings of istiwa mentioned in this passage?
A) istila (conquest)
B) 'uluww (elevation)
C) sujud (prostration)
D) tamam (completion)

Key Takeaway
Istiwa is a multifaceted term that must be understood according to linguistic usage, not physical modality, to preserve divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah's istiwa as a physical sitting, like a king on a throne? If so, what will you actively do to replace that image with the linguistic meanings (conquest, elevation, completion) taught in this lesson?
?
This lesson shows that understanding Arabic is essential to avoid misrepresenting Allah. Are you willing to invest time and effort to learn Arabic so that you can read the Qur'an and hadith with accurate understanding, or will you rely on others' translations that may miss these nuances?
?
When you recite or hear verses about Allah's actions, does your mind immediately form a mental picture? How will you train yourself to stop at the meaning given in revelation and not go beyond it, trusting that Allah's reality is beyond human imagination?

The Three Interpretations of Istawa and the Meaning of al-Arsh
Maturidi enumerates three interpretations of istawa and explains al-arsh as kingdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's report of three classical interpretations of 'istawa' (settling) and the meaning of 'al-arsh' as dominion, all without implying physicality or anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Istawa means conquest, elevation, or completion — never literal sitting.

Maturidi begins by establishing that God's attributes belong to His essence, not to anything created. He then turns to the Quranic terms 'arsh' and 'istawa', reporting the interpretations of earlier scholars. The term 'arsh' is explained as a metaphor for kingdom, and 'istawa' is given three possible meanings: conquest, elevation, or completion. This lesson explores these interpretations and their significance for maintaining divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic from the beginning through the three interpretations. Notice that the author first grounds his discussion in God's essential attributes before addressing the specific terms.
The opening affirms that all attributes belong to Allah by essence.
2
Identify the three interpretations
List each of the three meanings given for 'istawa' and note the evidence provided for each from the Quran or Arabic usage.
3
Consider the supplementary opinion
Reflect on the additional interpretation from literary scholars that 'istawa to the heaven' means 'created' by analogy with human action, and the caveat that intention cannot be attributed to Allah.
This shows the careful avoidance of anthropomorphism.
4
Connect to the principle of tawhid
Ask yourself: How do these interpretations help maintain the absolute transcendence of Allah?
This passage shows that classical scholars prioritized linguistic and rational flexibility to avoid anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : وهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ ، فجمع في هذه الأحرف ما فرق في [٣٤ ب] تلك ليُعلم أنه بكل ما سمي به ووصف / كان ذلك له بذاته لا بشيء من خلقه، وكذلك عزه وشَرَفَه ومَجدُه ، جل ثناؤه عن الأشباه ، ولا إله غيره . وقال بعضهم : يريد بالعرش الملك ؛ إذ هو اسم ما ارتفع من الأشياء وعلا حتی سمی به السطوح ورؤوس الأشجار . والاستواء قيل فيه بأوجه ثلاثة : أحدها الاستيلاء ، كما يقال : استوى فلان على كورة ٣ كذا بمعنى استولى عليها ، والثانى العلو والارتفاع كقوله : فَإِذَا اسْتَوَيْتَ أَنتَ وَمَنْ مَعَكَ عَلَى الفُلْكِ، والثالث التمام كقوله تعالى : ولَمَّا بَلَغَ أَشُدَّهُ واسْتَوَى ، وقد قيل بالقصد إلى ذلك وجه بعض أهل الأدب قوله : ثُمَّ استوى إلى السَّمَاءِ " بمعنى خلق على التمثيل بفعل الخلق فيما يتلو فعلهم أن يكون بالقصد ، وإن كان لا يقال : له قصد ، ولا قوة إلا بالله قال الشاعر : ظننت أن عرشك لا يزول ولا يغير وقال آخر : إذا ما بنو مروان ثلت عروشهم وقال النابغة : وأود وا كما أودت اباد وحمير عروش تفانوا بعد عز وأنهم هووا بعد ما نالوا السلامة والغنى وقال آخر : بعد ابن جفنة وابن. ماثل عرشه والمحاربين تؤملون فلاحاً
And His statement: 'And He is over all things competent' (Quran 2:106, etc.). So He gathered in these letters what He distributed in those [other verses] that it may be known that He, by everything by which He is named and described, that belongs to Him by His essence, not by any of His creation. Likewise His might, honor, and glory, exalted is His praise above likenesses, and there is no god but Him. And some of them said: He intends by the Throne (al-arsh) the dominion (al-mulk); for it (al-arsh) is the name of what is elevated from things and high, so that by it the roofs and treetops are named. And concerning istawa (settling/establishing) it is said in three aspects: the first is conquest (al-istila), as it is said, 'So-and-so istawa over such-and-such a district' meaning he conquered it; the second is elevation and height (al-uluww wa al-irtifa), as His statement: 'And when you are settled (istawayta) you and those with you on the ship' (Quran 23:28); the third is completion (al-tamam), as His statement, the Exalted: 'And when he reached his maturity and became complete (istawa)' (Quran 28:14). And it has been said with intent to that, the direction of some people of literature, His statement: 'Then He directed Himself (istawa) to the heaven' (Quran 41:11), meaning created, by way of analogy with the action of creatures, that their action entails intention, even though it is not said that He has intention, and there is no power except through Allah. The poet said: I thought that your throne (arsh) would not cease nor change. And another said: When the sons of Marwan, their thrones (urushuhum) were overturned. And al-Nabigha said: They perished, as perished Iyad and Himyar, thrones that vanished after might, and they fell after they had attained safety and wealth. And another said: After Ibn Jafna and Ibn..., his throne (arshuhu) tottered, and (you) hope for success while fighting?
Source pages p. 671

Commentary
This passage presents an extended commentary on two Quranic themes: God's absolute power and the meaning of al-arsh (Throne) and istawa (settling/establishing). It begins by explaining the verse 'He is over all things competent' as demonstrating that all of God's attributes belong to Him by His essence, not through anything created. Then it transitions to a report from 'some' (probably earlier exegetes) who interpret al-arsh as kingdom (al-mulk), noting that the word 'arsh' linguistically refers to anything elevated. The core of the passage is the enumeration of three interpretations of istawa: first, conquest (al-istila), as in 'istawa over a district' meaning conquered; second, elevation and height (al-uluww wa al-irtifa), citing the verse about being settled on the ship; third, completion (al-tamam), citing the verse about reaching maturity. A further opinion is mentioned, attributed to some literary scholars, that istawa toward the heaven means 'created' by analogy with human actions that involve intention. The passage then supports the linguistic usage with four poetic examples: the poet who thinks his throne (meaning his sovereignty) will not change; the one referencing the downfall of the Marwanid thrones; al-Nabigha's lines about vanished thrones of ancient tribes; and a final poet mentioning the tottering of Ibn Jafna's throne. These verses illustrate that 'arsh' is used metaphorically for kingdom or power. The passage thus presents the spectrum of interpretations without yet giving the author's own preference.

Key Terms
العرش (al-arsh)
الملك (al-mulk)
الاستواء (al-istawa)
الاستيلاء (al-istila)
العلو والارتفاع (al-uluww wa al-irtifa)
التمام (al-tamam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'istawa' means Allah physically settling on the Throne in a literal, spatial sense.
Correction: Maturidi shows that classical scholars understood 'istawa' metaphorically as conquest, elevation, or completion, none of which imply physical location or body.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many distinct interpretations of 'istawa' are mentioned?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Maturidi reports that 'istawa' has three possible interpretations — conquest, elevation, and completion — all of which avoid literal anthropomorphism, consistent with the principle that God's attributes belong to His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, what images or concepts come to your mind? Are you subtly visualizing a being with form or location? How does this lesson's linguistic flexibility challenge you to purify your conception of Allah from any resemblance to creation?
?
The passage shows that classical scholars used multiple linguistic interpretations to protect Allah's transcendence. Do you ever find yourself preferring simple, literal understandings over nuanced theology? Are you willing to engage with deeper learning to avoid misrepresenting Allah, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Poetic Evidence for al-Arsh as Kingdom
Maturidi cites four poetic examples to show 'arsh' means kingdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Arabic poetry supports the interpretation of 'al-arsh' as kingdom or sovereignty, reinforcing the metaphorical reading of the Throne.

Core Idea
Poets used 'arsh' to mean authority or kingship, confirming the metaphorical understanding.

After presenting the three interpretations of 'istawa', Maturidi adduces verses from Arabic poetry to demonstrate that the word 'arsh' is commonly employed to mean kingdom or royal power. By citing poets like al-Nabigha, he shows that the Quranic usage of 'arsh' is consistent with this figurative meaning. This lesson examines these poetic examples and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic verses in Arabic
Read each line of poetry carefully. Notice how 'arsh' and its plural 'urush' are used in contexts of power and downfall.
2
Identify the common theme
Each verse speaks of thrones being overthrown or tottering. What does that imply about the meaning of 'arsh'?
The verses clearly refer to dynasties and rule.
3
Connect to the theological argument
Reflect on how these verses support the interpretation of al-arsh as dominion, not a physical object.
This is a key example of lexical evidence in kalam.

Maturidi's Interpretation of 'Istawa' and 'Al-Arsh'
Maturidi's analysis of interpretations of 'istawa'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument for why 'istawa' should be interpreted as completion and elevation rather than conquest, and how this relates to God's sovereignty over creation.

Core Idea
Maturidi refutes 'istawa as conquest' and defends 'completion and elevation' as the correct meaning.

Imam Maturidi carefully examines two possible interpretations of the Quranic phrase 'istawa over the Throne.' He finds the common interpretation of 'conquest' problematic, because it would imply God is accountable for His creation. Instead, he argues that 'istawa' means the completion and elevation of divine dominion, especially manifest through the creation of humans. In this lesson, we will follow his reasoning through the Quranic evidence he presents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Let the flow of Maturidi's argument come through before interpreting.
The passage has an OCR issue at the end; focus on the main argument.
2
Understand the two interpretations
Maturidi discusses two possible meanings for 'istawa': conquest (istila) and completion/elevation (tamam/uluww). Identify where he presents each.
He clearly prefers the second.
3
Analyze his critique of 'istila'
Maturidi argues that if 'istawa' meant conquest, then God would be 'questioned about all His creation'—a problematic implication. Also note the verse 'Lord of the Mighty Throne' supporting the meaning of dominion.
4
Follow the evidence for 'tamam' and 'uluww'
Maturidi cites creation verses to show that 'istawa' refers to the completion of dominion when humans were created. Notice how he links the creation of the world to the purpose of humans.
This is the heart of his argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم الوجه فى ذلك لو كان على الاستيلاء ، والعرش الملك، إنه مسئول على جميع خلقه، وعلى هذا التأويل المحمول غير هذا، يدل على الأمرين قوله تعالى]: وَهُوَ رَبِّ العَرْشِ العظيم" بمعنى الملك العظيم، وفيه إثبات عروش غيره ، فذلك يتحتمل ما يحمل ويحف به الملائكة ، والله الموفق وأمنا على التمام والعلو فهو أن الله تعالى / قال : قُلْ أَئِنَّكُمْ لَتَكْفُرُونَ بِالَّذِي خَلَقَ الأَرْضِ فِي يَوْمَيْنِ ٢ إلى قوله : فَقَضَاهُنَّ سَبْعَ سَمَاوَاتٍ ۳ فأخبر بخلق ما ذكر في ستة أيام على التفاريق، ثم أجملها في موضع فقال : إِنْ الله الذي خَلَقَ السَّمَاوَاتِ والأَرْضِ فِي سِتَّةِ أَيَّامٍ ثُمَّ اسْتَوَى عَلَى العَرْشِ ، بمعنى خلق الممتحن من خلق الأرض والسماوات فيهم ظهر تمام الملك وعلا وارتفع ، المقصودون من خلق ما بينا ، فبذلك تم معنى الملك وعلا إذا وصل إلى الذين لهم. وقد قبل ذا في خلق البشر خاصة بقوله : هُوَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ لَكُمْ مَا في وقوله : وسَخَّرَ لَكُمُ اللَّيْلَ وَالنَّهَارَ " . وقوله : وسَخَّرَ لَكُمْ مَا فِي السَّمَاوَاتِ وَمَا فِي الأرْضِ . وذكر ابن عباس رضي الله عنه أن البشر خلق هم
Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Then the consideration in that is: if it were upon conquest (al-istila), and the Throne (al-arsh) is the dominion (al-mulk), then He would be asked about all of His creation. And upon this interpretation other than this is carried. The evidence for both matters is His statement, the Exalted: 'And He is the Lord of the Mighty Throne' (Quran 9:129), meaning the Mighty Dominion, and in it there is an affirmation of thrones (urush) other than His. That would then bear what is carried and surrounded by the angels, and Allah is the Giver of success. And as for completion (al-tamam) and elevation (al-uluww), it is that Allah, the Exalted, said: 'Say, do you indeed disbelieve in He who created the earth in two days?' (Quran 41:9) up to His statement: 'Then He completed them as seven heavens' (Quran 41:12). So He informed of the creation of what was mentioned in six days in a distributed manner. Then He summarized them in a place and said: 'Indeed your Lord is Allah who created the heavens and the earth in six days, then settled (istawa) over the Throne' (Quran 10:3). Meaning He created the one who is tested from the creation of the earth and the heavens, in them appeared the completion of the dominion, and it rose and became high, those who are intended from the creation of what we have explained. So by that the meaning of dominion became complete and elevated when it reached those for whom it was. And this has been said specifically about the creation of humans by His statement: 'He who created for you what is in' (Quran 2:29) and His statement: 'And He subjected to you the night and the day' (Quran 14:33). And His statement: 'And He subjected to you whatever is in the heavens and whatever is in the earth' (Quran 45:13). And Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, mentioned that humans were created them [?]
Source pages p. 672

Commentary
This passage contains Maturidi's own analysis and preferred interpretation of the meanings of al-arsh and istawa. He begins by critically examining the conquest (istila) interpretation: if al-arsh means dominion and istawa means conquest, then God would be questioned about all His creation—an implication he finds problematic. He notes that the Quranic verse 'Lord of the Mighty Throne' supports the meaning of 'Mighty Dominion,' and that verse implies the existence of other thrones (urush), which could refer to physical thrones that angels carry and surround. Then Maturidi turns to the completion (tamam) and elevation (uluww) interpretation, which he favors. He supports this by citing the creation sequence in Surah Fussilat: the earth and heavens were created in six days, and then summarised in another verse as 'created heavens and earth in six days, then istawa over the Throne.' He explains this as meaning that after creating the heavens and earth, the 'completion of dominion' appeared when the intended purpose—namely, the creation of humans—was realized. This is further supported by verses stating that everything was created for humans and subjected to them. The passage concludes with a reference to Ibn Abbas reporting that humans were created (the text breaks off). Thus, Maturidi prefers interpreting istawa as completion and elevation tied to the manifestation of divine sovereignty over creation, especially over humans as the purpose of creation.

Key Terms
الاستيلاء (al-istila)
الملك (al-mulk)
التمام (al-tamam)
العلو (al-uluww)
ربه العرش العظيم (rabb al-arsh al-azim)
الملائكة (al-malaika)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi simply accepts all three interpretations equally, but here he actively argues against the conquest interpretation and favors completion and elevation.
Correction: Maturidi clearly prefers the tamam/uluww interpretation over istila, based on Quranic evidence and rational implications.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what problem arises if 'istawa' is interpreted as conquest ('istila')?
A) It would imply God has a physical body
B) It would mean God is questioned about all of creation
C) It would contradict the verses about creation
D) It would imply change in God's essence

Key Takeaway
Maturidi favors interpreting 'istawa' as the completion and elevation of God's dominion, which becomes manifest with the creation of humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The interpretation of 'istawa' as conquest implies God would be questioned about His creation. Do you sometimes question God's actions or decrees in your life, acting as if He owes you an explanation? What would it look like to surrender that demand and trust His wisdom without condition?
?
All creation is subjected to you, according to the verses cited. Are you using the resources, time, and abilities God has entrusted to you as a servant of His purpose, or as a master of your own desires? What is one concrete change you will make this week to align your use of creation with the purpose of being a witness to God?

Refutation of Ka'bi's View on God's Location
Maturidi refutes Ka'bi's claim that God is in every place by knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of Ka'bi's interpretation that God is in every place by knowledge, and why he argues it implies change.

Core Idea
Ka'bi's own argument against place in God leads to the change he denies.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi engages with a Mu'tazili scholar al-Ka'bi, who attempts to reconcile God's transcendence with the phrase 'God is in every place' by claiming it means knowledge. Maturidi exposes the logical contradictions in this view, showing that it inadvertently attributes change to God. The discussion is a classic example of kalam reasoning about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read Ka'bi's initial argument
Identify his two premises: God cannot be in a place because that would imply need and change, and then his interpretation of 'in every place' as knowledge.
Notice the structure: Ka'bi first negates place, then reinterprets the phrase.
2
Maturidi's first counter-argument
See how Maturidi points out that Ka'bi's own position involves a change from not being in a place to being related to places (by knowledge).
3
Critique of Ka'bi's theology
Maturidi shows that Ka'bi's claim that God became Creator, Merciful, etc. after not being so is itself a change, contradicting his denial of change.
4
Analogy from the witnessed world
Understand Maturidi's argument that change in place does not necessarily entail change in essence, and if Ka'bi denies change in place for God, he should also deny change in action.
5
Final contradiction
Maturidi shows that Ka'bi's interpretation reduces to saying God is in places as preserving or acting, which brings back the spatial notion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الكعبي مرة : لا يجوز أن يكون الله عز وجل يحويه مكان؛ لما كان ولا مكان، لم يجز أن يحدث له حاجة إلى المكان إذ خلقه؛ لما لا يجوز عليه التغير. ثم قال : هو في كل مكان على معنى أنه عالم به، حافظ له كما يقال : فلان في بناء الدار أي في فعله. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : فما قال بأنه لا يحويه مكان بما كان ولا مكان خطأ الحق؛ إذ ذلك تغير. والقول بالحاجة لا يقوله خصمه فتعليق الدفع به ثم هو يزعم أنه كان غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا متكلم ثم صار كذلك بعد أن لم يكن ثبت به التغير، بل التغير في المكان من حيث أن يصير المرء في مكان لم يكن فيه بلا تغير نحو أن يتخذ له مكان يحيط به، ولا يجوز أن يوجد تغير من حيث لا تغير في ذات الفاعل في الشاهد، وإذ منع القول بهذا في المكان فهو في الفعل أولى؛ إذ يكون التغير فيه أشد وأولى ما لا يكون أحاد في من الشاهد فاعلا بلا تغير يعترضه، وجائز كونه في مكان، وهو الذي فيه خلق لا تغير؛ لذلك كان معنى التغير في الفعل أشد، والله الموفق. العجب في قوله : هو في كل مكان بمعنى العالم وهو بذاته عنده ليس في مكان، ولا تحقق الله علما ليبلغ المكان الذي قال : فيه تأملوا لتفهموا تناقضه في القول ... أنه يحفظه مرة، ومرة أنه يفعله، وحفظه وفعله في الأمكنة ليس غير الأمكنة، فصار حاصل قوله : الله في كل مكان في الأمكنة، وذلك خلف عالم بالأمكنة كلها قبل كونها وبعد كونها، والله الموفق من القول، بل هو قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : وأما رفع الأيدي إلى السماء فعلى العبادة
Al-Ka'bi once said: It is not permissible that Allah be contained by a place, because when He was and there was no place, it is not permissible that a need for place arises when He creates it; because change is not permissible upon Him. Then he said: He is in every place in the sense that He is knowledgeable of it, preserver of it, just as it is said: 'So-and-so is in building the house,' meaning in his action. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: What he said—that He is not contained by a place because He was and there was no place—is wrong, because that would be change. And the statement about need is not said by his opponent, so his attaching of the refutation to it [is invalid]. Then he claims that He was not Creator, nor Merciful, nor Speaker, then He became so after not being—this establishes change. Rather, the change with respect to place is with regard to a person coming to be in a place he was not in with change, like taking for himself a place that encompasses him. And it is not permissible that a change occurs where there is no change in the essence of the agent in the witnessed world. And if he prevents the statement about this concerning place, then concerning action it is more appropriate, because change in it is more severe and more appropriate—since there is no one in the witnessed world who is an agent without change overtaking him. And it is possible for Him to be in a place while He creates in it without change; therefore the meaning of change in action is more severe. And Allah is the Granter of success. Astonishing in his statement: 'He is in every place in the sense of the Knower, while He in His essence is not in a place,' and Allah is not realized as knowledge to reach the place. He said: 'Contemplate to understand his contradiction in the statement... that He preserves it once, and once that He does it, and preserving it and doing it in places are nothing but the places. So the result of his statement is: Allah is in every place in the places, and that is contrary to the Knower of all places before their coming to be and after their coming to be. And Allah is the Granter of success in statement. Rather, the faqih Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: As for raising the hands to the heaven, it is for worship.
Source pages p. 674

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument by al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili theologian, regarding God's relation to place. Ka'bi first argues that God cannot be contained in a place because He existed before places, and acquiring a place would imply change and need. He then interprets 'God is in every place' to mean that God knows and preserves every place, similar to saying a person is 'in' building a house meaning engaged in the action. Abu Mansur (Maturidi) responds by pointing out several problems. First, Ka'bi's own argument that God was without place and then later had a relation to place would itself imply change, because a new relation arises. Second, Ka'bi's opponent (presumably those who affirm God's presence in a literal sense) do not claim that God needs a place; so Ka'bi's refutation of a need is irrelevant. Third, Ka'bi's claim that God became Creator, Merciful, Speaker after not being so is itself a change. Maturidi then argues that change in place (like moving into a new location) does not necessarily entail change in essence, and in the witnessed world agents act without change in essence. If Ka'bi denies change in place for God, then he should also deny change in action, because action involves more change. Then Maturidi points out a contradiction in Ka'bi's position: Ka'bi says God is in every place as Knower but not in His essence, but then his interpretation reduces to saying God is in places as preserving or acting, which are activities in places. Thus Ka'bi ends up affirming God's presence in places anyway. The passage concludes with a note about raising hands to heaven in prayer, which is an act of worship, perhaps implying that Allah is not physically in heaven but the direction is for reverence.

Key Terms
مكان
تحيز
تغير
العلم
الحفظ
الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi's critique of Ka'bi implies that God is literally in a place.
Correction: Maturidi rejects both literal containment and Ka'bi's metaphorical interpretation. He affirms transcendence while explaining that raising hands to heaven is for worship, not to indicate spatial presence.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw Maturidi finds in Ka'bi's argument that God cannot be in a place because He existed before places?
A) Ka'bi's argument itself implies change in God.
B) Ka'bi's argument is based on a verse taken out of context.
C) Ka'bi's argument denies God's omnipresence.
D) Ka'bi's argument contradicts the Quranic statement about the Throne.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that interpreting God's omnipresence as mere knowledge leads to logical contradictions and implies the very change it seeks to avoid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you raise your hands in supplication towards the heavens, where do you truly believe Allah is? Does your heart feel He is there, or does your intellect affirm He is beyond space? This lesson forces you to reconcile your worship with your theology. Will you examine your own assumptions, or continue with comfortable contradictions?
?
Al-Ka'bi tried to solve the tension between God's transcendence and His omnipresence by reducing His presence to mere knowledge. Have you ever reduced your relationship with Allah to a mere intellectual acknowledgment, avoiding the pressing reality that He is intimately aware of every moment of your life? This lesson asks you: do you live as if Allah's knowledge of your actions is enough to change your behavior, or do you need the weight of His actual presence?
?
Maturidi's refutation exposes how our attempts to protect God's transcendence can actually lead to self-contradiction. What are the areas in your own understanding of Allah where you might be holding contradictory beliefs? Are you willing to let go of comfortable but inconsistent ideas about Him, even if it means rethinking long-held teachings?
