Allah’s Transcendence of Time and Space
Maturidi affirms Allah's transcendence of time and space.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah is not described by physical proximity or distance, and that He transcends both time and space because they are created limits.

Core Idea
Allah was without place before creation, and remains so.

Building on the rejection of spatial location, Maturidi now clarifies that nothing can be described as physically near or far from Allah. Distance and proximity belong to the realm of created limits. Since Allah existed before space and time, He is exalted above them. This lesson explores the implications of this transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Maturidi denies both nearness and farness in physical terms for Allah.
This is a direct negation of anthropomorphic concepts.
2
Identify the reason for the negation
He explains that physical proximity implies limits and measurement by places. Understand why that is incompatible with Allah.
Limits are associated with created objects.
3
Connect to pre-eternity
Reflect on the statement 'He was and there was no place' – this shows that Allah's existence does not depend on space or time.
This is a foundational principle in Islamic theology.
4
Grasp the conclusion
He concludes that Allah is exalted above time and space because all limits return to them.
Time and space are themselves created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And upon that, the statement of one who describes Him as being in every place is invalid; for there is no difference between a single place attributed to Him and the totality. Rather, the single in declaring His glorification is more appropriate, because in that there is specification of that thing by mention, and in mention there is honor and veneration. So it returns to mention upon that thing, and in the sending and gathering of all to His specification and reality is an attribute of God, as it is said: 'Lord of everything' and 'God of everything', upon the glorification and veneration of the Lord. And nothing is described as near to God by way of distance and area, nor is He near to anything from that aspect, because that is the direction of limits and measurement by places, while He was and there was no place. So He is as He was, exalted above time and space, for to them return the limits and ends of things.
Source pages p. 705

Commentary
Maturidi refutes the view that God is in every place (bi kulli makan) by arguing that if one place is inappropriate for God, then all places are equally inappropriate. Specifying a particular place, like the Throne, is for the honor of that place, not for God's limitation. Analogously, saying 'Lord of Ibrahim' honors Ibrahim. So attributing God to all places would be ascribing Him to the lowly, which is repugnant. He then concludes that God is transcendent above time and space (al-zaman wa al-makan), because they are the measures of limitations.

Key Terms
القرب
المسافة
الزمان
المكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah is close to His servants in a physical or spatial sense.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that nearness to Allah cannot be measured by distance; it is a spiritual closeness through obedience.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say nothing can be described as near to Allah by distance?
A) Because Allah is everywhere
B) Because distance implies limits and measurement by places
C) Because the Qur'an says so
D) Because nearness is only for prophets

Key Takeaway
Allah is transcendent over time and space, and physical nearness or farness does not apply to Him.

?
You pray in a mosque, a physical place. But this lesson says Allah is not in any place. How does that affect your understanding of where you direct your worship—and does it challenge your attachment to sacred spaces?

Allah's Transcendence of Place and the Throne
Maturidi argues against attributing place to Allah and refutes omnipresence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that Allah is transcendent beyond place, and that attributing the Throne or any place to Him is for veneration, not location.

Core Idea
Allah existed without place and remains so; space does not contain Him.

Maturidi tackles a subtle issue: how to understand Qur'anic references to the Throne and attributes like 'Lord of everything' without implying physical location for Allah. He argues that such attributions are for magnifying created things, not for locating the Creator. This passage establishes the absolute transcendence of Allah over space and time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice Maturidi's dense reasoning style.
2
Identify the foundational principle
Look at the opening lines: 'Allah existed and there was no place.' This is the cornerstone. Everything else builds on this.
3
Follow the argument about attribution
Observe how Maturidi explains that specifying attributions (like 'Lord of the Throne') is for honoring the thing, not for locating Allah. Then see how he refutes the view that Allah is everywhere.
He uses an analogy with 'Lord of everything' and 'God of Ibrahim'.
4
Grasp the final denial of nearness
The passage ends by rejecting any notion of distance or dimension in relation to Allah. Reflect on why this conclusion follows from the principle.
This ties back to Allah's pre-eternal existence without place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي ما يكون ذلك الاعتقاد عن علم تقدم بحال من يضاف إليه ذلك في الشاهد قبل الإضافة من الاحتمال، ثم الله سبحانه كان ولا مكان، وعلى ذلك اعتقاد الأنام، لم يجز أن يتغير الفهم عن الإضافة عما كان من قبل، وإليه ينصرف الفهم عن الإضافة إلى خلقه. على أن تخصيص إضافات الأشياء إلى الله في الشاهد يخرج مخرج التعظيم لها بما جعل فيها من الأمور المرضية والأحوال المحدودة، فما بال العرش من بين ذلك؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى ذلك يفسد قول من يصفه بكل مكان؛ إذ لا فرق بين مكان واحد يضاف إليه وبين الجملة، بل الفرد فى بيان تعظيمه أولى؛ إذ في ذلك تخصيص ذلك الشيء بالذكر، وفى الذكر تشريف وتكريم، فيرجع إلى ذكر على ذلك الشيء، وفى الإرسال وجمع الكل إلى تخصيصه وحقيقته صفة الله كما يقال: رب كل شيء وإله كل شيء، على تعظيم الرب وتبجيله، وإذا قيل: رب حميد، وإله ابراهيم، فإنما يقصد قصد تشريفها وتعظيمها، فقياس ذلك أن تكون الإضافة إلى العرش توجب تعظيم العرش وتكريمه، وإلى كل الأمكنة وصف الله بها، وذلك قبيح؛ إذ لم يكن يوصف به في الأزل. ولا يوصف شيء بالقرب إلى الله من طريق المسافة والمساحة، ولا هو بالقرب إلى شيء من ذلك الوجه؛ إذ ذلك جهة الحدود والتقدير بالأمكنة، وقد كان ولا مكان، فهو على ما كان يتعالى عن الزمان والمكان؛ إذ اليهما ترجع حدود الأشياء ونهايتها، ولا قوة إلا بالله
With Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: That belief (is based) on prior knowledge about the state of the one to whom it is attributed, before attribution in the witnessed world, due to possibility. Then Allah, exalted, existed and there was no place, and upon that is the belief of the people; it is not permissible that understanding change concerning attribution from what it was before. Understanding turns to His attribution to creation. The specification of attributions of things to Allah in the witnessed world is a way of magnifying them due to what is placed in them of pleasing matters and limited states. So what is the Throne among that? There is no power except with Allah. Upon that is invalidated the saying of one who describes Him with every place: for there is no difference between one place attributed to Him and the totality; rather the individual is more appropriate for manifesting His magnificence, for in that is specification of that thing by mention, and in mention is honor and veneration. So it refers to mention upon that thing, and in the general sending and gathering of all, to its specification and its reality as an attribute of Allah, as it is said: 'Lord of everything' and 'God of everything', for magnifying the Lord and exalting Him. And when it is said: 'Lord of Hamid' and 'God of Ibrahim', the intention is to honor and magnify them. So by analogy, the attribution to the Throne necessitates magnifying and honoring the Throne, and (attribution) to every place would be describing Allah by them, and that is ugly, for He was not described by that in pre-eternity. And nothing is described as nearness to Allah by way of distance or dimension, nor is He near to anything in that respect, for that is the direction of limits and measurement by places, and He existed when there was no place, so He is as He was, exalted above time and place, for to them return the limits and ends of things. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 705

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's argument for Allah's transcendence of place. He begins by grounding the discussion in the principle that belief must be based on prior knowledge of the one to whom something is attributed, considering possibilities before the attribution in the observable world ('shahid'). Then he states the foundational point: Allah existed when there was no place, and this is the belief of people. Therefore, understanding of the attribution (of things to Allah) cannot change from what it was before – i.e., the attribution is not to be understood as implying Allah's location. The specification of things attributed to Allah (like 'Lord of everything') is a means of magnifying those things due to the positive qualities placed in them. This leads to the question: why would the Throne be singled out for special mention? Maturidi argues if specifying an attribute to something implies its veneration, then applying the attribute to all places would be improper because it would describe Allah with them in a way not known from pre-eternity. He refutes the view that Allah is in every place by stating there is no difference between one place and the totality in this respect. Rather, singling out a specific thing for mention (like 'Lord of the Throne') is more appropriate for manifesting His greatness. He then extends the analogy: just as saying 'Lord of everything' or 'God of Ibrahim' is for honor, so attributing the Throne to Allah honors the Throne, but attributing Him to every place would be describing Him by them, which is invalid because He was not described as such in pre-eternity. Finally, he denies any notion of nearness to Allah via distance or dimension, as these involve limits and places. Allah existed without place, so He remains exalted above time and space.

مكان
إضافة
تعظيم
تخصيص
قرب
مسافة
مساحة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah is physically present in every place or that the Throne is a literal location for Allah.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that attributions like 'Lord of the Throne' are for venerating the Throne, not for assigning Allah a place. Allah was without place in pre-eternity and remains so.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it invalid to say that Allah is in every place?
A) Because Allah is confined to a specific place like the Throne.
B) Because Allah was without place in pre-eternity, and describing Him with every place would be attributing to Him something He was not described with in pre-eternity.
C) Because every place is equally honorable.
D) Because the Throne is the only place fit for Allah.

Key Takeaway
Allah was without place in pre-eternity and remains so; all spatial language in revelation is metaphorical for veneration, not literal location.

Answering 'Where is Allah?': Maturidi's Affirmation of Transcendence
Maturidi answers the question 'Where is Allah?' by affirming His transcendence from all spatial relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question 'Where is Allah?' is invalid and how Maturidi denies all spatial relations for Allah using the four categories of connection, disconnection, indwelling, and separation.

Core Idea
Allah exists without place — He was before all things.

Many people ask 'Where is Allah?' as though He were located in space. Maturidi explains why this question is fundamentally mistaken. In this passage, he clarifies that Allah is not subject to any spatial relationship — not connected, not disconnected, not indwelling, not separate in a spatial sense. This lesson will help you grasp the absolute transcendence of Allah over all spatial categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi begins by stating that the question implies a place, which he has already shown is impossible for Allah.
The phrase 'we have explained' refers to his earlier arguments in the text.
2
Identify the four denied categories
Find the four terms: connection (ittisal), disconnection (infisal), indwelling (hulul), and separation (khuruj). Understand each one and why it implies a spatial relationship.
These are the main ways we think about objects in space.
3
Understand the reason
Maturidi gives the reason: 'Allah existed and there was nothing else.' That means before creation, there was no space or objects. So spatial descriptions cannot apply to Allah.
This is a key proof for transcendence.
4
Note the nuance about 'separation'
Maturidi says interpreting separation in a way that is like attributes of creatures is permissible. This means metaphorical language about Allah 'leaving' or 'coming' might be acceptable if understood correctly, but not literal.
This shows careful linguistic precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول القائل: أين هو؟ سؤال عن مكان، وقد بينا أنه يتعالى عن ذلك، ولا يوصف الله سبحانه بالاتصال بالأشياء ولا الانفصال، ولا بالحلول فيها ولا بالخروج منها من جهة المسافة على ما هو؛ لأن الله تعالى كان ولا غيره، بيانه، وعلى التفسير بالخروج من صفات الخلق وشبهه يجوز، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the saying of the questioner: 'Where is He?' is a question about place, and we have explained that He is exalted above that. And Allah, glorified, is not described by connection (ittisal) with things nor disconnection (infisal), nor by indwelling (hulul) in them, nor by separation (khuruj) from them in terms of distance as it is; because Allah, exalted, existed and there was nothing else. Its explanation: the interpretation of 'separation' from the attributes of creation and the like is permissible. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 706

Commentary
This passage deals with the question 'Where is Allah?' which implies a place. Maturidi states that he has already explained Allah's transcendence of place. He then lists four categories that do not apply to Allah: connection (ittisal) with things, disconnection (infisal) from them, indwelling (hulul) in them, and separation (khuruj) from them in terms of distance. The reason is that Allah existed when there was nothing else. This affirms that all spatial relations are impossible for Allah. He then adds a nuanced point: interpreting 'separation' in a way that is like the attributes of creation (i.e., leaving a place) is permissible? This may refer to metaphorical language (e.g., 'Allah came out of Egypt' is not literal) but the explanation is not fully clear. The key is that Allah cannot be described with any of these spatial categories in a literal sense, because He is beyond space and time, having existed alone before creation.

اتصال
انفصال
حلول
خروج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying 'indwelling' (hulul) means Allah is spatially separate from creation.
Correction: Maturidi also denies disconnection (infisal), so Allah is not in a spatial relationship at all — He is beyond space.

Checkpoint

B) Because He existed when there was nothing else, so He is beyond all spatial relations.

D) Because He is separate from all things in a spatial sense.

Key Takeaway
Allah is beyond all spatial relationships; He existed before creation, so no place or distance applies to Him.

Allah's Transcendence of Place
Maturidi negates spatial attributes from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi negates spatial attributes from Allah based on the question 'where is He?'.

Core Idea
Allah is beyond all spatial relations – no 'where' applies to Him.

The simple question 'Where is Allah?' might seem innocent, but it carries a hidden assumption: that Allah occupies a place. Imam Maturidi unpacks this question to show why it cannot apply to the Creator. In this passage, he explains that Allah is not described by connection, separation, indwelling, or exiting in any spatial sense, because He existed before all creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key spatial terms: اتصال (connection), انفصال (separation), حلول (indwelling), خروج (exiting).

2

Maturidi begins by stating that the question 'where is He?' is a question about place. He then says Allah is exalted above being described with such spatial relations.

He lists four specific spatial attributes that cannot apply to Allah: connection with things, separation from things, indwelling in them, and exiting from them in terms of spatial distance.
These cover all possible spatial relationships.
4

The reason is that Allah existed before anything else; such spatial attributes are contingent and belong to created beings, not the Creator.

He then explains that Allah is exalted above being described with spatial relations. The reason is that Allah existed before anything else, and these attributes are contingent. The Three Aspects of Oneness
Maturidi explains the universal acknowledgment of the One and its three aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Imam Maturidi, the term 'al-wāḥid' (the One) has three distinct aspects: transcendence, essence, and attributes, and how this refines the concept of tawhid.

Core Idea
The One is incomparable in His transcendence, essence, and attributes—a threefold oneness.

Imam Maturidi begins by observing that despite differences, all religions affirm a single eternal Being. But what does 'the One' truly mean? In this passage, he unpacks three aspects of oneness that guard against common misunderstandings and elevate tawhid beyond vague monotheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words present the concept first.
Pay attention to the threefold structure at the end.
2
Notice universal affirmation and deviations
Maturidi notes that all religions affirm pre-eternity for the One, but some deviated to embodiment or sonship. This shows the core belief in one God is widespread, but details differ.
This sets up why we need a clear definition of oneness.
3
Identify the three aspects of the One
Look for the three explicit divisions: One in transcendence (fī 'uluww), One in essence (al-dhāt), and One in attributes (al-ṣifāt). Understand each definition.
4
Reflect on the need for each aspect
Consider why Maturidi distinguishes these three: each aspect rebuts a specific misunderstanding about God. Essence guards against likeness, attributes guard against sharing His perfections.
This is the kalam method: using reason to clarify revelation.
5
Conclude with the implication for tawhid
The threefold oneness is the true meaning of 'the One'. It is a comprehensive doctrine that affirms absolute uniqueness.
The final phrase 'there is no power but in Allah' expresses reliance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأهل الأديان يثبتون القدم للواحد حتى قال قوم بتجسمه من بعد، وقوم إن له ابنا. إذ فهم على اختلافهم أجمعوا على الواحد، ونحو ذلك أنه ليس بذى شبيه؛ ما محال ذلك؛ إذ لم يكن غيره فهو على ذلك؛ إذ الوجه الذى فيه شبه وجود في غيره من الحدث، وذلك بعيد، وهذا معنى الواحد؛ إنه إذ هو واحد في علوه، وواحد الذات [محال] من أن يكون له فى ذاته مثال؛ إذ ذلك يسقط وجلاله التوحيد، وقد بيناه، وواحد الصفات يتعالى عن أن يشركه أحد في حقائق ما وصف به العلم والقدرة والتكوين، بل كل وصف من ذلك لغيره به بعد وشحال مماثلة الحديث القديم، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the people of religions affirm pre-eternity for the One, until some said He is embodied afterwards, and some said He has a son. For they, despite their differences, agreed upon the One, and that He is not possessed of a likeness; that is not impossible; for since there is nothing other than Him, He is upon that; for the aspect in which likeness exists exists in other than Him among contingent things, and that is far removed. And this is the meaning of the One: that He is One in His transcendence, and One in essence, [impossible] that there be in His essence a likeness; for that would nullify tawhid and His majesty, and we have explained it, and One in attributes, exalted above that anyone should share with Him in the realities of what He described of knowledge, power, and creation; rather, every attribute of that from other than Him has distance and difficulty of likeness to the pre-eternal and the contingent, and there is no power but in Allah.
Source pages p. 718

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of al-wāḥid (the One) as affirmed by all religions, though some deviated. Maturidi begins by noting that people of religions affirm pre-eternity for the One, even if some later claimed He has a body or a son. Despite their differences, they agree on the oneness and that He has no likeness. The author then explains the meaning of al-wāḥid: first, He is One in His transcendence (wāḥid fī 'uluww); second, One in essence (wāḥid al-dhāt), meaning it is impossible for there to be a likeness in His essence; and third, One in attributes (wāḥid al-ṣifāt), meaning that no one shares in the realities of His knowledge, power, and creation. Any attribute of other than Him is distant and difficult to compare with the pre-eternal and contingent. The passage concludes with a statement of reliance on Allah.

Key Terms
الواحد
القدم
التجسم
التوحيد
الذات
الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All religions mean the same thing by 'one God' in every respect, without any deviation.
Correction: While all religions affirm a single eternal Being, Maturidi notes that some deviated to embodiment or sonship, and the true meaning of 'the One' must be understood in three specific aspects to avoid such errors.

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The oneness of Allah encompasses three dimensions: transcendence, essence, and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

How will you remind yourself of this unique reality during your next act of worship?

The passage says any likeness to contingent things is 'far removed.' Have you ever formed a mental image of Allah, even unwittingly, while reading the Quran or making du'a? What concrete method will you use to train your mind to affirm His absolute incomparability?

Refutation of Corporealism and Materialism
Maturidi refutes two groups: those who say God is a body and those who believe in self-acting eternal natures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects both corporealism and the idea of self-acting natural natures, and how these refutations uphold God's transcendence and unity.

Core Idea
God cannot be a body or subject to nature's forces because that would make Him temporal and dependent.

Imam Maturidi addresses two erroneous views about God: that He is a body (or became one) and that He is composed of elemental natures that act by themselves. In this brief but powerful passage, he exposes the logical contradictions in both positions, reaffirming that the Eternal must be free from division, change, and need for any external cause. Let's explore his concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze deeply. Then read the translation to grasp the basic meaning. Notice Maturidi's condensed style.
The text has some OCR issues; focus on the core ideas.
2
Identify the two groups refuted
Find where Maturidi mentions the corporealists (those who say God was not a body then became a body) and the proponents of natures (aṣḥāb al-ṭabā'i'). These are the targets of his refutation.
3
Analyze the first refutation
Maturidi argues that a body is divisible and changeable ('an image that divides and splits'). If God were a body, He would be subject to time and change, contradicting His eternity. This is a rational proof from the nature of bodies.
4
Analyze the second refutation
Maturidi argues that natures cannot act by themselves; they require an external combiner and separator. If they were eternal and self-sufficient, they would not need God. But they do need something to bring them together, so they are contingent, not eternal.
5
Connect to divine oneness and transcendence
Both refutations reinforce the same point: God is not composed of parts or subject to external forces. He is one, indivisible, and the sole agent. Understanding this deepens your appreciation of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قنوم الجزء والحد ، ويقولون : كان غير مجسم ثم تجسم، ومعلوم أن هو صورة تتجزأ وتتبعض. بلجسم وأصحاب الطبائع لم يوجبوا الطبائع لأنفسها تعمل حتى يكون من يجمع بينها ويفرق، وذلك أزلى عندهم.
People of division and limit, and they say: He was not a body then became a body, but it is known that He is an image that divides and splits. As for the body, and the proponents of the natures did not make the natures work by themselves until there is one who combines and separates them, and that is eternal according to them.
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two groups: those who attribute corporeality (jismiyya) to God, and those who believe in eternal natures (tabā'i'). Maturidi first addresses the corporealists, who claim that God was not a body but later became one. He points out the contradiction: a body is necessarily divisible and changeable, so it cannot be eternal. Then he turns to the materialists (aṣḥāb al-ṭabā'i'), who hold that natures act by themselves without an external agent. Maturidi argues that natures cannot independently combine or separate; there must be a being that brings them together and disperses them. Since they claim the natures are eternal, they inadvertently undermine the necessity of a Creator.

Key Terms
تجسيم
جسم
طبائع
جزء
حد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi's argument is merely dogmatic, without rational basis.
Correction: Maturidi provides a clear rational argument: a body is necessarily divisible and changeable, and natures require an external agent. Both points are logical proofs, not just scriptural assertions.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it impossible for God to be a body?
A) Because the Qur'an explicitly denies it.
B) Because a body is subject to division and change, which are incompatible with eternity.
C) Because bodies are inherently impure.
D) Because no one has ever claimed God is a body.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that attributing corporeality or autonomous natures to God leads to logical absurdities, affirming that the Eternal must be indivisible and self-sufficient.

?

How does Maturidi's argument about natures reveal the error in that thinking, and how can you remind yourself daily that Allah is the only true agent?
?
This lesson shows that even subtle deviations in belief about Allah's nature can lead to serious error. After studying this, what specific belief about Allah do you realize you may have held incorrectly, and what will you do to correct it permanently in your heart and mind?

Maturidi's Correction on the Question of God's Location
Maturidi corrects an erroneous answer to 'Where is God?'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects spatial analogies for God and how he grounds the answer in divine pre-existence and transcendence.

Core Idea
God is beyond place; He was when there was no place.

This passage presents Maturidi's sharp correction of an erroneous answer to the question 'Where is God?' Someone had claimed God is 'in things' managing them, using a human analogy. Maturidi shows why that answer fails — it still implies spatiality and confinement. He then gives the proper response: God existed before place and is utterly transcendent. This lesson will help you grasp the rigorous tanzih (transcendence) at the heart of Maturidi's theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source once, then the literal translation. Notice the flow from the erroneous answer to Maturidi's critique and correction.
The phrase 'لا على المحاول' is likely 'not in contact' or 'not in direct contact'.
2
Identify the erroneous answer
Find the claim that God is 'in things managing them' and the analogy used. What is the problem Maturidi identifies?
The error is that it still deals with place and uses a human analogy implying confinement.
3
Understand Maturidi's correct answer
Maturidi says the correct answer is to state that God existed when there was no place, and He is beyond description by places. He remains as He was without change.
This grounds the answer in God's eternity and transcendence.
4
Reject the analogy of being 'in work'
Maturidi explains that the phrase 'so-and-so is in his work' implies occupation and confinement, which are inapplicable to God.
This shows the danger of using conventional language without purification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب عن قوله: أين هو؟ إنه في الأشياء مدبر لها لا على المحاول، كيا يقال فلان في عمله وقال: لا على إحاطة الأشياء به. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وقد أخطأ في الجواب، بل حقه أن يقال: تسأل المكان، وقد كان ولا مكان وهو يتعالى عن الوصف بالأمكنة، بل هو على ما كان بلا تغير ولا زوال. والقبول بالكون في العمل إخبار في المتعارف عن العمل الشاغل له الحابس فيه عن غيره، والله يتعالى عن هذا الوصف.
Then he answered regarding His saying: 'Where is He?' He said: He is in things, managing them, not in contact, as it is said: so-and-so is in his work, and he said: not in the sense of things encompassing Him. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: He made a mistake in the answer. Rather, the correct answer is that he asked about place, and He was when there was no place, and He is exalted above description by places. Rather, He is as He was without change or passing away. And the acceptance of being in work is an expression in conventional speech for the work that occupies one and confines him from others, and God is exalted above this description.
Source pages p. 725

Commentary
This passage deals with the question 'Where is God?' Someone answered that God is in things managing them but not in contact, using the analogy of a person being 'in his work.' Maturidi sharply criticizes this answer. He says the questioner is asking about place, but God existed when there was no place, so He is beyond spatial description. The correct answer is that God is as He was before creation, without change or passing away. He also rejects the analogy of being 'in one's work' because that implies occupation and confinement, which are inapplicable to God. This shows Maturidi's strict adherence to divine transcendence (tanzih) and rejection of any spatial or anthropomorphic language.

Key Terms
أين
المكان
المحاول
إحاطة
يتعالى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can be described as being 'in' things or occupying a place.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such language implies confinement and change, whereas God is eternal and beyond place.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the answer that God is 'in things managing them'?
A) Because it implies God is in contact with things.
B) Because it uses spatial language not found in revelation.
C) Because it implies God is confined or affected by things, and because it uses a conventional analogy inapplicable to God.
D) Because it denies God's omnipresence.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that the proper response to 'Where is God?' is that He is beyond place, as He was before creation without change.

?

Refuting the Charge of Anthropomorphism in Negation
Maturidi answers the accusation that denying God's likeness to creation implies likeness in another way.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying God's likeness to creation does not imply a likeness, and how Maturidi uses logical reasoning to refute this accusation.

Core Idea
Negation of similarity does not create similarity.

A common sophism against tanzih is that by denying God's likeness to creation, one is actually engaging in anthropomorphism by setting up a category of 'unlikeness'. Imam Maturidi addresses this objection head-on, offering a clear logical distinction. This passage shows how classical kalam defends divine transcendence through precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi directly answers the one who claims that negation of likeness is itself a likeness.
The phrase 'ذلك فهى' may be read as 'ذلك فَهْوَ' meaning 'that is it'.
2
Understand the example of black and white
Maturidi uses the example of black and white to illustrate his point: saying one does not resemble the other does not make them similar. Reflect on why this analogy is effective.
3
Grasp the summary argument
The passage concludes that negation removes restrictions from imagination and intellect, so they cannot posit similarity. Similarity requires a substance, attribute, or limit—none of which apply to God.
4
Apply to the place objection
Maturidi extends the same logic to those who claim denying God a place implies a limit. The negation does not create a positive limit.
This shows the method can be generalized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب من سأله: إنكم إذا نفسيتم عن الله شبه خلقه وعن خلقه شبهه فقد شبهتم، فقال: ذلك فهى، وليس في النفي تشبيه ألا ترى أن السواد والبياض من أنه لا يشبه أحدهما الآخر، إنه لا يوجب التشابه، ولو كان بذلك تشابه لكان بقوله هذا يشبه ذا ايجاب وفي ذلك قلب الحقائق وإبطال المجاز كله، وجملته أن النفي يرفع النهي عن الوهم والعقل، وإذا ارتفع ذلك لم يقدراه، والتشابه هو الواقع تحت قدر من جوهر أو صفة أو حد؛ فلذلك بطل معناه. و بمثله يجاب لمن يزعم أنكم إذا لم تصفوا الله بمكان فقد حددتم، وأن الحد ليس كذلك.
Then he answered the one who asked: 'When you deny from God likeness to His creation and from His creation likeness to Him, you have made a likeness.' He said: That is it. There is no likeness in negation. Do you not see that black and white, in that one does not resemble the other, does not entail similarity? If that entailed similarity, then his saying 'this resembles that' would be an affirmation, and that would be an inversion of realities and an invalidation of all metaphor. The summary is that negation removes the prohibition from the imagination and the intellect, and when that is removed, they cannot imagine [similarity]. Similarity is what falls under the capacity of substance, attribute, or limit; therefore its meaning is invalid. And with the like of it one answers those who claim that when you do not describe God with a place you have limited Him, and that limit is not like that.
Source pages p. 725

Commentary
This passage refutes a common sophism: 'By denying likeness, you are actually making a likeness (by setting up a category of 'unlikeness').' Maturidi responds that negation itself does not create similarity. He gives the example of black and white: saying one does not resemble the other does not make them similar. If that were the case, saying 'this resembles that' would create similarity, which would invert reality and abolish metaphor. The essence of the argument is that negation lifts restrictions from the intellect and imagination, so they cannot posit similarity. Similarity requires a common substance, attribute, or limit; since God has none, the concept of similarity is invalid. Finally, the same logic applies to those who claim that denying God a place imposes a limit: the negation does not create a positive limit.

Key Terms
النفي
التشبيه
السواد والبياض
الوهم
العقل
الحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people claim that by denying God's likeness to creation (tanzih), one is actually engaging in anthropomorphism because they have set up a category of 'unlikeness' that compares God to creation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that negation does not create a positive similarity. The statement 'God is not like creation' does not imply any likeness; it simply denies a relation. The example of black and white illustrates this clearly.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi refute the claim that denying likeness creates a likeness?
A) By saying negation is itself a form of affirmation.
B) By using the example of black and white: saying they are not similar does not make them similar.
C) By citing a verse.
D) By admitting the objection is valid.

Key Takeaway
Negation of similarity between God and creation does not imply any form of anthropomorphism; rather, it affirms God's absolute transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah as 'unlike anything,' do you truly believe there is no comparison, or do you subconsciously still imagine a form in your mind? What specific habit will you adopt to negate even the thought of likeness?
?
Have you ever thought that by denying Allah certain attributes (like location) you have somehow limited Him? How does Maturidi's argument about negation removing prohibition, not affirming a limit, change your understanding of tanzih?
?
Maturidi says negation removes the prohibition from the intellect. What prohibition in your own mind about Allah's nature needs to be removed so that you can truly accept His absolute transcendence without reservation?

Rejecting the Flawed Answer to 'Where is God?'
Maturidi corrects a flawed answer to 'Where is God?' and gives the proper response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question 'Where is God?' is invalid and how Maturidi refutes a flawed answer while affirming Allah's transcendence beyond place.

Core Idea
Allah's existence precedes place; asking 'where' misplaces divinity.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid addresses a common but misguided question: 'Where is God?' Maturidi first presents a flawed answer that attempts to locate God within creation, then explicitly corrects it. He teaches us that the very question is invalid because Allah existed without any place and remains unchanged. The lesson is a powerful reminder of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and English to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: flawed answer, criticism, correct answer, explanation.
2
Identify the flawed answer
The initial answer claims God is 'in things managing them' like a person 'in his work'. What does this imply?
This answer tries to locate God relationally within creation.
3
Understand Maturidi's criticism
Maturidi says this answer is mistaken. Why? Because it implies contact (المحاول) and occupation (العمل الشاغل), which are not applicable to Allah.
4
Grasp the correct response
The correct answer is to reject the question's premise. 'You ask about place, and He was when there was no place.' Allah is exalted above places and remains as He was without change.
This affirms God's transcendence and timelessness.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why describing God as 'in things' or 'in work' is inadequate. How does this passage guard against anthropomorphism?
Maturidi emphasizes that God is not subject to spatial or temporal limitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب عن قوله : أين هو ؟ إنه فى الأشياء مدبر لها لا على المحاول، کیا يقال فلان في عمله وقال : لا على إحاطة الأشياء به - عن قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وقد أخطأ فى الجواب ، بل حقه أن يقال : تسأل المكان ، وقد كان ولا مكان وهو يتعالى عن الوصف بالأمكنة ، بل هو على ما كان بلا تغير ولا زوال . والقبول بالكون فى العمل إخبار في المتعارف عن العمل الشاغل له الحابس فيه عن غيره، والله يتعالى عن هذا الوصف .
Then he answered his saying: 'Where is He?' [With] 'He is in things, managing them, not upon contact,' as it is said 'So-and-so is in his work.' And he said: 'Not by encompassing of things by Him.' - Then the Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'He has erred in the answer. Rather, the correct response is to say: You ask about place, and He was when there was no place, and He is exalted above description by places. Rather, He is as He was without change or cessation. And the acceptance of being in work is a report in common parlance about work that occupies and detains one from others, and Allah is exalted above this description.'
Source pages p. 725

Commentary
This passage addresses the question 'Where is God?' The initial answer given by someone (likely Muhammad ibn Shabib) claimed that God is in things as a manager without contact, like a person 'in his work'. Maturidi criticizes this answer as mistaken. The correct answer is that the question itself is invalid because it presupposes place, whereas Allah existed before any place and is not described by places. He remains as He was without change. The metaphor of 'being in work' implies occupation, which is inapplicable to Allah. Thus, the proper response is to reject the premise of the question.

مكان
تعالى عن الأمكنة
لا تغير ولا زوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God can be located in a physical sense, like being 'in things' without contact.
Correction: Maturidi teaches that the question is invalid because Allah existed without any place; He is not described by places.

Checkpoint

A) God is in the sky
B) God is in everything
C) The question is invalid because Allah was before place
D) God is everywhere

Key Takeaway
Allah is not in a place; the question 'Where is God?' is invalid because He existed before any place and remains unchanged.

Negating Likeness Does Not Imply Anthropomorphism
Maturidi refutes two objections about negation and anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that denying similarity between God and creation amounts to anthropomorphism, and his argument that negation does not create likeness.

Core Idea
Negation removes the possibility of imagining likeness; it does not create similarity.

Imam Maturidi addresses a common objection: if we deny that God is like creation, haven't we compared them by the very act of denial? In this passage, he clarifies that negation of similarity is not a positive claim of likeness. Instead, it frees the intellect from false comparisons. Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two distinct objections and responses.
2
Identify the first objection and response
Find where Maturidi quotes the opponent who says negating similarity creates similarity. Then see his answer using the example of black and white.
The key phrase is 'إنكم إذا نفسيتم عن الله شبه خلقه...'
3
Understand the second objection about place
The passage extends the argument to place: denying place does not define God; rather, affirming place does.
Look for 'بمثله يجاب لمن يزعم أنكم إذا لم تصفوا الله بمكان فقد حددتم'.
4
Reflect on the core principle
Maturidi states that negation lifts prohibition from imagination and intellect, so they cannot conceive likeness. Consider why this matters for divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب من سأله : إنكم إذا نفسيتم عن الله شبه خلقه وعن خلقه شبهه فقد شبهتم ، فقال : ذلك فهى ، وليس في النفى تشبيه ألا ١:٠ في السواد والبياض من أنه لا يشبه أحدهما الآخر ، إنه لا ماذا يكون ذلك في الإثبات . ما ذكره حسن الخلاف أن تری من قال يوجب التشابه ، ولو كان بذلك تشابه لكان بقوله هذا يشبه ذا ايجاب وفي ذلك قلب الحقائق وإبطال المجاز كله ، وجملته أن النفي يرفع النهي عن الوهم والعقل، وإذا ارتفع ذلك لم يقدراه ، والتشابه هو الواقع تحت قدر من جوهر أو صفة أو حد ؛ فلذلك بطل معناه و بمثله يجاب لمن يزعم أنكم إذا لم تصفوا الله بمكان فقد حددتم ، وأن الحد هو نفاية المكان ، ومحال نفى تحديد فى الوصف به ، وكذلك الأمكنة ، بل القائل بكل مكان ، أو بمكان دون مكان هو الذي حده ؛ إذ أثبته على ما أثبت المكان المضاف إليه مما يُقدره العقل والوهم ، وعند ذلك التحديد والتشبيه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he answered the one who asked him: 'If you negate from God the likeness of His creation and from His creation the likeness of Him, then you have made them similar.' He said: 'That is... [؟] and there is no comparison in negation, except as in black and white, that neither resembles the other. Indeed, what would that be in affirmation? What he mentioned is good. The divergence that you see from those who say it necessitates similarity: if there were similarity by that, then by his saying 'this resembles that' there would be affirmation, and in that is reversal of realities and invalidation of all metaphor. The summary is that negation removes the prohibition regarding imagination and intellect, and when that is removed, they do not imagine it. Similarity is that which falls under the capacity of substance, attribute, or limit; therefore its meaning is nullified. And with a similar [response] he answers the one who claims that if you do not describe God with a place, then you have defined Him, and that definition is the negation of place. It is impossible to negate definition in description by it, and likewise places. Rather, the one who says 'in every place' or 'in a place rather than another' is the one who has defined Him, because he affirms Him upon what the place added to Him affirms, which the intellect and imagination estimate, and then definition and comparison occur. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 725

Commentary
This passage defends the practice of negating similarity between God and creation. An opponent argues that if you deny that God is like His creation and deny that creation is like God, you have effectively made them similar by the very act of comparison. Maturidi refutes this by saying negation does not create similarity; rather, it removes the potential for imagining likeness. He gives the example of black and white: saying one is not like the other does not make them similar. In fact, if someone claims similarity, that would be an affirmation, not a negation. The core point is that negation lifts the prohibition of imagination and intellect, so they cannot conceive of similarity, whereas similarity is only meaningful when applied to substances, attributes, or limits—none of which apply to God. He then extends the argument to the issue of place: denying place to God does not define Him; rather, the one who assigns a place to God is the one who defines and likens Him.

Key Terms
نفي
تشبيه
التجسيم
الحد
المكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating similarity between God and creation is itself a form of anthropomorphism because it involves comparison.
Correction: Maturidi argues that negation removes the possibility of imagining likeness; it does not create a positive comparison. The act of negating similarity does not imply that the two are similar; it only states they are not.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi argue that negating similarity does not imply anthropomorphism?
A) Negation itself creates similarity
B) Negation removes the possibility of imagining likeness
C) Affirmation of negation is the same as affirmation
D) The example of black and white shows they become similar by negation

Key Takeaway
Negation of similarity between God and creation does not imply comparison; it is a positive affirmation of transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to imagine Allah, do you ever catch yourself thinking of Him in finite terms or with a location? How does Maturidi's argument about place challenge that habit, and what concrete step can you take today to purify your conception of Allah?
?
Have you ever felt uneasy about negating attributes from Allah, fearing it might be disrespectful or make Him seem 'less'? How does Maturidi's example of black and white change your understanding of what negation truly does—and what will you remind yourself next time that discomfort arises?
?
If someone says, 'By saying Allah is not like anything, you are actually likening Him to nothingness,' how would you respond using Maturidi's own words from this passage? Write a one-sentence rebuttal that you could use in conversation, and commit to saying it aloud to yourself before the day ends.

Negation of Spatial Attributes for God
Maturidi negates spatial attributes and their opposites for God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi negates all spatial attributes and their opposites from God, and the importance of not affirming the reality of the utterance even in negation.

Core Idea
Allah transcends space and its opposites — even negation must be careful.

This lesson examines a critical passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid where he establishes the absolute transcendence of Allah beyond spatial categories. The author not only negates attributes like place, entering, and exiting, but also warns against inadvertently affirming their opposites. This careful approach safeguards tawhid from anthropomorphic misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the list of spatial terms and the rule that follows.
The passage is dense but concise.
2
Identify the two groups of negated attributes
The passage mentions two sets: spatial attributes (place, going out, entering, connection, separation, distance) and dynamic attributes (gathering, separating, motion, stillness). List them.
3
Understand the rule of negation
Maturidi says it is not permissible to describe God with these attributes, and even when negating them, one must not affirm the reality of the utterance (i.e., treat the negation as literal description).
This prevents falling into the opposite error.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why Maturidi emphasizes this caution. How does this protect tawhid? Connect to the idea that Allah is incomparable to creation.
This is a core principle of Maturidi creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذ لم يجز الوصف بالمكان وبالخروج أو الدخول أو الاتصال أو الانفصال أو البينونة أو نحو ذلك، على نفي أضداد تلك الأحوال من غير إثبات تحقيق الملفوظ، وكذلك شأن الاجتماع والافتراق والتحرك والسكون لم يجز الذي قالوا، وبالله التوفيق.
Then, since it is not permissible to describe [God] with place, or with going out or entering, or connection or separation, or distance or the like, upon negating the opposites of those states without affirming the reality of the utterance, likewise the matter of gathering and separating, motion and stillness is not permissible what they said. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 729

Commentary
This passage is a theological ruling on divine attributes. The author states that it is not permissible to describe God with spatial attributes such as place, entering, exiting, connection, separation, or distance. Furthermore, it is also impermissible to negate these attributes without affirming the intended meaning. The same applies to concepts like gathering, separating, motion, and stillness. The author concludes that what the opponents (likely anthropomorphists or those who affirm spatial attributes) claimed is not permissible. The passage emphasizes God's transcendence beyond spatial and physical categories.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that negating spatial attributes from God implies affirming their opposites (e.g., saying 'Allah is not inside' means 'He is outside').
Correction: Maturidi explicitly warns against this: even the negation must be done without affirming the reality of the utterance. Allah transcends both the attribute and its opposite.

Checkpoint

B) It is not permissible at all, and even negating them must be done without affirming the reality of the utterance.
C) It is permissible only for place.
D) It is permissible if one uses metaphorical language.

Key Takeaway
Allah is transcendent beyond all spatial and physical categories, and even the negation of such attributes must be done without affirming any literal meaning.

The Mu'tazilite Position on Divine Power
Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazilite reduction of God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite view that God's power does not extend to all actions and the contradiction between their claim and their doctrine.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites limit God's power, contradicting their own claim of absolute omnipotence.

In this passage, Maturidi first identifies the sectarian background of the question. He notes that the author follows the Mu'tazilites, who assert that God cannot create even a small action like that of a mosquito. This sets up a contradiction: they claim God is powerful over everything, yet they deny His power over many things.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Find the specific claim: that God cannot create an action of a mosquito or similarly small atoms. Notice how this limits divine power.
3
Reflect on the contradiction
The passage says they claim God is powerful over everything, but deny most things within possibility. Ask yourself: how can both be true?
This prepares you for the main argument in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصاحب الكتاب ينتحل نحلة الإعتزال، ومذهبهم أن الله لا يقدر على خلق فعل بعوض فما فوقه من الجواهر، وفعل ذلك كله واقع تحت القدرة أو في ذلك لغيره قدرة، فأبوا تحقيق ما ادعوا من أنه قادر على كل شيء في أكثر الأشياء التي هي في حد الإمكان في العقول لا معنى له، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم سأل نفسه : يقدر أن يخلق مثله، قال : ذا محال : لما فيه إيجاب مخاوف. والمخلوق محدث وهو قديم، فيبطل أن يكون مثله؛ لما به يسأل عن القدرة، وهو لا يخلو من مثل الأول في الإحالة. وأيضا قال : في ذلك إثبات مصنوع أن يكون جسما فيه آثار صنعه أو عرضا لا يقوم بنفسه، ونفسه تدل على الله. وهو ليس كواحد منها. قال : وأيضا أن كل محدث يحتمل الفناء، وهو يتعالى عن احتمال حدوث الفناء؛ لما يغير ما لا يجوز عليه الفناء وما لا يجوز في غيره وقت على قلب ذلك. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : ومن تأمل ما ذكر عرف حيد السائل في السؤال عن سنى القول فيها له احتمال التمكن في العقول : لأنه سأل : يقدر أن يخلق مثله
The author of the book adopts the Mu'tazilite persuasion, and their doctrine is that God is not able to create an action of a mosquito and above it of atoms, and all that action is under power or in that there is power for another, so they refused to realize what they claimed that He is powerful over everything in most things that are in the limit of possibility in intellects has no meaning, and there is no power except with God. Then he asked himself: Is He able to create a like of Himself? He said: That is impossible because in it is necessitating fears. And the created is temporal and He is eternal, so it is invalid that He be like Him; because of what he asks about power, and it is not free from like the first in impossibility. And also he said: In that is establishing a made thing that is a body having traces of His act or an accident not subsisting by itself, and itself indicates God. And He is not like one of them. He said: And also that every temporal is subject to annihilation, and He is exalted above the possibility of temporal annihilation; because that changes what annihilation is not permissible upon it and what is not permissible in other than it time upon reversing that. The Sheikh (may God have mercy on him) said: And whoever contemplates what was mentioned will recognize the deviation of the questioner in the question about the years of the statement in it has possibility of capability in intellects: because he asked: Is He able to create a like of Himself?
Source pages p. 730

Commentary
This passage addresses the Mu'tazilite position and a question about God creating a being like Himself. It begins with a brief comment that the author of the book follows the Mu'tazilites, who hold that God cannot create certain small actions, contradicting the claim that He is powerful over everything. Then the author poses a self-question: Can God create a being like Himself? The answer is 'impossible' because it would entail fear or contradiction. The created is temporal while God is eternal, so equality is impossible. Moreover, creating a like of God would involve producing a body or accident, but God is not like that. Also, any temporal being is subject to annihilation, while God is exalted above that. The final remark by 'the Sheikh' notes that contemplating this reveals the questioner's deviation.

قُدْرَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilites affirm God's absolute power over everything, but they actually limit it.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites deny God's power over small actions, contradicting their verbal claim of omnipotence.

Checkpoint

A) God can do everything without exception.
B) God cannot create even a small action like that of a mosquito.
C) God's power is limited only to big things.
D) God has no power at all.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites' claim of God's power over everything is contradicted by their denial of His power over small actions.

?

Why God Cannot Create a Being Like Himself
Maturidi refutes the possibility of God creating a being like Himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments proving that God cannot create a being equal to Himself.

Core Idea
A created being is temporal and subject to annihilation, while God is eternal and self-sufficient—so they cannot be alike.

After setting the sectarian context, Maturidi poses the question: Can God create a being like Himself? He immediately rejects it as impossible and provides three compelling arguments. This lesson examines those arguments, which are based on the fundamental distinction between the Creator and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and initial answer
Read the Arabic and translation for the self-question and Maturidi's immediate response: 'That is impossible.'
2
Examine the first argument
Identify the first reason: a created being is temporal (hadith) while God is eternal (qadim), so they cannot be alike.
3
Examine the second argument
Find the second reason: a created like would be a body or accident, but God is not like these.
4
Examine the third argument and conclusion
Notice the third reason: every temporal thing can be annihilated, but God is beyond that. Finally, read the Sheikh's remark about the questioner's deviation.
This sums up the lesson's key takeaway.

The Created Cannot Be Like the Eternal
Initial arguments against creating a like of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic proof that a created being cannot be like the eternal God.

Core Idea
A created thing is originated and transient, so it cannot equal the eternal and immutable God.

The author begins by directly tackling the question of whether God can create a being like Himself. He presents three initial arguments: the ontological gap between created (originated) and Creator (eternal), the impossibility of a like being a body or accident, and the fact that all originated things are subject to annihilation while God is not. These lay the foundation for why such a creation is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the three arguments
The passage presents three distinct proofs. Find each one: (1) created vs eternal, (2) body/accident, (3) susceptibility to annihilation.
These are the foundational points.
3
Reflect on the relationship
Consider why each argument independently shows that a created being cannot be like God. How do they reinforce each other?
Think about the concept of 'likeness' in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سأل نفسه : يقدر أن يخلق مثله ، قال : ذا محال : لما فيه ایجاب مخاوف . والمخلوق محدث وهو قديم ، فيبطل أن يكون مثله ؛ لما به يسأل عن القدرة ، وهو لا يخلو من مثل الأول في الإحالة . وأيضا قال : في ذلك اثبات مصنوع أن يكون جسماً فيه آثار صنعه أو عرضا لا يقوم بنفسه ، ونفسه يدل على الله . وهو ليش كواحد منها . قال : وأيضا أن كل محدث يحتمل الفناء ، وهو يتعالى عن احتمال حدوث الفناء ؛ لما يغير ما لا يجوز عليه الفناء وما لا يجوز في غيره وقت على قلب ذلك قال الشيخ رحمه الله : ومن تأمل ما ذكر عرف حيد السائل في السؤال عن سنى القول فيها له احتمال التمكن فى العقول : لأنه سأل : يقدر أن يخلق مثله ، فيكون مثله لا يكون جسما ولا عرضا ولا محدثا ولا محتملا للفناء ؛ لأنه إن كان على شيء من ذلك فلا يكون مثله ، وبه سأل ، فكيف يبقى ذلك الذي ذكر ، وما ذكر هو أبعاض ما فى كون ذلك نفيه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وقد قلنا على المعتزلة ما هو هو أوضح من ذلك ، مع ما يلزمهم من وجه آخر وهم أنهم يصفون الله بالقدرة على الكذب والسفه والظلم ، مما لو كان شيء من ذلك ليبطل ربوبيته ، ثم لم يجز فعله ذلك ، لذلك فليقل : يقدر على خلق مثله ولكن لا يفعل ؛ لأنه ليست الأعجوبة فى جعل الحادث قديما ، وما يحتمل الفناء غير فان ، وما يقع عليه أثر الصنع غير واقع ذلك إلا بالأعجوبة في جعل القديم حديثا والباقى فانيا والحكيم سفيها ، فان استقامت القدرة على هذا على إحالة فمثله الأول على مذهبهم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله ثم الإحالة على مذهبنا سهل وهو أن الله جل جلالة محال دخوله تحت القدرة ، فالقول بادخال غير تحت القدرة ليصير بها مثله دفع المثلية عنه ؛ لإحاله دخوله تحت القدرة والآخر بها يلحقه به ، ولا قوة إلا بالله الفعل. وإن شئت قلت : السؤال متناقض ؛ لأنه قال : يقدر أن يخلق مثله ، ومثله لا يكون مخلوقا ، فكأنه قال : يقدر على ما ليس له مثل من الخلق قبلى وأيضا أن فى الاحتمال غير ما هو عليه سقوط هويته ، فيكون ذلك الغير هو الحو الذى به يكون هوية الأشياء ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل أن الله سبحانه إنما ثبتت له الإلهية بما حقق تعاليه عن المثل والشيئية ، فمحال احتمال مثله لما سقوط ألوهيته . على أن الكلام متناقض من الوجه الذى يقول ؛ لأنه يخبر أن يجعل ، فصيره مجعولا ، ومحال كون مجعول جاعل على إزالة الجعل الذى به كان ؛ لما به زواله ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then he asked himself: Is He able to create a like of Himself? He said: That is impossible, because in it there is necessitating [something] fearful. The created is originated, and He is eternal, so it is invalid that there be a like of Him, because of what he asked about power. And it is not free from being similar to the first in impossibility. And also he said: In that there is an affirmation of a manufactured thing that is a body having traces of His work, or an accident that does not subsist by itself, while His Self indicates Allah, and He is not one of them. He said: And also that every originated thing is susceptible to annihilation, and He is exalted above susceptibility to the occurrence of annihilation, because it changes what is not permissible for it to be subject to annihilation, and what is not permissible in others is a moment upon the reversal of that. The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: And whoever contemplates what was mentioned knows the deviation of the questioner in the question about the years of the statement? There is for it a possibility of meaning in the intellects, because he asked: Is He able to create a like of Himself? So that like of Him would not be a body, nor an accident, nor originated, nor susceptible to annihilation; because if it were any of that, then it would not be a like of Him, and by that he asked. So how can that which he mentioned remain? And what he mentioned is parts of what is in the negation of that. And there is no power except with Allah. And we have said against the Mu'tazila what is clearer than that, along with what is incumbent upon them from another aspect; they ascribe to Allah power over lying, foolishness, and injustice. If any of that were [possible], it would invalidate His lordship. Then He does not permit to do that. Therefore, let him say: He is able to create a like of Himself but He does not do it, because the wonder is not in making the originated eternal, and what is susceptible to annihilation non-annihilated, and what is affected by the trace of making not affected, except by the wonder in making the eternal originated, and the remaining annihilated, and the wise foolish. If power upon this in impossibility stands, then the like of the first is on their school. And there is no power except with Allah. Then the impossibility in our school is easy; namely that Allah, majestic is His glory, is impossible to enter under power. So the statement of entering another under power so that it becomes a like of Him is a repulsion of likeness from Him, because of the impossibility of His entering under power, while the other by it joins to Him. And there is no power except with Allah. And if you wish, you may say: The question is contradictory, because he said: Is He able to create a like of Him? And His like cannot be created. So it is as if he said: Is He able over what has no like among creation before? And also, in the possibility of something other than what He is upon is a fall of His identity, so that other becomes the essence by which is the identity of things. And there is no power except with Allah. And the principle is that Allah, exalted, only has divinity established by His being exalted above having a like and being a thing. So the possibility of a like for Him is impossible due to the fall of His divinity. Moreover, the statement is contradictory from the aspect that he says; because he says 'that He makes', so He makes it made, and it is impossible for a made thing to be a maker that removes the making by which it was, because of its removal. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 730

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered refutation of the question 'Can God create a being like Himself?' It proceeds in several stages. First, an initial proof: a created being is originated, while God is eternal, so they cannot be alike. Any supposed like would be a body with traces of making or an accident—neither can equal God. Also, originated things are subject to annihilation, unlike God. The Shaykh's commentary clarifies the questioner's confusion: the 'like' would have to be uncreated, but then it wouldn't be created, so the question is self-defeating. Next, a comparison with Mu'tazilite views is made: they claim God can lie etc., but that would invalidate His lordship; similarly, they might say He can create a like but doesn't, but the real impossibility is in changing eternal to temporal. Then the author gives his own school's easy refutation: God cannot be under His own power, so bringing another under power to make it like Him is impossible. Two further contradictions are offered: the question is self-contradictory because a created like cannot exist, and the possibility of a like would destroy God's identity and divinity. Finally, the statement implies making the 'like' a made thing, which then cannot be a maker—a logical absurdity. Thus, the question is shown to be impossible from every angle.

Correction: The passage shows that a created being is inherently originated and temporal, while God is eternal and immutable. Likeness is impossible by definition.

The Questioner's Confusion and the Mu'tazilite Fallacy
Commentary on the questioner's confusion and refutation of Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question 'Can God create a like of Himself?' is conceptually confused, and how the Mu'tazilite position leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
The 'like' would have to be uncreated, making the question self-defeating, and Mu'tazilite theology collapses into impossibility.

The Shaykh's commentary unpacks the logical failure underlying the question. He shows that the hypothetical 'like' cannot be a body, accident, originated, or subject to annihilation—so it must be uncreated, contradicting the question. He then compares this to the Mu'tazilite claim that God could lie or act foolishly, which would invalidate His lordship, and argues by analogy that the same impossibility applies here.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary
Read the Shaykh's commentary carefully. Notice how he deconstructs the questioner's assumption.
2
Understand the self-defeating nature
The Shaykh argues that a true like of God cannot be created, so the question implies a contradiction.
Think: if it is like God, it cannot be created; if it is created, it cannot be like God.
3
Compare with the Mu'tazila
The author draws a comparison with the Mu'tazilite position on God's power over absurd acts. See how the same logic applies.
This shows the broader theological implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سأل نفسه : يقدر أن يخلق مثله ، قال : ذا محال : لما فيه ایجاب مخاوف . والمخلوق محدث وهو قديم ، فيبطل أن يكون مثله ؛ لما به يسأل عن القدرة ، وهو لا يخلو من مثل الأول في الإحالة . وأيضا قال : في ذلك اثبات مصنوع أن يكون جسماً فيه آثار صنعه أو عرضا لا يقوم بنفسه ، ونفسه يدل على الله . وهو ليش كواحد منها . قال : وأيضا أن كل محدث يحتمل الفناء ، وهو يتعالى عن احتمال حدوث الفناء ؛ لما يغير ما لا يجوز عليه الفناء وما لا يجوز في غيره وقت على قلب ذلك قال الشيخ رحمه الله : ومن تأمل ما ذكر عرف حيد السائل في السؤال عن سنى القول فيها له احتمال التمكن فى العقول : لأنه سأل : يقدر أن يخلق مثله ، فيكون مثله لا يكون جسما ولا عرضا ولا محدثا ولا محتملا للفناء ؛ لأنه إن كان على شيء من ذلك فلا يكون مثله ، وبه سأل ، فكيف يبقى ذلك الذي ذكر ، وما ذكر هو أبعاض ما فى كون ذلك نفيه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وقد قلنا على المعتزلة ما هو هو أوضح من ذلك ، مع ما يلزمهم من وجه آخر وهم أنهم يصفون الله بالقدرة على الكذب والسفه والظلم ، مما لو كان شيء من ذلك ليبطل ربوبيته ، ثم لم يجز فعله ذلك ، لذلك فليقل : يقدر على خلق مثله ولكن لا يفعل ؛ لأنه ليست الأعجوبة فى جعل الحادث قديما ، وما يحتمل الفناء غير فان ، وما يقع عليه أثر الصنع غير واقع ذلك إلا بالأعجوبة في جعل القديم حديثا والباقى فانيا والحكيم سفيها ، فان استقامت القدرة على هذا على إحالة فمثله الأول على مذهبهم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله ثم الإحالة على مذهبنا سهل وهو أن الله جل جلالة محال دخوله تحت القدرة ، فالقول بادخال غير تحت القدرة ليصير بها مثله دفع المثلية عنه ؛ لإحاله دخوله تحت القدرة والآخر بها يلحقه به ، ولا قوة إلا بالله الفعل. وإن شئت قلت : السؤال متناقض ؛ لأنه قال : يقدر أن يخلق مثله ، ومثله لا يكون مخلوقا ، فكأنه قال : يقدر على ما ليس له مثل من الخلق قبلى وأيضا أن فى الاحتمال غير ما هو عليه سقوط هويته ، فيكون ذلك الغير هو الحو الذى به يكون هوية الأشياء ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل أن الله سبحانه إنما ثبتت له الإلهية بما حقق تعاليه عن المثل والشيئية ، فمحال احتمال مثله لما سقوط ألوهيته . على أن الكلام متناقض من الوجه الذى يقول ؛ لأنه يخبر أن يجعل ، فصيره مجعولا ، ومحال كون مجعول جاعل على إزالة الجعل الذى به كان ؛ لما به زواله ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then he asked himself: Is He able to create a like of Himself? He said: That is impossible, because in it there is necessitating [something] fearful. The created is originated, and He is eternal, so it is invalid that there be a like of Him, because of what he asked about power. And it is not free from being similar to the first in impossibility. And also he said: In that there is an affirmation of a manufactured thing that is a body having traces of His work, or an accident that does not subsist by itself, while His Self indicates Allah, and He is not one of them. He said: And also that every originated thing is susceptible to annihilation, and He is exalted above susceptibility to the occurrence of annihilation, because it changes what is not permissible for it to be subject to annihilation, and what is not permissible in others is a moment upon the reversal of that. The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: And whoever contemplates what was mentioned knows the deviation of the questioner in the question about the years of the statement? There is for it a possibility of meaning in the intellects, because he asked: Is He able to create a like of Himself? So that like of Him would not be a body, nor an accident, nor originated, nor susceptible to annihilation; because if it were any of that, then it would not be a like of Him, and by that he asked. So how can that which he mentioned remain? And what he mentioned is parts of what is in the negation of that. And there is no power except with Allah. And we have said against the Mu'tazila what is clearer than that, along with what is incumbent upon them from another aspect; they ascribe to Allah power over lying, foolishness, and injustice. If any of that were [possible], it would invalidate His lordship. Then He does not permit to do that. Therefore, let him say: He is able to create a like of Himself but He does not do it, because the wonder is not in making the originated eternal, and what is susceptible to annihilation non-annihilated, and what is affected by the trace of making not affected, except by the wonder in making the eternal originated, and the remaining annihilated, and the wise foolish. If power upon this in impossibility stands, then the like of the first is on their school. And there is no power except with Allah. Then the impossibility in our school is easy; namely that Allah, majestic is His glory, is impossible to enter under power. So the statement of entering another under power so that it becomes a like of Him is a repulsion of likeness from Him, because of the impossibility of His entering under power, while the other by it joins to Him. And there is no power except with Allah. And if you wish, you may say: The question is contradictory, because he said: Is He able to create a like of Him? And His like cannot be created. So it is as if he said: Is He able over what has no like among creation before? And also, in the possibility of something other than what He is upon is a fall of His identity, so that other becomes the essence by which is the identity of things. And there is no power except with Allah. And the principle is that Allah, exalted, only has divinity established by His being exalted above having a like and being a thing. So the possibility of a like for Him is impossible due to the fall of His divinity. Moreover, the statement is contradictory from the aspect that he says; because he says 'that He makes', so He makes it made, and it is impossible for a made thing to be a maker that removes the making by which it was, because of its removal. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 730

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered refutation of the question 'Can God create a being like Himself?' It proceeds in several stages. First, an initial proof: a created being is originated, while God is eternal, so they cannot be alike. Any supposed like would be a body with traces of making or an accident—neither can equal God. Also, originated things are subject to annihilation, unlike God. The Shaykh's commentary clarifies the questioner's confusion: the 'like' would have to be uncreated, but then it wouldn't be created, so the question is self-defeating. Next, a comparison with Mu'tazilite views is made: they claim God can lie etc., but that would invalidate His lordship; similarly, they might say He can create a like but doesn't, but the real impossibility is in changing eternal to temporal. Then the author gives his own school's easy refutation: God cannot be under His own power, so bringing another under power to make it like Him is impossible. Two further contradictions are offered: the question is self-contradictory because a created like cannot exist, and the possibility of a like would destroy God's identity and divinity. Finally, the statement implies making the 'like' a made thing, which then cannot be a maker—a logical absurdity. Thus, the question is shown to be impossible from every angle.

Correction: The author argues that such powers would invalidate lordship, and by analogy, the ability to create a like is impossible.

The Author's School Refutation and Logical Contradictions
Final refutation and additional contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation from the author's school (that God cannot be under His own power) and the additional contradictions that demonstrate the impossibility of creating a like of God.

Core Idea
God cannot be under His own power, so no other can be made like Him, and the question leads to multiple logical absurdities.

After comparing with the Mu'tazila, the author presents his own school's straightforward refutation: God is impossible to enter under power, so the attempt to make another like Him fails. He then adds two further arguments: the question is contradictory because a like cannot be created, and the possibility of a like would destroy God's identity and divinity. Finally, he points out a logical absurdity in the very wording of the question.

Guided Path
1
Read the 'easy' refutation
The author states the refutation from his own school. Understand the key point: God cannot be under His own power.
This is a concise and powerful argument.
2
Understand the self-contradiction and identity arguments
The passage then presents two additional contradictions: the question implies a created uncreated, and any possibility of a like would undermine God's identity and divinity.
Think about the implications for tawhid.
3
Reflect on the maker-made absurdity
The final argument shows that the very wording of the question leads to a logical absurdity regarding maker and made.
This is a subtle point about causality.

Commentary
