Divine Transcendence
advanced
8 min
The Impossibility of a Like of God
Maturidi argues that God's like cannot be a body, accident, or originated, and refutes the Mu'tazilite claim about power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a like of God is impossible according to Maturidi, and how he refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God could create a like of Himself.

Core Idea
Nothing created can be truly like the Eternal Creator.

This lesson unpacks Maturidi's argument that any being like God would have to be uncreated, but all created things are originated and perishable. He then uses this to refute the Mu'tazilite claim that God could create a like of Himself if He willed. By the end, you'll see that the very concept of a created like is contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the English translation. Let the force of Maturidi's argument sink in before analyzing details.
This passage is dense and uses technical terms like hadith (originated) and qadim (eternal).
2
Identify the initial principle
Maturidi states what a like of God cannot be: a body, an accident, originated, or subject to annihilation. Make sure you see why each of these would contradict being truly like God.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite refutation
Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for attributing to God power over lying, foolishness, and injustice. He argues that such attributes would nullify God's lordship, so they must admit that God cannot do them. Then they should also admit that creating a like is impossible.
This part is tricky: Maturidi uses a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Grasp the final argument about power
Maturidi concludes that the real impossibility lies in God being subject to power (qudra). A created thing cannot be like God because God cannot be under power, while the created thing is brought into existence by power.
This seals the argument from divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن يكون مثله لا يكون جسما ولا عرضا ولا محدثا ولا محتملا للفناء ; لأنه إن كان على شيء من ذلك فلا يكون مثله ، وبه سأل ، فكيف يبقى ذلك الذي ذكر ، وما ذكر هو أبعاض ما فى كون ذلك نفيه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وقد قلنا على المعتزلة ما هو هو أوضح من ذلك ، مع ما يلزمهم من وجه آخر وهم أنهم يصفون الله بالقدرة على الكذب والسفه والظلم ، مما لو كان شيء من ذلك ليبطل ربوبيته ، ثم لم يجز فعله ذلك ، لذلك فليقل : يقدر على خلق مثله ولكن لا يفعل ; لأنه ليست الأعجوبة فى جعل الحادث قديما ، وما يحتمل الفناء غير فان ، وما يقع عليه أثر الصنع غير واقع ذلك إلا بالأعجوبة في جعل القديم حديثا والباقى فانيا والحكيم سفيها ، فان استقامت القدرة على هذا على إحالة فمثله الأول على مذهبهم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله ثم الإحالة على مذهبنا سهل وهو أن الله جل جلالة محال دخوله تحت القدرة ، فالقول بادخال غير تحت القدرة ليصير بها مثله دفع المثلية عنه ; لإحاله دخوله تحت القدرة والآخر بها يلحقه به ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And whoever is like Him is not a body, nor an accident, nor originated, nor subject to annihilation; because if he were something of that, he would not be like Him. And by it he asked, so how does that which was mentioned remain? And what was mentioned are parts of that in the negation of that. And there is no power except with Allah. And we have said against the Mu'tazila what is clearer than that, along with what is incumbent upon them from another aspect: that they describe Allah with power over lying, foolishness, and injustice, such that if any of that existed, it would nullify His lordship. Then it is not permissible for Him to do that. Therefore let him say: He is able to create a like of Him but does not do it; because the marvel is not in making the originated (hadith) old (qadim), and what is subject to annihilation is not imperishable, and what is affected by the work of craftsmanship does not occur except by a marvel in making the old new, the permanent perishable, and the wise foolish. If power over this (i.e., changing essences) is possible (in the sense of being conceivable), then the first like (the claim that God can create a like) on their doctrine (is impossible). Then the impossibility on our doctrine is easy: that Allah, majestic is His glory, is impossible to enter under power (qudra). So saying that something else enters under power to become thereby like Him is repelling likeness from Him, because of the impossibility of His entering under power, and the other (the created) by it (power) joins Him (i.e., becomes similar). And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 731

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that God can create a like of Himself. Maturidi begins by stating a logical principle: whatever is like God cannot be a body, an accident, originated, or subject to annihilation, because if it had any of these attributes it would not be like Him. He then criticizes the Mu'tazila for describing God as having power over lying, foolishness, and injustice, which would nullify His lordship. Since such acts are impossible for God, the Mu'tazila should instead say that God is able to create a like but does not do so. Maturidi then argues that the real marvel is not in changing a created thing into an eternal one, but in changing an eternal thing into a created one—which is impossible. If one grants that God could do such impossible things (like changing essences), then the claim that He can create a like would still be impossible on their own doctrine. However, on Maturidi's doctrine, the impossibility is straightforward: God cannot enter under power (qudra) at all. Saying that something else enters under power to become like God would actually repelling likeness from God, because God cannot be subject to power, and the created thing would merely become similar through that power.

Key Terms
مثل (mithl)
قدرة (qudra)
حادث (hadith)
قديم (qadim)
فناء (fana')
إحالة (ihala)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a 'like' allows similarity in some attributes while ignoring essential transcendence; e.g., a great power like God could be His like.
Correction: Maturidi shows that any created attribute—being a body, accident, originated, or subject to annihilation—precludes being truly like God. A like must share all essential attributes, but God's essence is unique and beyond creation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why can't God create a being exactly like Himself?
A) Because God's power is limited.
B) Because a like would have to be uncreated, but everything created is originated.
C) Because the Mu'tazila say He can but does not.
D) Because God is a body and cannot be duplicated.

Key Takeaway
The concept of a created 'like' of God is contradictory because a like must share God's uncreatedness, and God cannot be subject to power, while creation entails being subject to power.

You claim that nothing is like God. But do your actions reflect that belief? Consider an area of your life—perhaps your career, family, or possessions—where you treat something as if it is equal to God in importance. How does this lesson challenge you to realign your priorities? What step will you take today to dethrone that idol and place God alone as supreme?
?
Maturidi argues that God cannot be under power, even our imagined 'control' through prayer or rituals. Do you sometimes approach God as if He owes you a response because of your devotional acts? Confess a specific instance where you felt entitled to God's favor because of your efforts, and commit to approaching Him solely as the Sovereign who is beyond any human claim.
?
The Mu'tazilites limited God by assuming His power must conform to human logic. In what way do you unconsciously limit God—by expecting Him to act according to your sense of justice, mercy, or timing? How does this lesson free you to trust in God's absolute transcendence, even when His actions are beyond your understanding? What practical step can you take this week to surrender your expectations and submit to His unqualified lordship?

The Contradiction in Asking if God Can Create a Like
Maturidi exposes the internal contradiction in the question: 'Can God create a like of Himself?'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question 'Can God create a like of Himself?' is self-contradictory on both definitional and logical grounds.

Core Idea
The question 'Can God create a like of Himself?' is self-contradictory because a 'like' cannot be created.

Maturidi examines a commonly debated question among theologians and exposes its internal incoherence. He first shows how the very framing contradicts itself, then deepens the critique by showing that allowing such a possibility would undermine God's identity and divinity. This passage sharpens our understanding of divine transcendence and the logical boundaries of theological questioning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the flow of Maturidi's argument. Notice the dense logical style.
2
Identify the two contradictions
Maturidi points out a definitional contradiction and a logical contradiction. Find where he states each.
The first contradiction is in the phrase 'like cannot be created'. The second involves the fall of identity.
3
Understand the principle of divinity
Maturidi grounds his argument in the principle that God's divinity is established by His transcendence above likeness.
This principle is crucial for preventing anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the final point about making
Maturidi adds a third point about the circularity in the act of making. Consider why this is contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن شئت قلت : السؤال متناقض ; لأنه قال : يقدر أن يخلق مثله ، ومثله لا يكون مخلوقا ، فكأنه قال : يقدر على ما ليس له مثل من الخلق قبلى وأيضا أن فى الاحتمال غير ما هو عليه سقوط هويته ، فيكون ذلك الغير هو الحو الذى به يكون هوية الأشياء ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل أن الله سبحانه إنما ثبتت له الإلهية بما حقق تعاليه عن المثل والشيئية ، فمحال احتمال مثله لما سقوط ألوهيته . على أن الكلام متناقض من الوجه الذى يقول ; لأنه يخبر أن يجعل ، فصيره مجعولا ، ومحال كون مجعول جاعل على إزالة الجعل الذى به كان ; لما به زواله ولا قوة إلا بالله
And if you wish, say: The question is contradictory; because he said: He is able to create a like of Him, but His like cannot be created, so it is as if he said: He is able to what has no like among creation before me [؟]. And also that in the possibility (ihtimal) other than what it is, there is a fall of its identity (huwiyya), so that other is the 'hoo' [؟] by which the identity of things is. And there is no power except with Allah. The principle is that Allah, exalted, His divinity is only established by that which realizes His transcendence above the like (mithl) and thingness (shay'iyya). So impossible is the possibility of a like of Him, because of the fall of His divinity. Moreover, the speech is contradictory from the aspect that he says: he informs that He makes, so He made it made (maj'ul), and it is impossible for a made thing to be a maker in removing the making (ja'l) by which it was [made], because of that by which it [the making] ceases. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 731

Commentary
This passage is a logical analysis of the question: 'Can God create a like of Himself?' Maturidi first points out the contradiction: the questioner says 'He is able to create a like of Him,' but a 'like' of God cannot be created, because anything created is originated and not like God. So the question effectively asks if God can do something impossible. Maturidi then adds another angle: if we consider the possibility (ihtimal) of an entity other than God that is like Him, that would entail the collapse of God's distinct identity (huwiyya), because the other would be the essence by which things have identity. The foundational principle is that God's divinity (uluhiyya) is established precisely by His transcendence above having a like (mithl) or even being a 'thing' (shay') in the ordinary sense. Therefore, allowing a like would nullify His divinity. Finally, Maturidi notes another contradiction: the questioner says God 'makes' (ja'ala), making the like a made thing (maj'ul), but it is impossible for a made thing to be a maker in such a way as to remove the making by which it was made—that would require an absurd circularity.

Key Terms
مثل (mithl)
هوية (huwiyya)
إلهية (uluhiyya)
جعل (ja'l)
مجعول (maj'ul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The question 'Can God create a like of Himself?' is a meaningful theological inquiry that deserves consideration.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the question is inherently contradictory because a 'like' of God cannot be created, and allowing such a possibility would undermine God's unique divinity.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider the question 'Can God create a like of Himself?' contradictory?
A) Because God cannot do anything.
B) Because a 'like' is by definition uncreated, so asking if God can create it is nonsensical.
C) Because the Mu'tazila already answered it.
D) Because God's power is limited.

Key Takeaway
The question 'Can God create a like of Himself?' is self-contradictory because a like of God cannot be created by definition, and the possibility of a like would nullify God's unique divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What will you do to guard your thoughts from such contradictions?
?
The lesson asserts that God's divinity is established by His transcendence above any like. When you speak of God, do you ever unconsciously lower Him to the level of created concepts? This lesson demands that you recognize that even the category of 'thing' does not apply to Him in the same way. How will you train your mind to affirm His absolute otherness?
?
Maturidi warns that considering a like of God would 'fall of His identity'. Have you ever, in your imagination, pictured God as similar to anything? This lesson calls you to repent of any mental image that reduces God to something comparable. What concrete step will you take today to purify your understanding of God's uniqueness?

Why Questioning Divine Cause Is Invalid
Maturidi refutes the question about the purpose of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why questioning the cause of God's actions is considered invalid in Maturidi theology and what the proper response should be.

Core Idea
Asking 'why' about God's acts implies authority over Him—a logical impossibility.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a critical objection: if God did not need creation, why did He create? Some suggested He created for a reward. Maturidi refutes this by showing that questioning the cause (علة) of God's act is itself impossible, as it assumes a standard outside God. Instead, our duty is to know God, obey Him, and refrain from probing into divine causation. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is presented: someone claimed God created for everlasting reward. Notice how this tries to assign a purpose (wisdom) to God's action.
The objection assumes God must have a reason external to Himself.
2
Grasp the refutation
Focus on the author's argument that the question about the cause (علة) is impossible because it implies someone has authority over God. Consider what 'cause' means in this context.
Maturidi states that any search for a cause behind God's actions is logically invalid.
3
Recognize the proper response
Read the latter part where the author redirects attention to knowing God, His commands, and fulfilling obedience. Understand that this occupies the believer from seeking causes.
The questioner overstepped by focusing on God's reasons rather than their own responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سئل عن خلقه الأشياء إذ لم يكن له فيه نفع ولا كان عابئا به ، فزعم أنه خلق العرض على ثواب الأبد . وذلك حكمة ، فيكون فعله لنفع يكون لخلقه لا لعلمة تقدمت الخلق ، وهو كاتحاد البنيان وأنواع الأشياء يحدث من قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : وقد بينا نحن ما يقتضى هذا الحرف من الجواب عن الحكمة فنسأل عنه العباد على أن السؤال عن العلة محال ؛ لإحالة أنه يكون لأحد عليه سلطان ، أو يخرج فعله وبعد، فإن السؤال عن تعرف حكمة الربوبية وحق ربوبيته علينا معرفته ومعرفة حقه وأمره والقيام بما علينا من طاعته وتعظيمه ، والإعداد لحق الجواب في كل ما يقوله ويعلمه ، وذلك يُشغلنا عن طلب الاعتلال له في فعله ، أو الاحتجاج بالجواب عنه فما تعدى السائل طوره ، وأعرض عما عليه من أعذاره لفعله الذي هو مسئول عنه مجزي به، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he was asked about His creation of things when He had no benefit in it nor was He concerned with it. So he claimed that He created the universe for everlasting reward. And that is wisdom, so His act is for a benefit that occurs to His creation, not for knowledge that preceded creation. And it is like the cohesion of a building and the types of things that occur, from what Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And we have already shown what this point requires of answer concerning wisdom, so we ask about it from the servants, based on the fact that the question about the cause is impossible because it is impossible that anyone has authority over Him, or that His act is external. Then the question about knowing the wisdom of lordship and the right of His lordship upon us we must know Him and know His right and His command and fulfill what is upon us of obedience and veneration, and preparation for the correct answer in everything He says and knows. And that occupies us from seeking a cause for His act, or from arguing by answering it. So the questioner exceeded his bounds and turned away from what was upon him of excuses for his act for which he is responsible and requited. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 733

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a question posed about the purpose of creation. It begins by stating the objection: someone claimed God created for a reward. The author then argues that questioning the cause (علة) of God's act is impossible because it implies someone has authority over Him. Instead, the proper response is to focus on knowing God, His commands, and worshiping Him, which occupies us from seeking a cause for His actions. The questioner overstepped by turning away from his own responsibility. The passage ends by affirming reliance on Allah.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that seeking the reason behind God's actions is permissible and even commendable.
Correction: This passage shows that seeking a cause (علة) for God's actions is invalid because it implies external authority over God. The proper approach is to accept His actions as wise and focus on obedience.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, why is the question about the cause of creation invalid?
A) Because creation has no purpose.
B) Because it implies someone has authority over God.
C) Because God did not actually create.
D) Because the question is about benefit, not cause.

Key Takeaway
Questioning the cause of God's actions is invalid because it assumes a standard outside God; our duty is to know and obey Him.

Why Two Eternal Principles Cannot Coexist
Maturidi enumerates possible relations between two eternals and deduces their logical impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the very concept of two independent eternal beings leads to logical absurdities.

Core Idea
Two eternal rulers cannot both be all-powerful; one must be subordinate.

Imam Maturidi begins his refutation of dualism by analyzing the possible relationships between two claimed eternal principles. He shows that any arrangement leads to contradiction: either both are limited, or one is not truly eternal. This logical groundwork is essential for understanding why dualism fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possibilities
Maturidi considers three cases: each can annihilate the other, neither can, or only one can. As you read, note the implication of each.
None of the three allows both to be truly eternal.
2
Analyze incapacity and ignorance
If each can destroy the other or neither can, both are marked by incapacity (ʿajz) and ignorance (jahl). Consider why these are incompatible with divinity.
3
Conclude one must be subordinate
If only one is powerful, the other is a servant, not a lord. This reframes the entire dualist claim.
This conclusion directly attacks the dualist position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : نقول وبالله التوفيق إن القول بأكثر من أن كل واحد منهم يملك إفناء غيره أو لا ، أو يملك الواحد خاصة ، فإن كان الأول أو الثانى لحقها عجز ، مع ما فيه من الجهل بتدبير الإهلاك بالحيل ، إن لم يكن بالقوة ، وإن قدر الواحد بطل غيره ؛ لما لا يتركه يعاديه في ملكه وينازعه في ربوبيته وله قدرة تصفية الملك له واحد لا يخلو من . وبعد ، فإن العاجز الجاهل أحق أن يكون عبدا مربوبا دون أن يكون ربا متلكاً ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وهذا يبطل على من يقول بالظلمة والنور لما جهل النور حيث وقع في وثاق الآخر ، والظلمة حيث منعت عن عملها وهو الشر في الآخر ، ومع ما تفرقت أجزاؤهما وتشتتت أحوالهما حتى عجز كل واحد منها أن يظهر سلطانه ويستولى على ماله ، جل ربنا وتعالى عن أن يكون هذه صفته . وأيضا أن القول من أصحاب الإثنين قول بنهاية كل واحد منها من جانب وارتفاعها من سائر الجوانب ، فإن كان الارتفاع دليل القدم لزم الحدث في وجه الحد ، وإن لم يكن لزم الحدث في الكل، مع ما إن لم يقدر النور على تخليص جزوه المتناهى عن يد عدوه بالأجزاء التى لا تتناهى ، ولا كانت تلك الأجزاء قدرت على حفظ ذلك الجزء من يديها قبل الوقوع في وثاقها ، أنى يقدر إذا أراد بعدد الوقوع في وثاق الظلمة والتخليص من قيده . وعلى قول من يجعل الحواس كلها للنور دون الظلمة ، والعلم كله ، وكذلك جميع ما وصف به الظلمة مما قاله عليه : أعمى لا يبصر ، عاجز لا يقدر ، ضعيف لا يقوى ، شر بالطبع لا بالقوة ، فنسأل الله أن يعصمنا عن العدول عن سبيله والابتلاء بشبكة الثنوية ، فإنه لا قوة إلا بالله . مع ما كل اثنين لا بد من انفراد كل بمكان إن كان جسما أو عرضا فإن كان عرضا فمفارقته توجب تافه ، وإن كان جسمها فإما أن يكون مكان كل واحد منهما من جوهره فلا يقوم فى مضادة حاله كالماني في البر والليلي من البصر بالنهار ، وإن كان من غير جوهره ألف الخير بالشر، والشر بالخير وذلك ينقض معتمدهم فى القول بالعدد ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Abu Mansur said, may Allah have mercy on him: We say, and with Allah is success, that the statement about more than one (eternal) is either that each of them can annihilate the other or not, or that only one can annihilate. If the first or second, incapacity (ʿajz) attaches to them, along with ignorance (jahl) in managing destruction by devices, if not by force. And if one is powerful, the other becomes invalid; because the powerful one would not leave him to oppose him in his dominion and contend with him in his lordship, while having the power to purify the dominion for himself alone, one who is not free from… And further, the incapable ignorant one is more deserving to be a servant and subject than to be a lord and possessor. There is no power except with Allah. And this invalidates those who say (there are) darkness and light, because light is ignorant since it fell into the fetter of the other, and darkness (is ignorant) because it was prevented from its action which is evil in the other, and since their parts were scattered and their states dispersed, until each one of them was incapable of manifesting its authority and seizing its property. Exalted is our Lord and high above having such attribute. Also, the statement of the dualists is a statement of each one having an end from one side and extension from the other sides. If the extension indicates pre-eternity (qidam), then temporality (ḥudūth) necessarily applies to the side of the end; if not, temporality necessarily applies to the whole. Also, since the light could not rescue its finite part from the hand of its enemy by infinite parts, and those parts could not preserve that part from its hands before falling into its fetter, how can it (the light) be able, if it wills after falling into the fetter of darkness, to rescue it from its bond? And according to the statement of those who assign all senses to light and not to darkness, and all knowledge, and likewise all that with which darkness is described, what is said against it: blind, does not see; incapable, does not have power; weak, does not have strength; evil by nature (bi’l-ṭabʿ) not by power. So we ask Allah to protect us from deviating from His path and from being afflicted by the net of dualism (al-thunawiyya), for there is no power except with Allah. Also, every two (principles) must each occupy a separate place, whether they are a body or an accident. If an accident, its separation necessitates its worthlessness (tāfih). If a body, then either the place of each is from its essence, so it does not stand in opposition to its state, like the one preventing cold and the one preventing sight by day; or (the place) is from other than its essence, then it combines good with evil and evil with good, and that demolishes their reliance on multiplicity. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 739

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualism (al-thunawiyya), specifically the claim that there are two eternal principles: light (al-nūr) and darkness (al-ẓulma). Maturidi begins by categorizing the possible relations between two eternal beings: either each can destroy the other, neither can, or only one can. If either can destroy the other or neither can, both are marked by incapacity (ʿajz) and ignorance (jahl), which disqualifies them from divinity. If only one is powerful, the other is necessarily subordinate and thus a servant, not a lord. He then applies this to the dualist doctrine: light and darkness are both shown to be deficient—light falls into darkness's trap, darkness is prevented from its own action. Their parts are scattered and states dispersed, so neither can manifest full authority. This refutes their claim to eternality. Second, he argues that each must have a limit in one direction while extending infinitely in others; if that extension indicates pre-eternity (qidam), then the limited side implies temporality (ḥudūth); if not, the whole is temporal. Third, he points out the absurdity that light cannot rescue its finite part from darkness using infinite parts—if infinite parts cannot prevent the initial capture, they cannot later rescue it. Fourth, he critiques the dualists' assignment of all senses and knowledge to light alone, leaving darkness as blind, incapable, weak, and evil by nature—a pathetic opponent unworthy of being a co-eternal principle. Finally, he argues that two entities must occupy separate places. If they are accidents, their separation entails worthlessness; if bodies, their places are either essential or accidental—both cases lead to contradictions (like a cold-preventer coexisting with cold, or good mixing with evil). Thus the doctrine of multiplicity collapses. The lesson ends with a prayer for protection from dualism.

Key Terms
ثنوية (thunawiyya)
عجز (ʿajz)
جهل (jahl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two eternal beings can coexist in perfect harmony without conflict.
Correction: Maturidi shows that either they conflict or one is subordinate, both of which contradict true eternality.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the logical problem with the dualist claim that two eternal principles exist?
A) They cannot both be all-powerful because they would limit each other.
B) They are made of light and darkness, which are opposites.
C) The universe is too complex for two creators.
D) The Quran explicitly denies it.

Key Takeaway
Two eternal beings cannot both be divine; any arrangement leads to logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your heart, do you sometimes serve two masters—seeking Allah's pleasure while also craving people's approval? How does the logical impossibility of two eternal rulers expose the foolishness of such divided loyalty, and what will you do today to cut that second master out?
?
Maturidi argues that any being marked by incapacity is unworthy of worship. Where in your life are you relying on your own limited strength rather than turning to Allah in utter need? Will you confess that hidden incapacity and surrender that area to Him now?
?
If a truly powerful being would never tolerate a rival in His dominion, what does it mean that you allow competing loves, fears, and ambitions to reign alongside Allah in your soul? What step will you take this week to dethrone those rivals and make His lordship undivided in your life?

The Incompetence of Light and Darkness
Maturidi shows the specific deficiencies of light and darkness as principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi points out the specific failures of light and darkness that prove they cannot be eternal gods.

Core Idea
Light falls into darkness, and darkness cannot act—both are deficient.

After establishing the logical impossibility of two eternals in general, Maturidi applies the critique to the specific dualist claim that the world originates from light and darkness. He highlights their mutual failures and dispersed states, showing they lack the authority expected of divine beings.

Guided Path
1
Note light's ignorance
Light is said to be ignorant because it fell into the fetter of darkness. Ask yourself: can a true god be trapped by another?
2
Observe darkness's impotence
Darkness is prevented from its own action (which is evil). It cannot manifest its will. Consider why this makes it an imperfect principle.
3
Consider the scattered parts argument
Both light and darkness have parts that are scattered and states dispersed. Neither can exercise full sovereignty. This undermines their claim to be eternal and independent.
Maturidi then exalts Allah above such attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : نقول وبالله التوفيق إن القول بأكثر من أن كل واحد منهم يملك إفناء غيره أو لا ، أو يملك الواحد خاصة ، فإن كان الأول أو الثانى لحقها عجز ، مع ما فيه من الجهل بتدبير الإهلاك بالحيل ، إن لم يكن بالقوة ، وإن قدر الواحد بطل غيره ؛ لما لا يتركه يعاديه في ملكه وينازعه في ربوبيته وله قدرة تصفية الملك له واحد لا يخلو من . وبعد ، فإن العاجز الجاهل أحق أن يكون عبدا مربوبا دون أن يكون ربا متلكاً ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وهذا يبطل على من يقول بالظلمة والنور لما جهل النور حيث وقع في وثاق الآخر ، والظلمة حيث منعت عن عملها وهو الشر في الآخر ، ومع ما تفرقت أجزاؤهما وتشتتت أحوالهما حتى عجز كل واحد منها أن يظهر سلطانه ويستولى على ماله ، جل ربنا وتعالى عن أن يكون هذه صفته . وأيضا أن القول من أصحاب الإثنين قول بنهاية كل واحد منها من جانب وارتفاعها من سائر الجوانب ، فإن كان الارتفاع دليل القدم لزم الحدث في وجه الحد ، وإن لم يكن لزم الحدث في الكل، مع ما إن لم يقدر النور على تخليص جزوه المتناهى عن يد عدوه بالأجزاء التى لا تتناهى ، ولا كانت تلك الأجزاء قدرت على حفظ ذلك الجزء من يديها قبل الوقوع في وثاقها ، أنى يقدر إذا أراد بعدد الوقوع في وثاق الظلمة والتخليص من قيده . وعلى قول من يجعل الحواس كلها للنور دون الظلمة ، والعلم كله ، وكذلك جميع ما وصف به الظلمة مما قاله عليه : أعمى لا يبصر ، عاجز لا يقدر ، ضعيف لا يقوى ، شر بالطبع لا بالقوة ، فنسأل الله أن يعصمنا عن العدول عن سبيله والابتلاء بشبكة الثنوية ، فإنه لا قوة إلا بالله . مع ما كل اثنين لا بد من انفراد كل بمكان إن كان جسما أو عرضا فإن كان عرضا فمفارقته توجب تافه ، وإن كان جسمها فإما أن يكون مكان كل واحد منهما من جوهره فلا يقوم فى مضادة حاله كالماني في البر والليلي من البصر بالنهار ، وإن كان من غير جوهره ألف الخير بالشر، والشر بالخير وذلك ينقض معتمدهم فى القول بالعدد ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Abu Mansur said, may Allah have mercy on him: We say, and with Allah is success, that the statement about more than one (eternal) is either that each of them can annihilate the other or not, or that only one can annihilate. If the first or second, incapacity (ʿajz) attaches to them, along with ignorance (jahl) in managing destruction by devices, if not by force. And if one is powerful, the other becomes invalid; because the powerful one would not leave him to oppose him in his dominion and contend with him in his lordship, while having the power to purify the dominion for himself alone, one who is not free from… And further, the incapable ignorant one is more deserving to be a servant and subject than to be a lord and possessor. There is no power except with Allah. And this invalidates those who say (there are) darkness and light, because light is ignorant since it fell into the fetter of the other, and darkness (is ignorant) because it was prevented from its action which is evil in the other, and since their parts were scattered and their states dispersed, until each one of them was incapable of manifesting its authority and seizing its property. Exalted is our Lord and high above having such attribute. Also, the statement of the dualists is a statement of each one having an end from one side and extension from the other sides. If the extension indicates pre-eternity (qidam), then temporality (ḥudūth) necessarily applies to the side of the end; if not, temporality necessarily applies to the whole. Also, since the light could not rescue its finite part from the hand of its enemy by infinite parts, and those parts could not preserve that part from its hands before falling into its fetter, how can it (the light) be able, if it wills after falling into the fetter of darkness, to rescue it from its bond? And according to the statement of those who assign all senses to light and not to darkness, and all knowledge, and likewise all that with which darkness is described, what is said against it: blind, does not see; incapable, does not have power; weak, does not have strength; evil by nature (bi’l-ṭabʿ) not by power. So we ask Allah to protect us from deviating from His path and from being afflicted by the net of dualism (al-thunawiyya), for there is no power except with Allah. Also, every two (principles) must each occupy a separate place, whether they are a body or an accident. If an accident, its separation necessitates its worthlessness (tāfih). If a body, then either the place of each is from its essence, so it does not stand in opposition to its state, like the one preventing cold and the one preventing sight by day; or (the place) is from other than its essence, then it combines good with evil and evil with good, and that demolishes their reliance on multiplicity. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 739

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualism (al-thunawiyya), specifically the claim that there are two eternal principles: light (al-nūr) and darkness (al-ẓulma). Maturidi begins by categorizing the possible relations between two eternal beings: either each can destroy the other, neither can, or only one can. If either can destroy the other or neither can, both are marked by incapacity (ʿajz) and ignorance (jahl), which disqualifies them from divinity. If only one is powerful, the other is necessarily subordinate and thus a servant, not a lord. He then applies this to the dualist doctrine: light and darkness are both shown to be deficient—light falls into darkness's trap, darkness is prevented from its own action. Their parts are scattered and states dispersed, so neither can manifest full authority. This refutes their claim to eternality. Second, he argues that each must have a limit in one direction while extending infinitely in others; if that extension indicates pre-eternity (qidam), then the limited side implies temporality (ḥudūth); if not, the whole is temporal. Third, he points out the absurdity that light cannot rescue its finite part from darkness using infinite parts—if infinite parts cannot prevent the initial capture, they cannot later rescue it. Fourth, he critiques the dualists' assignment of all senses and knowledge to light alone, leaving darkness as blind, incapable, weak, and evil by nature—a pathetic opponent unworthy of being a co-eternal principle. Finally, he argues that two entities must occupy separate places. If they are accidents, their separation entails worthlessness; if bodies, their places are either essential or accidental—both cases lead to contradictions (like a cold-preventer coexisting with cold, or good mixing with evil). Thus the doctrine of multiplicity collapses. The lesson ends with a prayer for protection from dualism.

Key Terms
نور (nūr)
ظلمة (ẓulma)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Light and darkness are perfect opposites that together create the world.
Correction: Maturidi shows they are both deficient: light is ignorant and trapped, darkness is prevented from acting, and their parts are scattered.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the state of light and darkness?
A) They are both all-powerful but in different domains.
B) They are ignorant and their parts are scattered.
C) They are perfectly balanced.
D) They are both evil.

Key Takeaway
Light and darkness are both shown to be deficient and incapable of being eternal principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you, like the dualists, attributed independent power to something other than Allah—perhaps your own abilities, wealth, or relationships? How does this passage's exposure of the incompetence of light and darkness challenge you to reassess those attachments?
?
If you truly believe that Allah alone has perfect authority, why do you still fear the 'darkness' of your circumstances as if it could overpower His will? What step will you take today to realign your fears with the tawheed this passage teaches?
?
Maturidi shows that light and darkness are scattered and cannot control their own parts. Where in your life have you tried to manage scattered pieces—your time, your loyalties, your priorities—as if you were independent from Allah? How will you commit to placing your whole trust in the One who holds everything together?

The End and Extension Argument Against Dualism
Maturidi argues that the finite side of each principle implies temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if each eternal principle has a limit, that limit implies temporality, contradicting their supposed pre-eternity.

Core Idea
A limited side means a beginning in time, so the whole cannot be eternal.

Maturidi presents another logical argument: each claimed eternal principle must have an end in one direction and extension in others. If the extension indicates pre-eternity (qidam), then the limited side implies temporality (ḥudūth); if extension does not indicate pre-eternity, then the whole thing is temporal. Either way, dualism's claim to eternity fails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise: each has an end and extension
Maturidi states that dualists claim each eternal has a 'nihaya' (end) from one side and 'irtifa' (extension) from others. This is a spatial or conceptual boundary.
2
Follow the first conditional
If the extension indicates pre-eternity, then the limited side (the end) must be temporal. This would mean the principle is partly temporal.
3
Consider the alternative
If extension does not indicate pre-eternity, then the whole principle is temporal because no part is eternal.
Thus dualism's claim to eternity fails in both cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?

Why Light Cannot Rescue Its Fallen Part
Maturidi argues from light's inability to rescue its own part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that light's inability to rescue its finite part from darkness proves its impotence.

Core Idea
If infinite parts cannot prevent capture, they cannot rescue later.

Maturidi presents a subtle argument: light consists of infinite parts and one finite part. That finite part falls into darkness's grip. If the infinite parts of light could not protect the finite part before it was captured, how could they rescue it afterwards? This reveals light's powerlessness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the finite and infinite parts
Maturidi posits that light has a finite part (a specific portion) and infinite parts (the rest). These are two types.
2
Understand the failure before capture
Before the finite part fell into the fetter of darkness, the infinite parts could not preserve it. This shows the infinite parts lacked the power to protect.
3
Consider the impossibility of rescue after capture
If they couldn't prevent capture, they certainly cannot rescue afterward. Darkness's grip is a form of constraint that light cannot overcome.
Thus light is not omnipotent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : نقول وبالله التوفيق إن القول بأكثر من أن كل واحد منهم يملك إفناء غيره أو لا ، أو يملك الواحد خاصة ، فإن كان الأول أو الثانى لحقها عجز ، مع ما فيه من الجهل بتدبير الإهلاك بالحيل ، إن لم يكن بالقوة ، وإن قدر الواحد بطل غيره ؛ لما لا يتركه يعاديه في ملكه وينازعه في ربوبيته وله قدرة تصفية الملك له واحد لا يخلو من . وبعد ، فإن العاجز الجاهل أحق أن يكون عبدا مربوبا دون أن يكون ربا متلكاً ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وهذا يبطل على من يقول بالظلمة والنور لما جهل النور حيث وقع في وثاق الآخر ، والظلمة حيث منعت عن عملها وهو الشر في الآخر ، ومع ما تفرقت أجزاؤهما وتشتتت أحوالهما حتى عجز كل واحد منها أن يظهر سلطانه ويستولى على ماله ، جل ربنا وتعالى عن أن يكون هذه صفته . وأيضا أن القول من أصحاب الإثنين قول بنهاية كل واحد منها من جانب وارتفاعها من سائر الجوانب ، فإن كان الارتفاع دليل القدم لزم الحدث في وجه الحد ، وإن لم يكن لزم الحدث في الكل، مع ما إن لم يقدر النور على تخليص جزوه المتناهى عن يد عدوه بالأجزاء التى لا تتناهى ، ولا كانت تلك الأجزاء قدرت على حفظ ذلك الجزء من يديها قبل الوقوع في وثاقها ، أنى يقدر إذا أراد بعدد الوقوع في وثاق الظلمة والتخليص من قيده . وعلى قول من يجعل الحواس كلها للنور دون الظلمة ، والعلم كله ، وكذلك جميع ما وصف به الظلمة مما قاله عليه : أعمى لا يبصر ، عاجز لا يقدر ، ضعيف لا يقوى ، شر بالطبع لا بالقوة ، فنسأل الله أن يعصمنا عن العدول عن سبيله والابتلاء بشبكة الثنوية ، فإنه لا قوة إلا بالله . مع ما كل اثنين لا بد من انفراد كل بمكان إن كان جسما أو عرضا فإن كان عرضا فمفارقته توجب تافه ، وإن كان جسمها فإما أن يكون مكان كل واحد منهما من جوهره فلا يقوم فى مضادة حاله كالماني في البر والليلي من البصر بالنهار ، وإن كان من غير جوهره ألف الخير بالشر، والشر بالخير وذلك ينقض معتمدهم فى القول بالعدد ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Abu Mansur said, may Allah have mercy on him: We say, and with Allah is success, that the statement about more than one (eternal) is either that each of them can annihilate the other or not, or that only one can annihilate. If the first or second, incapacity (ʿajz) attaches to them, along with ignorance (jahl) in managing destruction by devices, if not by force. And if one is powerful, the other becomes invalid; because the powerful one would not leave him to oppose him in his dominion and contend with him in his lordship, while having the power to purify the dominion for himself alone, one who is not free from… And further, the incapable ignorant one is more deserving to be a servant and subject than to be a lord and possessor. There is no power except with Allah. And this invalidates those who say (there are) darkness and light, because light is ignorant since it fell into the fetter of the other, and darkness (is ignorant) because it was prevented from its action which is evil in the other, and since their parts were scattered and their states dispersed, until each one of them was incapable of manifesting its authority and seizing its property. Exalted is our Lord and high above having such attribute. Also, the statement of the dualists is a statement of each one having an end from one side and extension from the other sides. If the extension indicates pre-eternity (qidam), then temporality (ḥudūth) necessarily applies to the side of the end; if not, temporality necessarily applies to the whole. Also, since the light could not rescue its finite part from the hand of its enemy by infinite parts, and those parts could not preserve that part from its hands before falling into its fetter, how can it (the light) be able, if it wills after falling into the fetter of darkness, to rescue it from its bond? And according to the statement of those who assign all senses to light and not to darkness, and all knowledge, and likewise all that with which darkness is described, what is said against it: blind, does not see; incapable, does not have power; weak, does not have strength; evil by nature (bi’l-ṭabʿ) not by power. So we ask Allah to protect us from deviating from His path and from being afflicted by the net of dualism (al-thunawiyya), for there is no power except with Allah. Also, every two (principles) must each occupy a separate place, whether they are a body or an accident. If an accident, its separation necessitates its worthlessness (tāfih). If a body, then either the place of each is from its essence, so it does not stand in opposition to its state, like the one preventing cold and the one preventing sight by day; or (the place) is from other than its essence, then it combines good with evil and evil with good, and that demolishes their reliance on multiplicity. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 739

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualism (al-thunawiyya), specifically the claim that there are two eternal principles: light (al-nūr) and darkness (al-ẓulma). Maturidi begins by categorizing the possible relations between two eternal beings: either each can destroy the other, neither can, or only one can. If either can destroy the other or neither can, both are marked by incapacity (ʿajz) and ignorance (jahl), which disqualifies them from divinity. If only one is powerful, the other is necessarily subordinate and thus a servant, not a lord. He then applies this to the dualist doctrine: light and darkness are both shown to be deficient—light falls into darkness's trap, darkness is prevented from its own action. Their parts are scattered and states dispersed, so neither can manifest full authority. This refutes their claim to eternality. Second, he argues that each must have a limit in one direction while extending infinitely in others; if that extension indicates pre-eternity (qidam), then the limited side implies temporality (ḥudūth); if not, the whole is temporal. Third, he points out the absurdity that light cannot rescue its finite part from darkness using infinite parts—if infinite parts cannot prevent the initial capture, they cannot later rescue it. Fourth, he critiques the dualists' assignment of all senses and knowledge to light alone, leaving darkness as blind, incapable, weak, and evil by nature—a pathetic opponent unworthy of being a co-eternal principle. Finally, he argues that two entities must occupy separate places. If they are accidents, their separation entails worthlessness; if bodies, their places are either essential or accidental—both cases lead to contradictions (like a cold-preventer coexisting with cold, or good mixing with evil). Thus the doctrine of multiplicity collapses. The lesson ends with a prayer for protection from dualism.

Key Terms
تخليص (takhliṣ)
جزء (juz')
متناهٍ (mutanāhin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Light, being good, has the power to overcome darkness eventually.
Correction: Maturidi shows that light cannot even rescue a part of itself from darkness, proving its power is limited.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from light's inability to rescue its finite part?
A) Light is merciful.
B) Light is not all-powerful.
C) Darkness is stronger.
D) The finite part is unimportant.

Key Takeaway
Light's failure to rescue its own part shows it is not omnipotent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you genuinely believed that nothing besides Allah has the power to save you from the darkness of your own sins, how would that change the way you seek forgiveness tonight?
?
When you look at your life, in what specific areas do you still act as if something other than Allah—such as your own efforts, status, or others' approval—has the ultimate power to help you? What would it take to uproot that reliance?

The Incoherence of Dualism: Senses and Space
Maturidi critiques dualist attributes of darkness and presents a spatial argument against two principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the dualist assignment of all senses to light and the logical problems of two principles occupying space.

Core Idea
Darkness is described as blind and weak, and two principles cannot share space without contradiction.

Maturidi continues his refutation by examining what dualists say about the attributes of light and darkness. He points out that if light has all senses and knowledge, darkness is reduced to a pitiful entity—blind, incapable, and evil by nature. Then he raises a spatial problem: two principles must each occupy a place, leading to contradictions whether they are bodies or accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of darkness
Dualists describe darkness as blind, incapable, weak, and evil by nature. Maturidi uses this to show that such a being cannot be a god.
Notice the contrast with light.
2
Consider the prayer of protection
Maturidi asks Allah to protect from dualism. This marks a transition.
3
Analyze the spatial argument
Maturidi argues that any two distinct beings must occupy separate places. If they are accidents, separation renders them worthless. If bodies, their places are either essential or accidental, leading to contradictions.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Either way, the dualist position is refuted. The prayer emphasizes reliance on Allah.

?

?

Maturidi's Refutation of the Dualist Claim on Darkness and Light
Maturidi refutes a dualist claim by presenting a temporal dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the dualist claim about the meeting of darkness and light by presenting a dilemma regarding its temporal status.

Core Idea
Every event must be either eternal or originated; dualists cannot escape this dilemma.

Imam Maturidi engages with a dualist argument about darkness moving to meet light. He first points out that if darkness acts by choice, their theory of fixed natures is undermined. Then he presents a logical dilemma: the meeting of darkness and light must either be eternal or have a beginning, both of which contradict their position. This short passage demonstrates the use of rational argument in kalam to defend Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the literal meaning.
Notice the compact style of Maturidi's argument.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The passage has two distinct parts: first a critique of the concept of natures, then a temporal dilemma. Locate where each part begins.
3
Analyze the dilemma
List the two options presented: either the meeting is eternal (أبدا) or it began (بالابتداء). Consider what each implies for the dualist doctrine.
Think about why both options are problematic for the dualists.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how this dilemma disproves the dualist claim. How does Maturidi's argument rely on the categories of eternal and originated?
This connects to the broader kalam concept of temporal origination (حدوث).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالظلمة فعاله باختيار ، فالقول في الطباع على ذلك فاسد . وأخيرا عنهم تحرك الظلمة إلى أن لقيت [النور] فدخلت عليه ، إن قالوا : أبدا ما مر فى كلام " الدهر ، وإن قالوا بالابتداء لزم الحدث ، والله الموفق .
Darkness is an act by choice, so the statement about natures on that is corrupt. And finally about them: darkness moved until it met [light] and entered upon it. If they say 'always' it would pass in time, and if they say 'at the beginning' it necessitates temporal origination. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 760

Commentary
This passage refutes a dualist claim about the movement of darkness towards light. Maturidi first notes that if darkness acts by choice, their theory of fixed natures (طباع) is undermined. He then presents a dilemma: either the meeting of darkness and light was eternal, which would make it part of an infinite temporal sequence, or it began at a point in time, which would make it originated (حادث). Both options contradict their doctrine. The passage concludes with a prayer phrase.

Key Terms
طباع
الدهر
الابتداء
الحدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the dualist view of the meeting of darkness and light is coherent and does not lead to a contradiction.
Correction: Maturidi shows that whether the meeting is eternal or temporally originated, it leads to a contradiction with the dualist position.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does Maturidi present to the dualists regarding the meeting of darkness and light?
A) Either it was an act of choice or an act of nature.
B) Either it was eternal or it began in time.
C) Either it was good or evil.
D) Either it was by mixing or by separation.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's dilemma refutes dualism by showing that the meeting of darkness and light cannot be eternal nor originated without contradicting their own principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or evil, do you ever find yourself thinking there is a force of darkness outside God's control? How does Maturidi's argument that even darkness is an act by choice challenge that thought, and what would it mean for you to truly affirm God's sovereignty over all things?
?
If you claim to believe in one God, where in your life do you subtly act as if two opposing powers are at work—for example, blaming 'bad luck' or 'society' instead of your own choices? Reflect on one area where you can accept that everything is from God and respond with faith rather than complaint.
?
Maturidi forces dualists to choose: either darkness meeting light is eternal (meaningless infinite) or originated (contradicts eternity). Are you willing to accept that God's wisdom is beyond your understanding, or do you try to force a logical explanation for evil? Will you submit to the mystery and trust His decision today?

Maturidi's Critique of Anthropomorphism in Dualist Thought
Maturidi critiques dualists for anthropomorphizing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers anthropomorphism the root error of dualists and how he critiques their methodology.

Core Idea
Judging God by human needs and limits is the source of theological error.

This passage continues Maturidi's refutation of dualism. He argues that the dualists' failure to grasp divine wisdom leads them to project human limitations onto God, thus misjudging His actions. Understanding this critique helps clarify the Islamic principle of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a positive assertion followed by a critique.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two main claims
First, Maturidi states that belief in one God does not compel acceptance of another. Second, he identifies the dualists' error: they measure God by human attributes.
These claims set up the critique.
3
Reflect on Maturidi's diagnostic method
Ask yourself: Why does Maturidi call their assumption ignorance? Note how he uses a conditional (لو تأملوا) to show that reflection would reveal their error.
This highlights the importance of sound methodology in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول بالواحد لا يضطر صاحبه إلى القول بآخر بوجه . وأصل ذلك أن هؤلاء قوم لم تبلغ تقولهم المبلغ الذى يدرك به حكمة الربوبية في الأشياء ، وظنوا أن يكون الرب على صفتهم من الحاجات والشهوات واحتمال الآفات وشوائب العاهات ، فقدروا أفعاله بالذى علموا الحكمة بأفعال أنفسهم ، ولو تأملوا ما فيه من الضرورات السواتر المانعة عن الإحاطة بالأشياء ، ثم بمصالح أنفسهم التي في ذلك جل كدهم ، وجهدوا لعلموا أن الجهل هو الذي سدهم عن إدراك الحكمة في ذك ، وأحق الناس بهذا هم
Then the statement of the One does not compel its holder to affirm another in any way. The root of this is that these people's intellects have not reached the level by which the wisdom of Lordship (hikmat al-rububiyya) in things is perceived. They thought that the Lord should be upon their description of needs (hajat), desires (shahawat), susceptibility to afflictions, and blemishes of defects. So they measured His actions by what they knew of wisdom in their own actions. And if they reflected on the necessities and obstacles that prevent encompassing things, and then on the interests of their selves which are the sum of their effort, they would know that ignorance is what prevented them from perceiving wisdom therein. And the most deserving people of this [ignorance] are they.
Source pages p. 764

Commentary
This passage is both a positive assertion and a critique. The author first states that belief in one God (tawhid) does not force one to accept another principle. He then explains that the dualists' error stems from their inability to comprehend divine wisdom because they judge God by human standards: needs, desires, and vulnerability. They anthropomorphize God and thus fail to see the true wisdom in creation. Their ignorance is self-inflicted.

Key Terms
حكمة الربوبية
حاجات
شهوات
آفات
عاهات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi's charge of ignorance is merely an ad hominem attack.
Correction: Maturidi is making a theological methodology critique: the dualists' error stems from a flawed epistemic foundation, not personal deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the root cause of the dualists' error?
A) They lack logical reasoning
B) They measure God by human attributes
C) They reject all revelation
D) They are intellectually dishonest

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that projecting human attributes onto God is a fundamental methodological error leading to dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When in your life have you caught yourself expecting God to act according to your sense of fairness or wisdom? How does this passage expose the pride behind that expectation?
?
The dualists measured God by their own actions. What are the 'human standards' you most often project onto God — and what would it cost you to surrender them and admit you cannot fully comprehend His wisdom?
?
Maturidi says ignorance, not insight, blinds the dualists. What comfortable assumption about God are you holding onto that might actually be a blind spot? Will you ask Him tonight to expose it?

The Limits of Imagination and Senses in Knowing Allah
Maturidi refutes denial of the unseen by critiquing the limits of imagination and senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying Allah based on the inability to imagine or sense Him is logically flawed, as argued by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Imagination depends on the senses; God transcends both.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi confronts those who deny the existence of the unseen (al-ghāʾib) because it cannot be imagined or sensed. He exposes the self-defeating nature of such reasoning and establishes a foundational principle: imagination is tied to sensory experience, and even our knowledge of the senses themselves involves the unseen. This lesson unpacks his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the source text carefully. Notice the flow: from a hypothetical conditional to a refutation of the Magians, then to a definition of imagination, and finally to a principle about sense knowledge.
The passage is dense; read it twice to follow the logic.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Find the conditional statement: 'if it were permissible to deny a thing from nothing because it is not conceived in imagination...' Understand the absurd consequence that would follow.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Note the refutation of the Magians
Maturidi mentions the Magians and others who blindly follow their predecessors. See how this is connected to the earlier conditional: their denial is based on faulty reasoning about imagination.
4
Understand the nature of imagination
Read Maturidi's definition: imagination is an estimation based on what is sensed. When the sensed object is absent, one imagines the state of sensing it or something similar. This shows imagination is limited to the sensory realm.
5
Grasp the final principle
The concluding lines state that imagination is knowledge of the sensed, but sense-knowledge itself proves the necessity of knowing the unseen. Everyone is ignorant of the essence of sense, so denying God because He is not sensed is invalid.
This principle refutes the denial of the unseen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جاز إنكار الشيء لا من شيء بما لا يتصور في الوهم الجاز لكل مؤوف الحاسة إنكار ما يدرك بها إذ هو غير مدرك إنكار كل غائب بلغه الحاسة. وفي ذلك نقض المجوسية وغيرهم: إذ جميعا اتبعوا أوائلهم. ثم التصور في الوهم تقديره مما تقع عليه الحاسة، عليه الحاسة، إذا ارتفعت فتصور حال وقوع الحاسة في وهمه، أو يقدر مثله في الوهم. ثم الله سبحانه لم يعرف من طريق الحواس ولا له مثال في المعروف، بطل التقدير به. ثم الأصل أن التصور في الوهم هو علم الحسن، أو في علم الحس دليل لزوم العلم بما لم يحس، ولأن يعرف: إذ كل ذي جاهل بمائية الحس وكيفيته، فلزم ذلك في كل حس هو كذلك.
And if it were permissible to deny a thing from nothing because it is not conceived in imagination, then it would be permissible for every deficient of sense to deny what is perceived by it, since it is not perceived—denial of every unseen that the sense has reached. And in that is a refutation of the Magians and others: for they all followed their predecessors. Then conception in imagination is estimation of something upon which the sense falls; if sense is removed, then he conceives the state of sense falling in his imagination, or he estimates its like in imagination. Then God, exalted, is not known through the path of senses and has no example in the known; the estimation by it is invalid. Then the principle is that conception in imagination is knowledge of the sense, or in knowledge of sense is evidence for the necessity of knowledge of what is not sensed, and because to know: for every possessor is ignorant of the essence of sense and its quality, so that is necessary for every sense that is likewise.
Source pages p. 774

Commentary
This passage refutes those who deny the unseen (al-ghāʾib) because it cannot be imagined or sensed. Maturidi argues that if such denial were allowed, then anyone with defective senses could deny what they perceive—which is absurd. The Magians (Majus) and others are refuted because they blindly follow their forefathers. He then explains the nature of imagination (tawahhum): it estimates something based on what is sensed. If the sensed thing is absent, one imagines the state of sensing it. But God is not known through the senses and has no likeness in the known world, so imagining Him is invalid. The principle (asl) is that imagination is knowledge of the sensed, but sense-knowledge also requires knowledge of what is not sensed. Indeed, everyone is ignorant of the essence (māhiyya) and quality of sense itself. So the same applies to every sense: we know it by its effects, not essence. Thus, denying God because He is not sensed is unwarranted.

Key Terms
التصور في الوهم
الحاسة
الغائب
المجوسية
مائية الحس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot exist because He cannot be imagined or sensed.
Correction: Maturidi shows that imagination is limited to sensory objects and that even sense perception involves the unseen. Denying God on this basis is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the consequence of allowing denial of things not perceived by senses?
A) It leads to the denial of all unseen things including sound theological beliefs
B) It would allow anyone with defective senses to deny what they perceive
C) It proves God's existence
D) It is the foundation of knowledge

Key Takeaway
Denying the unseen because it cannot be imagined or sensed is self-defeating, since imagination depends on senses and even sense-knowledge points to the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth about Allah simply because it didn't fit your limited imagination? What does that reveal about your trust in your own mind over revelation? Will you now consciously reject that tendency and submit to what Allah has told you about Himself, even if you cannot picture it?
?
The Magians followed their forefathers without question. Where in your life have you accepted beliefs about God just because they were familiar, without checking them against the Quran and reason? Are you willing to uproot any inherited misconception and replace it with what this lesson teaches?
?
The lesson shows that even the essence of your own senses is unknown to you. You rely on them every day, yet they remain a mystery. Does this humble your intellectual pride? How will you now approach the unseen realities of Allah—with humility and submission rather than demanding sensory proof?

Why Only Jesus? The Part-Son Argument
Al-Maturidi refutes the sonship of Jesus by questioning the uniqueness of a part becoming son and the age incompatibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's logical refutation of the Christian claim that Jesus is the Son of God as a part of God, focusing on the uniqueness problem and the age contradiction.

Core Idea
If Jesus is a part of God, why is he the only son? And how can an ancient part be younger?

Al-Maturidi begins by challenging the very basis of the Christian claim that Jesus is the Son of God as a part of the divine essence. He points out the logical inconsistency: if the spirit in Jesus is a part of God, then why is Jesus uniquely called 'son' while other parts of God are not? Moreover, the very notion of sonship implies a relationship of age, which cannot apply to two co-eternal beings. This lesson unpacks the first two arguments from this dense passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Christian claim
Read the passage to understand the Christian claim that Jesus has a pre-eternal spirit that is a part of God, making him the Son.
2
Analyze the uniqueness argument
Notice how al-Maturidi questions why only this part became a son if all parts are similar. Consider the counter-argument that it is the smallest part, leading to all parts being sons.
This exposes a logical absurdity.
3
Examine the age problem
Reflect on the statement that a son is younger than the father. Since both the spirit and God are claimed to be pre-eternal, the relationship of father-son becomes impossible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how these two arguments together undermine the Christian doctrine of Jesus as the divine Son.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله: فيقال لهم: إذ كانت الروح التي فيه قديمة، وهي بعض، كيف صار ابنا ولم يصل غيره من الأبعاض؟ فإن قيل: لأنه أقل ... لزمه جعل كل أبعاض العالم البنين للأكبر منها، ويلزمه أن يجعل كل بعض من البقية كذلك، فيصير بكليته بنين. ثم المعروف أن الابن يكون أصغر الأب، كيف صارا قديمين. وإن جعل الكل في البذرا قيل له: أي شيء منه الابن؟ فإن قال: الكل، صير الكل ابنا وأبا، وفي ذلك جعل الأب ابنا لنفسه. فإن قيل: هو جزء فيه، من غير أن كان في كلية الأصل نقصان نحو الجزء المأخوذ من السراج، عورض بما لو كان الجزء المأخوذ حادثا كما حدث في الذي يؤخذ من السراج، فيبطل قوله في قدم الروح، وهو الابن. وإن زعم منقول من الله كالمأخوذ من السراج] حل[ عليه ما سلف. وبعد، فها أنه يدريه أن المأخوذ من السراج لا ينتقض؟ فإن قيل: معاينتنا إياه كذلك، قيل: لعل الله أحدثه، أو يكون كالنار في الحجر فيخرج، وأيهما كان فهو حادث، والحادث مخلوق، فلم جاز أن يكون ابنا؟
The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: So it is said to them: Since the spirit that is in him is ancient, and it is a part, how did it become a son while no other of the parts reached [him]? If it is said: Because it is the smallest ... then he would have to make every part of the world sons for the greatest of them, and he would have to make every part of the rest likewise, so that in their entirety they become sons. Then it is known that the son is younger than the father; how can both be ancient? And if he makes the whole [place] in the body, let it be said to him: Which thing of him is the son? If he says: The whole, then he makes the whole both son and father, and in that he makes the father a son to himself. If it is said: It is a part in him, without there being any deficiency in the totality of the original, like the part taken from a lamp, it is objected: if the part taken were created just as what happens in that which is taken from a lamp, then his statement about the pre-eternity of the spirit—which is the son—is invalidated. And if he claims it is transferred from God like that taken from a lamp, [then] what preceded applies. Then, indeed, he knows that what is taken from the lamp is not diminished? If it is said: Our observation shows it is so, it is said: Perhaps God created it, or it is like fire in stone that comes out, and whichever it is, it is created, and the created is made, so how can it be a son?
Source pages p. 809

Commentary
This passage is a logical refutation of the Christian doctrine that Jesus is the Son of God as a part of God. The author presents a series of arguments against the notion that Jesus has an ancient divine spirit that is a part of God. The first argument: if the spirit in Jesus is a part of God (a 'some' of His parts), why is Jesus uniquely called 'son' while other parts did not become incarnate? If the opponent replies that it is because this part is the smallest (or most special), then every part of the universe would have to be considered a son relative to something greater, leading to an infinite regress of sons. Moreover, the son is traditionally younger than the father, but if both the spirit and God are ancient, this cannot be. The second argument: if the opponent says that God wholly dwells in Jesus (i.e., the whole essence), then Jesus would be both father and son, making the father a son to himself—a contradiction. Third, if the opponent says it is a part of God in Jesus without any diminution of the divine essence, analogous to taking a flame from a lamp, then the author objects that the part taken from a lamp is a new creation (it comes into existence when taken), so it is created, not eternal. If it is claimed that the part is transferred from God like a flame, then the same objection applies: the transferred part is created, and a created thing cannot be an eternal son. Fourth, if the opponent relies on observation that the part taken from a lamp does not diminish the original, the author replies that either God creates a new flame or it emerges like fire from stone—in either case it is created. Since the created is a creature (makhluq), it cannot be a son. This passage systematically dismantles the concept of a divine sonship based on a part of God.

Key Terms
بعض (part)
ابن (son)
قديم (pre-eternal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Jesus is the only part of God to become incarnate as son.
Correction: Al-Maturidi argues that if it is a part, then all parts would logically be sons, or the uniqueness is arbitrary.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi, if Jesus is a part of God and the smallest part, what follows?
A) Only Jesus is son.
B) All parts become sons.
C) God has no parts.
D) Jesus is not a part.

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi shows that the claim of Jesus being a part of God leads to absurd conclusions about uniqueness and age.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that your special relationship with God is unique and not extendable to others? How does Al-Maturidi's argument expose the arrogance of such exclusivity, and what would it mean for you to truly see others as equally capable of nearness to God?
?
When you call God 'Father', do you reflect on the logical implication that you are then younger than Him? How does this challenge any casual or anthropomorphic understanding of God, and what concrete change will you make in how you address Him?
?
If this argument demonstrates that human reasoning about God's attributes can lead to absurdity, how does it confront your own tendency to rely on your intellect over divine revelation? Will you submit to the Qur'an's clear teaching of tawhid, even when it contradicts popular or comfortable beliefs?

The Whole God or a Part? Contradictions in Christian Doctrine
Al-Maturidi refutes the indwelling and part-without-deficiency arguments, concluding that any part taken is created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's refutation of the Christian idea that God wholly indwells Jesus or that a part is taken without deficiency, using the lamp analogy.

Core Idea
If God wholly dwells in Jesus, then Jesus is both father and son. If a part is taken like a flame, it is created.

Having challenged the uniqueness of sonship, al-Maturidi now addresses two more Christian claims: that God entirely dwells in Jesus, or that a part of God is in Jesus without diminution, like taking a flame from a lamp. He demonstrates that both lead to logical contradictions or imply that the part is created, not eternal. This lesson explores these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Consider the indwelling claim
Read the part where the opponent claims God wholly dwells in Jesus. Notice the question: which thing of him is the son? If the whole, then both son and father.
2
Analyze the 'whole God in Jesus' argument
Understand that if Jesus is entirely God, then he is both father and son, making the father a son to himself — a contradiction.
3
Examine the lamp analogy
Look at the Christian analogy of taking a part from a lamp without diminishing it. Al-Maturidi objects that the part taken is a new creation (hadith), so it cannot be pre-eternal.
4
Understand the response to observation
The Christian cites observation that the lamp flame is not diminished. Al-Maturidi counters that either God creates a new flame or it emerges like fire from stone — in both cases it is created.
Thus the part taken is created, not eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: So it is said to them: Since the spirit that is in him is ancient, and it is a part, how did it become a son while no other of the parts reached [him]? If it is said: Because it is the smallest ... then he would have to make every part of the world sons for the greatest of them, and he would have to make every part of the rest likewise, so that in their entirety they become sons. Then it is known that the son is younger than the father; how can both be ancient? And if he makes the whole [place] in the body, let it be said to him: Which thing of him is the son? If he says: The whole, then he makes the whole both son and father, and in that he makes the father a son to himself. And if he claims it is transferred from God like that taken from a lamp, [then] what preceded applies. Then, indeed, he knows that what is taken from the lamp is not diminished? If it is said: Our observation shows it is so, it is said: Perhaps God created it, or it is like fire in stone that comes out, and whichever it is, it is created, and the created is made, so how can it be a son?

This passage is a logical refutation of the Christian doctrine that Jesus is the Son of God as a part of God. The author presents a series of arguments against the notion that Jesus has an ancient divine spirit that is a part of God. ?

Commit to scrutinizing them using the Qur'an and reason.

What if you secretly believe that some 'part' of your good deeds or spirituality is independent of God? Consider that every good is created by Him alone. Repent from attributing any power to yourself.
