Refutation of Tashbih: Human Acts vs Divine Acts
Maturidi presents and refutes the tashbih objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection of tashbih is invalid because human actions are inherently defective and divine actions are perfect, and that similarity in the mere fact of origination does not imply similarity in essence.

Core Idea
Human acts are defective; divine acts are perfect – different essence, no tashbih.

In this passage, Maturidi responds to the claim that calling human actions 'created' makes them similar to God's creation. He first refutes by pointing out the inherent defects in human actions compared to God's perfect actions. Then he explains that even the common aspect of origination does not justify tashbih because the context differs, just as knowledge and power are not similar despite both being attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the two parts: the opponent's citation and the response.
2
Identify the two refutations
The response has two parts. First, the qualitative difference between human and divine acts. Second, the argument that origination itself is not a basis for similarity because the aspect differs.
3
Understand the analogy with knowledge and power
Maturidi uses the example of 'knowing' and 'powerful' – these terms are not considered similar despite being shared between humans and God, due to difference in meaning. This illustrates his point about origination.
This is a key logical move: shared terms do not imply shared reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: واحتجوا أيضا بقوله تعالى: (أم جعلوا لله شركاء خلقوا كخلقه) [الرعد:16]، إنه على قولكم تشبيه فعلكم خلقه، فقال: معاذ الله، بل فعلنا عبث وفساد وخضوع وذلة، وفعله حكمة وصواب وتفضل وتطول. قال: وليس من حيث الحدث، والحدث والخروج من العدم تشابه لاختلاف الجهة، كما لم يكن في عالم وقادر لاختلاف المعنى. قال: وبعد، فإن فعلنا يخالف فعل الله لعينه، ثم الإيجاد والحدث معنى يوجب التشابه، وإنما يجوز ذلك في الأعيان بما يحل فيها، مع ما يعارض بقول جهم حيث قال: في تحقيق الفعل تشابه. ثم قال: العجب من إلزامهم التسمية بالإحداث، ولم يلزموا أنفسهم فيما فعلوا فعل ربهم في الحقيقة. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: نقول وبالله التوفيق: أثبت الله تعالى التشابه من حيث الفعل حيث قال: (خلقوا كخلقه فتشابه الخلق عليهم) [الرعد:16]، نفى أن يكون من أحد خلق كخلقه، وأوجب لهم العذر في عبادتهم ما كانوا يعبدون لو كان منهم خلق كخلقه. ثم لا سبيل إلى معاينة كيفية الإنشاء وإنما يعلم بالمنشأ، إنه في حق الكسب في حق الخروج من العدم إلى الوجود والحدث من لا أصل، أو والتحرك والسكون، فمن حقق للعبد من الفعل من الوجه الذي يحقق من الله فقد وجد خلق كخلقه؛ إذ لا وجه لفعله غير ذلك، ولو كان بالذي يذكره دفع لكان لا وجه للاحتجاج؛ لأنهم لو أثبتوا يقولون ليس ذلك كذلك؛ لأن الذي منكم كان بفلاح، وهذا النوع من الخيال، ثم اتباع ذلك الحرف ودفع إمكان حقيقته بقوله: (خالق كل شيء) [الأنعام:102] ليعلم كل أنه أي شيء أضافه إلى أحد أنه خلقه لم يقدروا عليه لوجود ضرورات له فيه توجب تدبير غيره في ذلك، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد قال الله عز وجل: (إذا لذهب كل إله بما خلق) [المؤمنون:91]، وإذا جعلت لكل عند المعنى الذي به وصف الله تعالى بالخلق، ويذهب كل بالذي فكان في ذلك تثبيت آلهة، ذهب كل بما خلق، ولا قوة إلا بالله. مع الله في الخلق سوى الوجود، وذلك بعينه قد يوجد، فأى ما ليس من معنى بقى مما به تمام التشابه، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم من قول المسلمين في نفي تشبيه الخلق عن الله لأنه من الوجه الذي يقع فيه تشابه يوجب حدثه بحدث الآخر، فلو لم يقع من حيث الحدث تشابه لم يكن ينفى من حيث لزوم الحدث، مع ما كانت الحوادث في الأجسام أدلة حدثها، وحدث الأجسام هو دلالة المحدث الصانع، وذلك كله آية التشابه. فقوله: لا يقع بذا تشابه، لا معنى له، إذا؛ فإن خلق الشيء عندهم هو الخلق، ولا شك في الخلق ذلة وخضوع وحاجة وعيوب وشيطان وشر وفتنة وبلاء وفساد ونتن وخبث وقذر، كل هذا أوصاف فعل الله تعالى عند المعتزلة بقولهم: خلق.
He said: And they also argued with His saying: (Or have they assigned to Allah partners who created like His creation) [13:16], that according to your statement it is tashbih of your action to His creation. He said: God forbid, rather our action is vanity, corruption, humiliation, and lowliness, and His action is wisdom, rightness, favor, and beneficence. He said: And it is not from the aspect of origination, and origination and coming out of non-existence are similar due to difference of aspect, just as there is no likeness in knowing and powerful due to difference of meaning. He said: And moreover, our action differs from God's action in its very essence; then bringing into existence and origination is a meaning that necessitates similarity, but that is only permissible in entities by what inheres in them, along with what is opposed by the statement of Jahm where he said: In the reality of the action there is similarity. Then he said: The wonder is from their imposition of the name by origination, and they do not impose on themselves in what they do the action of their Lord in reality. The jurist, may God have mercy on him, said: We say, and with God is success: God the Exalted established similarity from the aspect of action where He said: (They created like His creation, so the creation became similar to them) [13:16], He negated that anyone should have creation like His creation, and He obliged for them an excuse in their worship of what they used to worship if there were from them creation like His creation. Then there is no way to witness the how of creation, but it is known by the created thing; it is regarding acquisition (kasb) regarding coming out of non-existence into existence and origination from no source, or motion and stillness. So whoever actualizes for the servant from action in the aspect that is actualized from God, then he has found creation like His creation; for there is no aspect of his action other than that. And if it were as he mentions to repel, then there would be no aspect for the argument; because if they established, they say it is not so; because that which from you was with success, and this type of imagination. Then following that letter and repelling the possibility of its reality by His saying: (Creator of all things) [6:102], so that everyone knows that anything they attribute to anyone that he created it, they cannot do it due to the existence of necessities in it that necessitate management of other than him in that, and there is no power except with Allah. And Allah the Exalted said: (Then each god would have taken away what he created) [23:91], and if you assign to each according to the meaning by which Allah the Exalted is described with creation, and each would take away that which he created, then in that would be the establishment of gods, each took away what he created, and there is no power except with Allah. With Allah in creation besides existence, and that very thing may exist, so what meaning remains other than that by which complete similarity occurs, and there is no power except with Allah. Then from the statement of the Muslims in negating tashbih of creation from Allah because from the aspect in which similarity occurs necessitates its origination by the origination of the other. So if similarity did not occur from the aspect of origination, they would not negate from the aspect of necessity of origination, along with what the events in bodies were proofs of their origination, and the origination of bodies is the indication of the originating Maker, and all of that is a sign of similarity. So his saying: similarity does not occur by that, has no meaning, since; for the creation of a thing according to them is creation, and there is no doubt that creation includes lowliness, submission, need, defects, devil, evil, trial, affliction, corruption, stench, filth, and dirt; all of this are attributes of the act of God the Exalted according to the Mu'tazila by their saying: created.
Source pages p. 847

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the tashbih objection. The author first presents the opponents' argument that calling human actions 'created' makes them similar to God's creation, citing the verse 'خلقوا كخلقه'. The response is that human actions are characterized by defect (عبث وفساد) while divine actions are perfect. The author then clarifies that similarity in the mere fact of 'origination' does not imply tashbih because the aspect differs, just as knowledge and power do not imply similarity. He also critiques the Jahmiyya for claiming inherent similarity. The key proof is from the verse 'خلقوا كخلقه فتشابه الخلق عليهم', which shows that if human actions were truly like God's creation, the worship of other gods would be excusable. The passage then discusses the nature of acquisition (كسب) and the impossibility of witnessing the 'how' of creation. It concludes that all created things bear signs of deficiency, which are wrongly attributed to God by the Mu'tazila when they say God creates those defects.

Key Terms
خَلَقُوا
كخلقه
تشبيه
حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If human actions are described as 'created', then humans must be creators like God, leading to tashbih.
Correction: Human actions are not like God's actions because human actions are inherently defective and perfect, while God's actions are perfect. Moreover, the mere fact of origination does not imply similarity due to differing aspects.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why doesn't the fact that both human and divine acts involve 'origination' lead to tashbih?
A) Because origination is not a real concept
B) Because the aspects differ
C) Because humans do not actually originate anything
D) Because God's origination is eternal

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes tashbih by showing human acts are defective and that shared origination does not imply similarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself comparing your own actions to God's actions, perhaps thinking that your good deeds are similar to His? How does Maturidi's distinction between human acts (defective) and divine acts (perfect) challenge your sense of self-righteousness? Commit to reminding yourself that your best acts are still tainted by deficiency, and only God's acts are truly wise and perfect.
?
When you read the Quranic verse (13:16) about partners creating like God, do you ever try to claim any share in God's creative power through your own abilities? Reflect on the vanity of your actions – how do they become 'corruption' when you rely on them? What is one area of your life where you need to stop attributing success to yourself and acknowledge God alone as the source of all good?
?
Maturidi argues that shared terminology (like 'knowing' or 'originating') does not mean shared reality. Have you ever been guilty of mistaking the label for the essence? For example, do you think your 'knowledge' of God is like God's knowledge? How does this lesson push you to humble yourself before God's absolute transcendence? Write down one specific way you will cultivate greater awareness of God's incomparability in your daily worship.

The Excuse for Idolaters and the Nature of Action
Maturidi deepens the refutation with the verse about excuse for idolaters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's deeper argument that if human actions were truly like God's creation, idolaters would be excused, and his critique of Jahm's view.

Core Idea
True creation is unique to God; human acts differ in essence.

Maturidi continues his refutation by emphasizing that human action differs from God's action in its very essence. He then addresses the objection by invoking the Quranic verse that if humans could create like God, the idolaters would have an excuse. This lesson also touches on Jahm's problematic claim about similarity in action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice three parts: the essence argument, the mention of Jahm, and the verse about excuse.
2
Analyze the essence argument
Maturidi says 'فعلنا يخالف فعل الله لعينه' – our action differs from God's in its very essence. This is a stronger claim than the earlier qualitative difference.
3
Understand the verse as a proof
The verse 13:16 is used to show that if humans could create like God, then the worship of other gods would be excusable. But since it is not excusable, humans cannot create like God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: واحتجوا أيضا بقوله تعالى: (أم جعلوا لله شركاء خلقوا كخلقه) [الرعد:16]، إنه على قولكم تشبيه فعلكم خلقه، فقال: معاذ الله، بل فعلنا عبث وفساد وخضوع وذلة، وفعله حكمة وصواب وتفضل وتطول. قال: وليس من حيث الحدث، والحدث والخروج من العدم تشابه لاختلاف الجهة، كما لم يكن في عالم وقادر لاختلاف المعنى. قال: وبعد، فإن فعلنا يخالف فعل الله لعينه، ثم الإيجاد والحدث معنى يوجب التشابه، وإنما يجوز ذلك في الأعيان بما يحل فيها، مع ما يعارض بقول جهم حيث قال: في تحقيق الفعل تشابه. ثم قال: العجب من إلزامهم التسمية بالإحداث، ولم يلزموا أنفسهم فيما فعلوا فعل ربهم في الحقيقة. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: نقول وبالله التوفيق: أثبت الله تعالى التشابه من حيث الفعل حيث قال: (خلقوا كخلقه فتشابه الخلق عليهم) [الرعد:16]، نفى أن يكون من أحد خلق كخلقه، وأوجب لهم العذر في عبادتهم ما كانوا يعبدون لو كان منهم خلق كخلقه. ثم Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'خلقوا كخلقه' proves that human actions are similar to God's creation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the very same verse is used to negate that anyone can create like God, and it implies that if they could, idolaters would be excused – which is false.

According to Maturidi, what would be the consequence if humans could truly create like God?
A) Humans would be gods
B) Idolaters would have an excuse for their worship
C) Tashbih would be proven

?

?

?

Divine Transcendence
advanced

Kasb, Polytheism, and the Deficiency of Creation
Maturidi discusses kasb and concludes that creation implies deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that human acts are kasb (acquisition) not true creation, that true creation would imply polytheism, and that all creation involves deficiency which cannot be attributed to God.

Core Idea
Human acts are not creation; true creation is unique and implies deficiency.

In this final segment, Maturidi explains that human action is acquisition (kasb), not creation, and that the 'how' of creation is unknowable. He then invokes Quran 23:91 to show that if multiple creators existed, each would take its creation, leading to polytheism. Finally, he notes that creation involves lowliness and deficiency, which opponents wrongly attribute to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read carefully, noting the key concepts: kasb, the verse about each god taking its creation, and the list of deficiencies.
The translation may have unclear phrases due to OCR.
2
Understand the concept of kasb
Maturidi says there is no way to witness the how of creation; it is known by the created thing. Human action is acquisition (kasb), not creation in the true sense.
3
Analyze the argument from Quran 23:91
If there were multiple gods, each would take away what he created. This implies that if humans could create like God, they would be like gods, but this is impossible.
4
Reflect on the nature of creation
Maturidi concludes that creation involves lowliness, defects, evil, etc. These are attributes of God's act according to the Mu'tazila, but Muslims reject that God creates evil in that sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يوجد، فأى ما ليس من معنى بقى مما به تمام التشابه، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم من قول المسلمين في نفي تشبيه الخلق عن الله لأنه من الوجه الذي يقع فيه تشابه يوجب حدثه بحدث الآخر، فلو لم يقع من حيث الحدث تشابه لم يكن ينفى من حيث لزوم الحدث، مع ما كانت الحوادث في الأجسام أدلة حدثها، وحدث الأجسام هو دلالة المحدث الصانع، وذلك كله آية التشابه. فقوله: لا يقع بذا تشابه، لا معنى له، إذا؛ فإن خلق الشيء عندهم هو الخلق، ولا شك في الخلق ذلة وخضوع وحاجة وعيوب وشيطان وشر وفتنة وبلاء وفساد ونتن وخبث وقذر، كل هذا أوصاف فعل الله تعالى عند المعتزلة بقولهم: خلق.
He said: And they also argued with His saying: (Or have they assigned to Allah partners who created like His creation) [13:16], that according to your statement it is tashbih of your action to His creation. He said: God forbid, rather our action is vanity, corruption, humiliation, and lowliness, and His action is wisdom, rightness, favor, and beneficence. He said: And it is not from the aspect of origination, and origination and coming out of non-existence are similar due to difference of aspect, just as there is no likeness in knowing and powerful due to difference of meaning. He said: And moreover, our action differs from God's action in its very essence; then bringing into existence and origination is a meaning that necessitates similarity, but that is only permissible in entities by what inheres in them, along with what is opposed by the statement of Jahm where he said: In the reality of the action there is similarity. Then he said: The wonder is from their imposition of the name by origination, and they do not impose on themselves in what they do the action of their Lord in reality. The jurist, may God have mercy on him, said: We say, and with God is success: God the Exalted established similarity from the aspect of action where He said: (They created like His creation, so the creation became similar to them) [13:16], He negated that anyone should have creation like His creation, and He obliged for them an excuse in their worship of what they used to worship if there were from them creation like His creation. Then there is no way to witness the how of creation, but it is known by the created thing; it is regarding acquisition (kasb) regarding coming out of non-existence into existence and origination from no source, or motion and stillness. So whoever actualizes for the servant from action in the aspect that is actualized from God, then he has found creation like His creation; for there is no aspect of his action other than that. And if it were as he mentions to repel, then there would be no aspect for the argument; because if they established, they say it is not so; because that which from you was with success, and this type of imagination. Then following that letter and repelling the possibility of its reality by His saying: (Creator of all things) [6:102], so that everyone knows that anything they attribute to anyone that he created it, they cannot do it due to the existence of necessities in it that necessitate management of other than him in that, and there is no power except with Allah. And Allah the Exalted said: (Then each god would have taken away what he created) [23:91], and if you assign to each according to the meaning by which Allah the Exalted is described with creation, and each would take away that which he created, then in that would be the establishment of gods, each took away what he created, and there is no power except with Allah. With Allah in creation besides existence, and that very thing may exist, so what meaning remains other than that by which complete similarity occurs, and there is no power except with Allah. Then from the statement of the Muslims in negating tashbih of creation from Allah because from the aspect in which similarity occurs necessitates its origination by the origination of the other. So if similarity did not occur from the aspect of origination, they would not negate from the aspect of necessity of origination, along with what the events in bodies were proofs of their origination, and the origination of bodies is the indication of the originating Maker, and all of that is a sign of similarity. So his saying: similarity does not occur by that, has no meaning, since; for the creation of a thing according to them is creation, and there is no doubt that creation includes lowliness, submission, need, defects, devil, evil, trial, affliction, corruption, stench, filth, and dirt; all of this are attributes of the act of God the Exalted according to the Mu'tazila by their saying: created.
Source pages p. 847

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the tashbih objection. The author first presents the opponents' argument that calling human actions 'created' makes them similar to God's creation, citing the verse 'خلقوا كخلقه'. The response is that human actions are characterized by defect (عبث وفساد) while divine actions are perfect. The author then clarifies that similarity in the mere fact of 'origination' does not imply tashbih because the aspect differs, just as knowledge and power do not imply similarity. He also critiques the Jahmiyya for claiming inherent similarity. The key proof is from the verse 'خلقوا كخلقه فتشابه الخلق عليهم', which shows that if human actions were truly like God's creation, the worship of other gods would be excusable. The passage then discusses the nature of acquisition (كسب) and the impossibility of witnessing the 'how' of creation. It concludes that all created things bear signs of deficiency, which are wrongly attributed to God by the Mu'tazila when they say God creates those defects.

Key Terms
كسب
خالق
آلهة
حادث
ذلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human actions are created in the same sense as God's creation, only lesser in degree.
Correction: Human actions are kasb, not true creation; they are fundamentally different in kind, and true creation is unique to God.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi use Quran 23:91 to prove?
A) That God is the only creator
B) That if humans could create like God, polytheism would be implied
C) That creation involves deficiency
D) That Jahm was wrong

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that human acts are not true creation; true creation would lead to polytheism and involves deficiency, which cannot be attributed to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you see your daily actions – your work, your worship, your relationships – as your own creation or as something Allah creates and you acquire? If they are acquired, how would that change your sense of pride or despair over them?
?
The passage says creation includes lowliness, need, evil, and corruption. Are you comfortable acknowledging that your own failures and suffering are part of what Allah creates? Or do you instinctively blame yourself or others, as if you were the independent creator of your own life?
?
Maturidi argues that claiming creation for yourself leads to polytheism. In what areas of your life do you subtly act as your own creator – deciding outcomes, taking full credit, or resenting when things don't go your way? What would it take for you to truly internalize that only Allah creates?

The Rational Necessity of Negating Tashbih
Maturidi explains the rational basis for negating tashbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that negating resemblance (tashbih) between God and creation is rationally necessary to avoid implying God's origination.

Core Idea
Resemblance to creation would make God contingent.

Maturidi begins by establishing the Muslim consensus on negating anthropomorphism. He provides a rational argument: if God resembled creation, He would be subject to the same origination as created things, which contradicts His eternity. This lesson unpacks that logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi presents the Muslim position and then gives the logical consequence of resemblance.
The passage is dense; take time to see the cause-and-effect structure.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the sentence where Maturidi states that resemblance would necessitate God's origination by another's origination (يوجب حدثه بحدث الآخر).
3
Understand the proof from bodies
Maturidi uses the originated things in bodies as evidence for their own origination, which points to a Maker. See how this connects to the negation of resemblance.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this argument firmly establishes that God cannot be compared to creation in any way that implies contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من قول المسلمين في نفي تشبيه الخلق عن الله لأنه من الوجه الذي يقع فيه تشابه يوجب حدثه بحدث الآخر، فلو لم يقع من حيث الحدث تشابه لم يكن ينفى من حيث لزوم الحدث، مع ما كانت الحوادث في الأجسام من أدلة حدثها، وحدث الأجسام هو دلالة المحدث الصانع، وذلك كله آية التشابه، فقوله : لا يقع بذا تشابه، لا معنى له، إذا؛ فإن خلق الشيء عندهم هو الخلق، ولا شك في الخلق ذلة وخضوع وحاجة وعيوب وشيطان وشر وفتنة وبلاء وفساد ونتن وخبث وقذر، كل هذا أوصاف فعل الله تعالى عند المعتزلة بقولهم : خلق الشيء هو خلق ذلك الشيء، فكيف أنكر هذه الأحوال عن خلقه، وقوله هو تطول وتنقل، فإذا إبليس هو خلقه عندهم، وفعله في الحقيقة تطول وتفضل، وهو خير يحسن، وهو حكمة وصواب، وهذا كله قول وحش، لم يجز إطلاق ذلك بصلات توضح المراد، فمثله الذي ذكر، وليس من هذا الوجه دفع التشابه ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then from the statement of the Muslims in negating the resemblance of creation from Allah, because from the aspect in which resemblance occurs, it necessitates its origination by the origination of the other. So if resemblance did not occur from the aspect of origination, negation would not occur from the aspect of the necessity of origination, while the originated things in bodies are among the proofs of their origination, and the origination of bodies is the evidence of the originating Maker, and all of that is a sign of resemblance. So his saying 'resemblance does not occur by this' has no meaning, because the creation of a thing according to them is the creation, and there is no doubt that in creation there is abasement, submission, need, defects, devil, evil, trial, affliction, corruption, stench, impurity, and filth. All of these are descriptions of the act of Allah the Exalted according to the Mu'tazila by their saying: 'the creation of a thing is the creation of that thing.' So how can he deny these states from His creation? And his saying is [grace?] and [transmission?], for Iblis is His creation according to them, and His action in reality is grace and favor, and He is good, doing good, wise, and correct. And all of this is a wild saying; it is not permissible to utter that without connections that clarify the intent. So the like of that which he mentioned is not from this aspect a repulsion of resemblance, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 848

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on creation and a defense of the negation of tashbih (anthropomorphism) from God. Maturidi begins by stating the Muslim consensus that resemblance between God and His creation is negated because resemblance would necessitate that God originates as originated things originate, which implies contingency. He argues that if resemblance did not occur from the aspect of origination, then the negation of resemblance would not be necessary. He then criticizes the Mu'tazila who say that 'the creation of a thing is the creation of that thing' (i.e., creation is identical to the created thing), and from this they attribute to God's creation qualities like abasement, need, defects, evil, etc. Maturidi asks how they can deny these states from God's creation if they claim creation is identical to the thing. He then points out that by this logic, Iblis (Satan) is God's creation, and God's action would then be described as grace and favor, which is absurd. He concludes that this is a wild saying and does not serve to negate resemblance.

Key Terms
تشبيه (tashbih)
حدث (hadath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that negating resemblance means denying that God has any attributes at all.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny attributes; he denies that God's attributes resemble created attributes in a way that implies contingency.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that negating resemblance is necessary?
A) Because the Quran explicitly says so
B) Because resemblance would necessitate God's origination by another's origination
C) Because the Mu'tazila affirmed resemblance
D) Because created things are proofs of their own origination

Key Takeaway
Negating tashbih is a rational necessity to preserve God's eternal nature, as resemblance would imply contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, do you ever unconsciously picture a being with human-like qualities (e.g., seeing, hearing, or acting in a manner similar to yourself)? How does Maturidi's rational argument that resemblance would imply contingency force you to confront that tendency? What specific steps will you take to uproot any mental images that contradict Allah's transcendence?
?
This lesson shows that negating tashbih is not just a theological doctrine but a rational necessity. Think about a time you attributed a human-like limitation to Allah in your own thoughts (e.g., thinking He might 'change His mind' or 'need' our worship). How does the proof from this passage expose the logical flaw in such thinking? What will you do to remind yourself daily of His absolute transcendence?
?
The proof that originated things in bodies lead to evidence of a Maker is clear. Yet, how often do you pause to reflect on the implication that if Allah resembled His creation, He would be subject to the same chain of evidence? If you truly internalized this, how would it change the way you speak about Allah, think about His attributes, or even teach others about tawhid?

Critique of the Mu'tazila on Creation
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite identification of creation with the created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazilite view that creation is identical to the created thing, and how this leads to attributing defects to God.

Core Idea
Identifying creation with the created makes God responsible for evil.

Maturidi now turns to refute a specific Mu'tazilite position. They say 'the creation of a thing is the creation of that thing' – meaning creation is identical to the created object. Maturidi argues this forces them to attribute all the imperfections of creation directly to God, leading to absurdities like Satan being God's act of grace.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note how Maturidi presents the Mu'tazilite claim and then lists the negative qualities that follow from it.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite position
Find the phrase 'خلق الشيء عندهم هو الخلق' – the creation of a thing is the creation. Understand that they make no distinction between the act and the product.
3
Examine the list of qualities
Maturidi lists abasement, need, defects, evil, etc. See why these would be ascribed to God if creation equals the created.
4
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Maturidi points out that by this logic, Iblis is God's creation, yet the Mu'tazila claim God's action is grace and favor – a contradiction.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi dismisses this as a wild saying that does not properly negate resemblance.

Source Text

Maturidi begins by stating the Muslim consensus that resemblance between God and His creation is negated because resemblance would necessitate that God originates as originated things originate, which implies contingency. ?

?

?
Maturidi called the Mu'tazilite position a 'wild saying.' Are there any comfortable statements about God you have made that, under scrutiny, might be equally wild? Commit to testing your own words about God against the revelation and sound reason from now on.

The Wisdom Behind Evils and Diseases in Maturidi's Kalam
Maturidi refutes the objection that God's creation of evils implies He also creates disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Maturidi refutes the objection that God's creation of evils and diseases implies He also creates disbelief, by distinguishing between relative evil and divine wisdom.

Core Idea
Evils and diseases have wise purposes; disbelief does not share that wisdom.

In this dense passage, al-Maturidi confronts a logical objection against divine agency: if God creates harmful things like diseases and evils, why not also create disbelief? He first notes that this question only arises in opposition, then answers by showing that these things are not truly evil in the divine scheme—they serve as mercy through repentance and deterrence. Disbelief, however, cannot be justified in the same way.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal English translation. Pay attention to the flow of objection and response.
The Arabic contains technical terms like الشرور and الحكمة.
2
Identify the objection and the jurist's remark
Locate where the objection is introduced ('ثم احتج لهم') and where the jurist comments that the question is only asked in opposition. Understand why this remark is important.
3
Analyze the answer: relative evil vs. absolute wisdom
Al-Maturidi answers that evils and diseases are not evil in divine wisdom; they are mercy because they lead to repentance and deter sin. Contrast this with disbelief, which has no such wisdom except in a limited sense.
Notice he uses 'رحمة' (mercy) for the creation of evils.
4
Reflect on the key distinction
Consider why the author insists on differentiating between created 'evils' and disbelief. How does this preserve God's justice and wisdom?
This distinction is central to the Maturidi defense of divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج لهم بخلق الشرور والأسقام ، وإن كانت ضارة لم يقل في الكفر ؟ قال الفقيه : وهذا سؤال لا يسأله أحد على الابتداء إلا على المعارضة ، إذ لم يسم خالق هذه الأشياء بها وجب أن بخلقه أفعال الخلق بأسمائها ، يسمي وهذا نوع ما ليس لغيره فعل في الحقيقة ، وفي أفعال الشرور ذلك . فأجاب هذه الأشياء ليست بشرور في الحكمة ، بل هي رحمة بذكر التوبة وبزجر عن المعصية ، والكفر ليس بحكمة إلا بوجه ، ترى أنه لا يجوز خلقه لا عن أحد ويجوز في الأول .
Then they argued by the creation of evils and diseases, although they are harmful, why did He not say about disbelief? The jurist said: This question is not asked by anyone initially except in objection, because He did not name the Creator of these things as such, it is necessary that by His creation of the actions of creation by their names, He names, and this is a type for which no other has action in reality, and in actions of evils that. So he answered: These things are not evil in divine wisdom, rather they are mercy by mentioning repentance and deterrence from sin, and disbelief is not wisdom except in one aspect, you see that it is not permissible to create it from no one, and it is permissible in the first.
Source pages p. 851

Commentary
This passage presents an objection: if God creates evils and diseases, why not also create disbelief? The author responds that this question is raised only in rebuttal. The answer distinguishes between relative evil and absolute wisdom: evils and diseases are not truly evil in the divine plan; they serve purposes like inducing repentance and deterring sin. Disbelief, however, is not wisdom except in a certain way (perhaps as a test). The author implies that God's creation of evils is justifiable, but disbelief is not created from nothing without reason.

Key Terms
الشرور
الأسقام
الكفر
الحكمة
التوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that God's creation of evils and diseases makes Him unjust or that it justifies attributing evil actions to Him.
Correction: Al-Maturidi argues that these things are not truly evil in the divine plan; they serve wise purposes like leading to repentance and deterring sin. Disbelief is not comparable because it does not share that wisdom.

Checkpoint
Why are evils and diseases not considered truly evil according to al-Maturidi?
A) Because they are created by God
B) Because they are beneficial in some way
C) Because they are not real
D) Because they lead to repentance and deter sin

Key Takeaway
Evils and diseases are not truly evil in divine wisdom; they serve as means of mercy, unlike disbelief which lacks such wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a serious illness or loss, do you instinctively recognize it as a mercy from Allah meant to turn you back to Him? Think of a specific hardship you've endured—did it lead you to repent or draw closer to Allah? If not, what does that reveal about your heart? Will you commit to viewing your next trial as a divine invitation, not a punishment?
?
The lesson says disbelief is not wisdom except in one aspect. Have you ever excused your own disbelief or sin by convincing yourself there might be some benefit in it? In this very moment, are you harboring any doubt that you rationalize as 'seeing wisdom'? Confess it to yourself, and ask Allah to replace that with true clarity.
?
If you truly believed that every evil and disease is a mercy from Allah, how would your reaction to suffering change? Would you still complain, or would you fall to your knees in gratitude? What is stopping you from embracing this truth today? Make a concrete plan to respond to the next difficulty with repentance and trust.

Reason Distinguishes, Intuition Does Not
Maturidi argues against intuition and for reason's role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that intellects are composed to distinguish and unite meanings, and that intuitive reason is not a valid basis for knowledge.

Core Idea
Intellects are designed to distinguish and unite, not to intuit.

Maturidi opens by questioning whether realities are known by simple intuition. He argues that intellects are structured to differentiate between things based on their meanings and to unite similar ones. This sets the stage for rejecting claims made on the basis of unaided intuition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the first part of the Arabic passage carefully, noting how Maturidi challenges the notion of intuition.
Focus on the phrase 'بداهة العقول'.
2
Understand the function of intellect
Maturidi describes intellects as 'مركبة مميزة بين مختلف الأشياء بمعانيها' and 'مؤلفة بين مجتمعها'. Grasp these two functions.
Intellects both distinguish and unite.
3
Connect to knowledge and power
The passage then states that one cannot claim knowledge or power without capacity. This reinforces that knowledge is based on proper reasoning.
4
Reflect on the rejection of intuition
Maturidi dismisses the claim of 'بديهة العقل' as merely natural temperament. Consider why he finds this insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومتى تدرك حقائق الأشياء ببداهة العقول ، وإنما العقول ركبت مميزة بين مختلف الأشياء بمعانيها التي توجب الاختلاف ومؤلفة بين مجتمعها بمعانى توجب الجمع ، وذا حق الفكر والنظر ليوصل بهما إلى ذلك ثم لا أحد عالما بشيء لا و لا علم له به ، قادرا على / شيء لا قدرة عليه ، بل كل معروف ينفى القدرة والعلم موصوف بالجهل والعجز إذا احتمل الوصف بالقدرة والعلم إنه عالم قادر ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وإن رجع إلى اعتبار المعانى التى هى أسباب حقائق الأشياء فذلك له مسلم ، ولا معنى لقوله ببديهة العقل ، إنما ذلك حق الطباع ونفاره . ثم ينكر أن يكون في خلق الله قبيحا فى الحقيقة وشرا وفسادا ، على وجود ما لا يحصى من ذلك على هذه الأوصاف بالعقول ، بل الله جل ثناؤه بما جعلها كذلك صرف بها مثل ما قبح من الأفعال وتقطيع منظره أوعد به ذو عقل اين الذي ينكر مثل هذا من دعوى بداية العقول . ثم لم يزل أئمتهم كلموا الثنوية بجواز كون الخير والشر والطيب والخبيث من واحد ليدفعوا به القول بالاثنين ، ثم رجعوا إلى إحالة أحد الوجهين الله ، فمن حق الوجه الآخر فى الموجود فى العالم أوجب ما قالت الثنوية ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And when are realities of things perceived by intuition of intellects (badahat al-uqul)? Rather, intellects are composed as distinguishers between different things by their meanings that necessitate difference, and as unifiers between similar things by meanings that necessitate union. And that is the right of thought and reflection to reach that. Then no one is knowing of something he has no knowledge of, nor powerful over something he has no power over. Rather, every known that negates power and knowledge is described with ignorance and incapacity, if it admits the description of power and knowledge: that He is knowing, powerful. And there is no power except with God. And if he returns to considering the meanings that are causes of realities of things, that is conceded to him. And there is no meaning to his statement by intuition of reason (badahat al-aql); rather, that is the right of natures and their aversion. Then he denies that there is in God's creation anything ugly (qabih) in reality, or evil (sharr) or corruption (fasad), despite the existence of countless instances of that with these descriptions according to intellects. Rather, God, exalted be His mention, by making them thus, turned away by them the like of ugly actions and the cutting of his sight [؟] threatened with it anyone of intellect. Where is the one who denies such a thing from the claim of the beginning of intellects? Then their imams continued to speak to the Dualists (al-thanawiyya) about the permissibility of good and evil, pleasant and repugnant coming from one [source] to repel by that the claim of two [principles]. Then they returned to deeming impossible one of the two aspects from God, so from the right of the other aspect in what exists in the world necessitated what the Dualists said. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 866

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the denial of real ugliness in creation and an argument against the Dualists. Maturidi first argues that realities are not known by intuition but by reason operating through distinguishing and uniting meanings. He then asserts that it is impossible for one to claim knowledge or power where none exists. He then addresses an opponent who rejects the validity of intuitive reason, stating that if they instead consider meanings as causes, that is acceptable, but the claim of 'intuition of reason' is baseless—it is merely natural temperament and aversion. He then attacks the denial of ugliness (qabih), evil (sharr), and corruption (fasad) in creation, pointing out that intellects clearly perceive countless such instances. God's making things so does not negate their ugliness; rather, it serves as a warning. Finally, Maturidi turns to the Dualists: he notes that even the opponents (probably Mu'tazila) argued against Dualists by allowing good and evil from one source, yet they later deemed one of the two aspects (presumably God's creating evil) impossible, thus inadvertently conceding the Dualist position that good and evil must come from two separate principles.

Key Terms
بداهة العقول (badahat al-uqul)
العقول (al-uqul)
المعاني (al-ma'ani)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi rejects reason in favor of intuition.
Correction: He rejects intuition and affirms reason's role in distinguishing meanings.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about intellects?
A) They are intuitive and require no effort.
B) They are composed to distinguish and unite meanings.
C) They are useless for knowing realities.
D) They only function through divine inspiration.

Key Takeaway
Intellects are tools for distinguishing and uniting meanings, not for intuitive knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely more on your gut feelings (intuition) than on careful, reasoned reflection when thinking about Allah's attributes? Identify one specific area where you have substituted intuition for deep thought, and commit to spending time today in thoughtful reflection on that topic.
?
Maturidi says intellects are made to distinguish. Where in your life have you confused two similar yet distinct truths about Allah because you followed a comfortable intuition instead of making a careful distinction? Write down one distinction you need to make more clearly, and take a concrete step to study it.
?
The passage ends by linking knowledge and power to capacity. How does your pride in your own intellect claim a capacity you do not have? Will you humble yourself and acknowledge that true knowledge comes only through proper reflection on revelation, not from your own innate intuition? What will you change in your daily study routine?

The Reality of Ugliness and Evil in Creation
Maturidi refutes the denial of real ugliness and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that ugliness, evil, and corruption are real in creation, as perceived by intellects, and that God's making them so does not negate their reality.

Core Idea
Evil and ugliness are real, and intellects perceive them.

Having dismissed the reliance on intuition, Maturidi now confronts a position that denies any inherent ugliness or evil in God's creation. He points out that countless things are recognized as ugly, evil, or corrupt by intellects. God's act of bringing these into existence does not make them not ugly; rather, they serve as warnings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the section beginning with 'ثم ينكر أن يكون في خلق الله قبيحا' and note the denial.
The opponent denies real ugliness.
2
Identify the evidence
Maturidi points to the existence of countless things described as ugly or evil, perceived by intellects.
He uses 'على وجود ما لا يحصى من ذلك على هذه الأوصاف بالعقول'.
3
Understand God's role
Maturidi says God made them as such, and in doing so, He turned away similar ugly actions and threatened with them.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
The passage ends with a challenge: 'Where is the one who denies such a thing from the claim of the beginning of intellects?'
This underscores the self-evidence of the reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A) Through revealed scripture
B) They perceive countless instances of them
C) They infer from the absence of good
D) They rely on intuition

Ugliness and evil are real and universally recognized by intellects, and God's creation of them does not negate their reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If evil and ugliness are objectively real, as this lesson insists, then you cannot simply explain them away as illusions. Think of the most painful evil you have witnessed or experienced. Have you ever tried to deny its reality to protect your faith? What would it look like to truly acknowledge it as evil and yet still trust in God’s wisdom? Will you today commit to honesty before God about the evil you see, rather than rationalizing it?
?
The passage says God uses ugliness and evil as a threat to turn people away from similar actions. When you encounter something ugly—whether in the world or in yourself—do you feel that warning? Or have you become numb? What is one specific ugly habit or sin in your life that you have stopped seeing as truly ugly? Ask God to restore your sensitivity to it, and decide today to turn from it.
?
Maturidi challenges those who deny the reality of evil by pointing to universal intellectual recognition. Is there an area of your life where you are intellectually dishonest—where you know something is evil but you refuse to call it that because it would demand change? What would it cost you to stop the denial? Are you willing to pay that price today?

Refuting Dualism: The Inconsistency of Opponents
Maturidi refutes dualism by exposing opponents' inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of those who argued against dualism but later conceded its logic by deeming one source of evil impossible.

Core Idea
Those who deny evil from God inadvertently support dualism.

Maturidi concludes his argument by addressing the Dualists (al-thanawiyya) who claim good and evil come from two distinct principles. He points out that even the opponents of dualism (likely the Mu'tazila) initially argued that both good and evil can come from one source, but later they deemed it impossible for God to create evil, thereby conceding the Dualist position that evil requires a separate source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the final section starting with 'ثم لم يزل أئمتهم كلموا الثنوية' and identify the two stages of argument.
2
Understand the opponents' initial move
Maturidi says the imams (of the opponents) spoke to Dualists, permitting good and evil from one source to deny dualism.
This is a concession.
3
Notice their later reversal
They then deemed one of the two aspects (presumably God creating evil) impossible, thus necessitating the Dualist conclusion.
This inconsistency undermines their position.
4
Reflect on the implication
By rejecting the possibility of evil from God, they inadvertently affirm the Dualist claim that evil must come from another source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Denying that evil can come from God ultimately supports the dualist idea of two separate sources.

?

?
If you were to live today with the conviction that both good and evil come from God alone, what would change in the way you respond to hardship? Is there a specific hardship you are facing right now that you need to surrender to His sole sovereignty?
?
Reflect on a time you found it easier to blame a person or circumstance rather than see God's hand. How does this passage call you to a deeper trust in God's wisdom, even when you cannot understand why evil exists? What step can you take this week to practice that trust?

First Argument: Sovereignty Constraint
Maturidi's first argument: al-aslah implies constraints on divine sovereignty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first argument against al-aslah: that if God's sovereignty is not absolute, He would fail to prevent disobedience, which is less than misguidance, so the principle is invalid.

Core Idea
Absolute sovereignty means God's actions are not constrained by any duty to create the best.

Maturidi begins by refuting the doctrine that God must do what is best for creatures (al-aslah). He argues that if God's sovereignty were limited by such a duty, He would be compelled to prevent all disobedience, but that would be less severe than misguidance, leading to contradiction. This first argument establishes that al-aslah cannot be a necessary attribute of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a premise followed by a conclusion.
The ellipsis indicates an uncertain phrase in the manuscript.
2
Identify the key premise
The passage states: 'if Allah did not have His kingdom as He has it, He would not prevent whoever prevents from obedience.' This means God's absolute sovereignty is assumed.
3
Understand the logical consequence
If God were required to do the best, He would prevent disobedience, but that would be 'less than misguidance'—a contradiction. Therefore, the principle is false.
The conclusion is 'the saying about the best is false and invalid.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ووجه الاعتبار به ليس على ما قدر، ولكن بما يقول بالأصلح، ومعلوم أن الله لو لم يكن ملكه على ما ملكه لم يكن ويمنع من يمنعه عن طاعة رسوله، وكان ذلك أقل من الضلالة. . . فثبت أن القول بالأصلح باطل مضمحل. والثانى أنه إذا أخبر أنه لا يؤمن بالله، وقد علم ذلك وهو عدوه، وإقدار العدو على تسفيه المقدر وتقويته على نقض ملكه وإبطاله ربوبيته خارج عن حد الحكمة ببديهة العقل، مع ما فيه تمكين عدوه الأعظم مينة له عليه أن يقول لي عليك كل منة، أو ملكنى نقض ربوبتك، بما لا يكون ربا جاهلا، وقويتي على إزالة حكمتك، بما لا يكون حكيم كذوب، وقد جعلت لى القدرة على ذلك، وبذلك أمرتني، وقد تعلم أنى لو شئت لفعلت فتمت لك الربوبية وسلمت لك الحكمة، فمينتي عليك أعظم ونعمنى لديك أعم، فبأى نعمة لك تعاقبني، وبأى حكمة تأمرنى، وبي تمت لك ؟، ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثالث أن طريق معرفة فساد القول باثنين ليس إلا قدرة أحدها على ما لا يعلمه الآخر، وفى ذلك إيجاب ذلك، ولو جاز ذا من غير أن يكون في ذلك فساد الألوهية لبطل قول الموحدين فيها به أبطلوا قول الثنوية. وقوله : أنه يعلم لو أنه لا آمن كيف يكون، فهذا معنى لا منفعة فيه؛ لأنه علمه بذلك يعلم أنه لا يؤمن أولا، فإن قال : لا، سفهه، وإن قال : نعم، قيل : في ذلك وقعت المطالبة، وقد ذكرت أنه لو آمن لم يخرج من علمه، فكيف لم يخرج ؟ وعلمه أنه لا يكون، وكان، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأما قوله : لو لم يقدر عليه لم يكن ملوما، فهو مثل القول سواء؛ ودليله أنه قوله، بل عليه أعظم اللائمة؛ هو صنع القدرة حيث أعرض عن الذى به يأتيه. وقوله لا يلزمنا لأن القدرة غير الايمان، يدفع أيضا فما فيه ما يمنع اللزوم، بل إنما لزم ذلك؛ لأن القدرة غير الإيمان. ثم اعتباره بالأمر فاسد؛ لأنه استعباد
Shaykh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The aspect of consideration is not based on what he determined, but on what he says about the best [principle]. It is known that if Allah did not have His kingdom as He has it, He would not prevent whoever prevents from obedience to His messenger, and that would be less than misguidance. So it is established that the saying about the best is false and invalid. Second: that when He informs that he will not believe in Allah, and He knows that and he is His enemy, empowering the enemy to mock the Determiner and strengthen him to nullify His kingdom and invalidate His lordship is beyond the scope of wisdom by the first principle of reason, along with what it contains of enabling His greatest enemy to have a favor over Him, so that he says: I have every favor on you, or you gave me power to nullify your lordship, such that you are not a Lord ignorant, and my strength to remove your wisdom such that you are not a wise liar. And you have given me power over that, and by that you commanded me, and you know that if I wished I would have done it, so your lordship is complete and your wisdom is sound, so my favor on you is greater and my blessing with you is more general. So with which of your blessings do you punish me, and with which wisdom do you command me, and by me you are complete? And there is no power except through Allah. Third: that the way of knowing the corruption of the claim of two [gods] is only the power of one over what the other does not know, and in that is necessitating that. And if this were permissible without that implying corruption of divinity, then the statement of the monotheists by which they refuted the dualists would be invalid. And His statement: He knows that if he did not believe how it would be, this is a meaning with no benefit; because His knowledge of that knows that he will not believe first. If he says: no, he makes him foolish; if he says: yes, it is said: in that lies the demand, and you have mentioned that if he believed he would not leave His knowledge, so how did he not leave? And His knowledge that he will not be, and he was? And there is no power except through Allah. And as for his statement: if he did not have power over it, he would not be blameworthy, it is the same as the saying; and its proof is his statement, rather the greater blame is on him; it is the work of power where he turns away from that by which he comes. And his statement: it does not necessitate us because power is not faith, this is also refuted because in it there is what prevents necessity, rather it is necessary because power is not faith. Then his comparison with the command is corrupt because it is enslavement...
Source pages p. 874

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine of al-aslah (that God must do what is best for creatures). Maturidi presents three arguments. First: if God did not have full control, He would not prevent disobedience, and preventing disobedience would be less than misguidance, so the principle fails. Second: if God informs that someone will not believe and then empowers that enemy, it leads to absurdities where the enemy can claim favor over God, undermining lordship and wisdom. Third: the argument against dualism relies on one god's power over what the other does not know; if this principle is allowed, monotheistic refutation of dualism collapses. Then additional points are made about divine knowledge and blame: knowing what would happen is useless if it does not change outcomes, and the objection about blame is countered by noting that blame stems from turning away from the means of faith. The passage ends by asserting that power is distinct from faith, and that the comparison with divine commands is corrupt.

Key Terms
الأصلح
الملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of Maturidi's first argument against al-aslah?
A) God must do what is best for creatures
B) The principle of al-aslah is false and invalid
C) God's kingdom is limited
D) Disobedience is always prevented

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's first argument shows that al-aslah would constrain God's absolute sovereignty, leading to an absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever complained to God about your circumstances, secretly assuming He owes you something better? How does Maturidi's argument that 'al-aslah' is false expose that assumption as a hidden constraint on His sovereignty? What specific grudge will you release today in light of this truth?
?
If God's sovereignty is absolutely unrestricted, He is under no obligation to make your life easy or fair. Does that truth make you feel relieved or resentful? Why? Take a moment to tell God directly: 'You owe me nothing.'
?
This argument shows that if al-aslah were true, God would be compelled to prevent all disobedience—yet He doesn't. How does that reality make you feel about His patience with your own sins? Will you stop demanding that God 'fix' others' behavior and instead submit to His wisdom?

Second Argument: Empowering the Enemy
Maturidi's second argument: al-aslah leads to an enemy claiming favor over God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument: that if God empowers His enemy (the disbeliever) to act, the enemy could claim favor over God, undermining divine lordship and wisdom.

Core Idea
Empowering an enemy to claim favor over God is absurd and contradicts divine wisdom.

Maturidi's second argument is more elaborate: if God informs that someone will not believe and then gives that person power over obedience, the person could argue that God is indebted to him for completing His lordship. This leads to a contradiction with divine attributes of lordship and wisdom. The argument highlights the blasphemous implications of al-aslah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. This is a longer, more dramatic argument with a hypothetical speech by the disbeliever.
Identify the voice of the disbeliever in the translation.
2
Follow the hypothetical dialogue
The disbeliever claims: 'I have every favor on you... you gave me power to nullify your lordship.' Recognize that this is a reductio ad absurdum.
The passage uses a rhetorical style to show the absurd consequence.
3
Recognize the absurdity
If God empowers the enemy, the enemy could claim that God's lordship and wisdom are dependent on him, which is impossible for God. Therefore, al-aslah leads to blasphemy.
The conclusion is implicit: the argument disproves al-aslah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ووجه الاعتبار به ليس على ما قدر، ولكن بما يقول بالأصلح، ومعلوم أن الله لو لم يكن ملكه على ما ملكه لم يكن ويمنع من يمنعه عن طاعة رسوله، وكان ذلك أقل من الضلالة. . . فثبت أن القول بالأصلح باطل مضمحل. والثانى أنه إذا أخبر أنه لا يؤمن بالله، وقد علم ذلك وهو عدوه، وإقدار العدو على تسفيه المقدر وتقويته على نقض ملكه وإبطاله ربوبيته خارج عن حد الحكمة ببديهة العقل، مع ما فيه تمكين عدوه الأعظم مينة له عليه أن يقول لي عليك كل منة، أو ملكنى نقض ربوبتك، بما لا يكون ربا جاهلا، وقويتي على إزالة حكمتك، بما لا يكون حكيم كذوب، وقد جعلت لى القدرة على ذلك، وبذلك أمرتني، وقد تعلم أنى لو شئت لفعلت فتمت لك الربوبية وسلمت لك الحكمة، فمينتي عليك أعظم ونعمنى لديك أعم، فبأى نعمة لك تعاقبني، وبأى حكمة تأمرنى، وبي تمت لك ؟، ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثالث أن طريق معرفة فساد القول باثنين ليس إلا قدرة أحدها على ما لا يعلمه الآخر، وفى ذلك إيجاب ذلك، ولو جاز ذا من غير أن يكون في ذلك فساد الألوهية لبطل قول الموحدين فيها به أبطلوا قول الثنوية. وقوله : أنه يعلم لو أنه لا آمن كيف يكون، فهذا معنى لا منفعة فيه؛ لأنه علمه بذلك يعلم أنه لا يؤمن أولا، فإن قال : لا، سفهه، وإن قال : نعم، قيل : في ذلك وقعت المطالبة، وقد ذكرت أنه لو آمن لم يخرج من علمه، فكيف لم يخرج ؟ وعلمه أنه لا يكون، وكان، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأما قوله : لو لم يقدر عليه لم يكن ملوما، فهو مثل القول سواء؛ ودليله أنه قوله، بل عليه أعظم اللائمة؛ هو صنع القدرة حيث أعرض عن الذى به يأتيه. وقوله لا يلزمنا لأن القدرة غير الايمان، يدفع أيضا فما فيه ما يمنع اللزوم، بل إنما لزم ذلك؛ لأن القدرة غير الإيمان. ثم اعتباره بالأمر فاسد؛ لأنه استعباد

Third Argument and Objections
Maturidi's third argument and responses to objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's third argument linking al-aslah to the refutation of dualism, and his responses to objections about divine knowledge and blame.

Core Idea
The principle of al-aslah, if accepted, would undermine the monotheistic argument against dualism and lead to theological incoherence.

Maturidi's third argument draws a parallel: the refutation of dualism relies on one god's power over what the other does not know. If al-aslah is allowed, this refutation fails. He then addresses objections about divine knowledge and blame, asserting that power and faith are distinct. The passage concludes with a brief remark on divine command.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. This lesson covers the third argument and two objections.
The passage ends abruptly; it may be incomplete.
2
Understand the third argument
The argument against dualism depends on one god's exclusive power over some things. If al-aslah is valid, that exclusivity is undermined, so monotheistic refutation collapses.
3
Follow the objection about knowledge
An objection: God knows that a person will not believe, so why does He still command? Maturidi replies that such knowledge has no benefit; it does not change the obligation.
4
Grasp the distinction between power and faith
Another objection: if one has power to believe but does not, is he blameworthy? Maturidi says blame is greater, and power is distinct from faith; the presence of power does not necessitate faith.
The passage ends with a criticism of analogical reasoning about divine commands.

Divine Transcendence
beginner
4 min
The Guarantee of Provision and Its Implication for Divine Power
Maturidi refutes the notion that anyone can deprive another of God-guaranteed provision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that no creature can prevent Allah from fulfilling His guarantee of provision, and how this defends divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
No one can deprive another of God-guaranteed provision—that would imply Allah is weak.

This passage tackles a profound question: if Allah guarantees provision for every creature, can anyone actually withhold it? Maturidi argues that allowing such a possibility would lead to an absurd consequence—implying that Allah is under another's power. Read carefully to see how he uses a dilemma to uphold divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Focus on understanding the overall argument before diving into details.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the dilemma
Notice that Maturidi presents two possibilities: either someone has power to prevent Allah's guarantee, or no one does. Identify these two sides.
Look for the Arabic structure 'فأما أن يكون... أو لا يكون'.
3
Understand the first possibility
If someone could prevent the guarantee, it would imply Allah breaks His promise, is incapable, and is under another's power. Recognize why this is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Confirm the second possibility
The second possibility must be true: no one can prevent the guarantee, confirming that provision is entirely from Allah.
This defends divine omnipotence.
5
Reflect on implications
Consider how this argument impacts your understanding of human agency and reliance on Allah for provision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تكلم في سؤال الرزق بوجه لا يرضى به سؤال، بل الوجه في ذلك أن الله تعالى إذ ضمن الرزق بقوله: وَمَا مِن دَابَّةٍ فى الأَرْضِ إِلَّا عَلَى اللهِ رِزْقُهَا، كان ذلك يملك بملكة أو بما يطعمه. فأما أن يكون لأحد قدرة في الله منع وفاء ما ضمن من الوجه الذي ضمن حتى يلحقه الخلف فى الوعد والعجز عن وفاء ما ضمنه، فيكون الله فى فعله تحت قدرة غيره، وبغيره يقدر على انجاز الوعد ووفاء العهد، وهذا أمر عظيم، أو لا يكون، فيبطل أن يكون أحد يرزق بما هو في الحقيقة رزق غيره من ذلك الوجه، أو يقدر عليه، ولو كان ذلك فيما القدرة معه لكانت هذه الوحشة تلحقه.
Then he spoke about the question of provision in a way not pleasing to ask, but rather the proper way is that Allah Almighty, when He guaranteed provision by His saying: 'And there is no creature on earth except that upon Allah is its provision', that is possessed by ownership or by what He feeds. So either someone has a power in Allah to prevent the fulfillment of what He guaranteed from the aspect He guaranteed, so that He would be guilty of breaking His promise and incapacity to fulfill what He guaranteed, thus Allah in His act would be under the power of another, and by another He would be able to fulfill the promise and keep the covenant, and this is a grave matter. Or it is not, so it becomes invalid that anyone provides with what is in reality the provision of another from that aspect, or is able to do so. And if that were such that power is with it, this desolation would befall him.
Source pages p. 883

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about divine provision (rizq). Maturidi begins by criticizing a certain way of asking about provision, then states the correct understanding: Allah has guaranteed provision for every creature as per Q. 11:6. This provision is given through ownership or direct feeding. The author then presents a dilemma: either someone has power over Allah to prevent Him from fulfilling His guarantee, which would imply that Allah breaks His promise and is incapable, and thus would be under another's power—this is an impossible and grave matter. Or no one has such power, which means that no one can truly provide what is actually another's provision, nor can they have power over it. If it were otherwise, that is, if power were concurrent with the act of providing, then this desolation would afflict the one claiming such power. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum defending divine omnipotence and the direct nature of provision from Allah.

Key Terms
الرزق
الضمان
القدرة
الوعد
العهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that a person can be deprived of their provision by another human being, meaning that someone has independent power over provision.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if anyone could prevent Allah's guaranteed provision, it would imply that Allah is under that person's power, which is impossible. Therefore, no one can truly withhold or control provision except Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if someone could prevent Allah's guaranteed provision?
A) It would prove human independence.
B) Allah would be under another's power.
C) It would invalidate the guarantee.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defends divine omnipotence by showing that the guarantee of provision cannot be thwarted by any creature, lest Allah be seen as weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel anxious about your provision because you think someone else can deprive you of it? How does this passage challenge that fear, and what would it look like to live with full confidence in Allah's guarantee?
?
In what area of your life do you still act as if your provision depends more on people than on Allah? What would change if you truly believed that no one can thwart His guarantee?
?
When you see others in need, do you sometimes feel that their lack is due to someone's power to deprive? How does Maturidi's argument reshape your view of justice and reliance on Allah—and what responsibility does it place on you to trust Him while still acting as a means of provision for others?

What Cannot Be Attributed to Allah
Maturidi lists things not attributed to Allah and states the rule of attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why certain things like disbelief and evil cannot be attributed to Allah in speech, even though He creates them, and the principle that attribution is for blessings and commands.

Core Idea
Attribution to Allah follows praise, not creation.

Maturidi begins by clarifying a crucial linguistic and theological rule: while Allah is the Creator of everything, not everything is said to be 'from Allah.' He explains that the expression 'from Allah' carries connotations of blessing or command, so it cannot apply to evil or filth. This lesson establishes the foundational distinction between creating and attributing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the list of things Maturidi says we do not attribute to Allah.
The list includes disbelief, evil, Iblis, the devil, filth, and corruption.
2
Identify the principle
Find the sentence that states the principle: speech from Allah issues in the manner of a claim of command or attribution of blessings. That is the key rule.
3
Understand the implication
Even though Allah is the Creator of all things, we do not attribute blameworthy things to Him because language requires the attribution to be praiseworthy.
4
Reflect on the wisdom
Consider why this rule matters for how Muslims speak about Allah. It protects divine transcendence and ensures respectful language.
This connects to the core idea that attribution follows praise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : فنقول : لا نقول الكفر من الله من طريق ، ولا شر بإطلاق القول من الله ، وكذلك من الله . وكذلك لا أحد يقول : إبليس من الله ، أو الشيطان من الله ، أو كل قذر ونتن من الله ، أو كل فساد ثبت أن هذا اللفظ فاسد فيما كانت الخلق أيضا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل في ذلك أن القول منه يخرج مخرج دعوى الأمر أو إضافة الأنعام ، وليس فى ذلك واحد منها البتة ، فلا يجوز الإضافة إليه ، وهو كما قلنا : إن الله في التحقيق وإن كان رب كل شيء وإله كل شيء وخالق كل شيء ، وكل شيء له ، لا يقال ذلك فى الأرواث والخبائث والشيطان ونحو ذلك من الأشياء التي لا تذكر أنفسها إلا على الاستحقاق بها ، فإضافتها الواحد يخرج على ذلك ، وإن كانت فى أنها مخلوقة كفرها مما يضاف إلى الله ، فمثله الذي نحن فيه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وعلى هذا يكره القول فى الكفر والمعاصى إنها بقضاء الله وقدره وإرادته لوجهين : أحدهما ما ذكر من القبح، أو هي لا تذكر إلا على الاستقباح والاستهانة ، والذى ذلك وصفه لا يضاف إلى الله تعالى على ما أخبرت ، وإن كان في التحقيق من قول ووجه آخر إنه يتكلم به على الاعتذار والاحتجاج ، ذلك المفهوم منه ، وقد بينا أن لا عذر لهم في ذلك ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وكذلك عند الناس لا يقال : يا خالق الخبائث والأنجاس ونحو ذلك ، وإن كان هو في الحقية لكل شيء خالقا ، فمثله الذي ذكرنا . وأصل ذلك إنه يضاف إلى الله تعالى كل ما كانت الإضافة إليه تخرج مخرج التعظيم أو مخرج الشكر أو مخرج ذكر نعمه أو أمره ، وما خرج على غير ذلك لا يضاف اليه ، وإن كان فى الحقيقة خلقه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وجملة ذلك إن الله يوصف بفعله ، وهو خارج على معنى العدل أو الفضل في الحقيقة ، وربما يضاف إليه ما ليس فى الحقيقة فعله أو صفته ، فإن كان يقتضى معنى محمودا يجوز ذلك ؛ لما نيل ذلك بإنعامه وأفضاله ، وإن لم يكن لم يضف ؛ لما ليس ذلك في الحقيقة فعله فيوصف به ، وهو من حيث فعله ، وذلك الشيء فما عند الخلق بغير هذا الوصف ، والله تعالى يجل ويتعالى عن غير هذين الوصفين ؛ إذ فى أفعاله صفة عدل وحكمة أو فضل وإحسان ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: So we say: we do not say disbelief is from Allah in any way, nor evil by absolute speech from Allah, and similarly from Allah. And similarly no one says: Iblis is from Allah, or the devil is from Allah, or every filth and impurity is from Allah, or every corruption—it is established that this expression is corrupt in what creation also had, and there is no power except through Allah. The principle in that is that speech from Him issues in the manner of a claim of command or attribution of blessings, and none of that is in any of them at all, so attribution to Him is not permissible. And it is as we said: Allah in reality, even though He is the Lord of everything, the God of everything, the Creator of everything, and everything belongs to Him, that is not said concerning dung, impurities, the devil, and similar things that are not mentioned except in the context of deserving blame. So their attribution to the One issues on that basis, even if they are created—their kufr is something attributed to Allah, so the like of which we are in, and there is no power except through Allah. And on this basis it is disliked to say concerning disbelief and sins that they are by Allah's decree, determination, and will for two reasons: one is what we mentioned of ugliness, or they are not mentioned except for the purpose of considering them ugly and contemptible, and that which has such description is not attributed to Allah as I informed you, even if in reality it is His creation. And another aspect is that one speaks thereby in the way of excuse and argumentation, which is understood from it, and we have shown that they have no excuse in that, and there is no power except through Allah. Similarly, among people it is not said: O Creator of impurities and filth and the like, even if He in truth is the Creator of everything, so its like is what we mentioned. The principle of that is: whatever attribution to Allah issues in the manner of glorification, or in the manner of gratitude, or in the manner of mentioning His blessings or His command, is attributed to Allah, and whatever issues otherwise is not attributed to Him, even if it is in reality His creation. And there is no power except through Allah. The summary of that is that Allah is described by His action, and it issues upon the meaning of justice or grace in reality. Sometimes what is not in reality His action or attribute may be attributed to Him; if it implies a praiseworthy meaning, it is permissible because that is attained by His favor and bounty. If not, it is not attributed, because that is not in reality His action so as to be described by it, and He is from the aspect of His action, and that thing—what is with creation is other than this description. And Allah, exalted and transcendent above other than these two descriptions, for in His actions there is the attribute of justice and wisdom or grace and beneficence, and there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 911

Commentary
This passage defines the theological and linguistic principle for attributing things to Allah. The author begins by explicitly stating that disbelief, evil, Iblis, the devil, filth, and corruption are not said to be 'from Allah' even though Allah is the Creator of all things. The reason is that the expression 'from Allah' in Arabic implies a praiseworthy or grateful attribution—it issues in the manner of claiming command or attributing blessings. Since none of these evil things are blessings or commands, it is not permissible to attribute them to Allah in that way. The author then explains that although Allah is truly the Lord and Creator of everything, we do not say that dung, impurities, and the devil are from Him because they are only mentioned with blame and contempt. The attribution to Allah is based on how the thing is normally spoken of, not on the fact of creation. He then gives two reasons why it is disliked to say that disbelief and sins are by Allah's decree and will: first, because they are ugly and only mentioned disdainfully, so they should not be attributed to Allah in a positive sense; second, because such speech might be interpreted as an excuse for sin, which is invalid. The author further illustrates with common usage: people do not invoke Allah as 'Creator of filth,' even though He creates everything. The overarching principle is that only attributions that express glorification, gratitude, mentioning of blessings, or command are made to Allah; other attributions are avoided even if He creates them. Finally, the author summarizes that Allah is described by His actions, which are either just or gracious. A thing may be attributed to Him if it implies a praiseworthy meaning, even if not literally His action; otherwise it is not. Thus, Allah's descriptions are confined to justice and grace.

Key Terms
إضافة
كفر
شر
إبليس
خبائث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Everything Allah creates should be equally attributed to Him in speech.
Correction: Linguistic convention restricts attribution to what is praiseworthy; blameworthy things are not attributed even though He creates them.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it not permissible to say 'disbelief is from Allah'?
A) Because Allah did not create disbelief.
B) Because it implies praising disbelief.
C) Because the phrase 'from Allah' is only used for blessings and commands.
D) Because disbelief is not a real thing.

Key Takeaway
The expression 'from Allah' is reserved for blessings and commands; it is not used for blameworthy things even if Allah creates them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a difficulty—like illness or loss—do you ever find yourself saying 'this is from Allah' with a hint of resentment? How does Maturidi's principle challenge you to examine the tone of your heart when attributing events to Allah?
?
The Shaykh says we do not attribute Iblis to Allah even though Allah created him. If you truly internalized that, would it change how you speak about trials that come from others' sins? Will you commit to guarding your tongue from attributing evil to Allah, even when you feel its sting?
?
Allah's blessings you readily call 'from Allah'—but what about a moment of anger or a wrong thought? Do you ever attribute those to yourself or to shaytan, or do you subtly blame Allah for your own weakness? This lesson demands you re-evaluate: what in your life have you been attributing to Allah that He does not accept as from Him?
